The Dilemma of Eternal Motion: Intrinsic vs. Extrinsic Impossibility
Imam Razi presents a dilemma to refute the impossibility of eternal motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Imam Razi uses to refute the impossibility of eternal motion, distinguishing between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility.

Core Idea
Eternal motion is not impossible by itself; any impossibility must be from an external factor, leading to regress.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical refutation of the claim that eternal motion is impossible. Imam Razi sets up a dilemma: if motion in eternity were intrinsically impossible, it could never exist, but it does. If it is not intrinsically impossible, its impossibility must come from something else, but that leads to infinite regress. Here we unpack that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'
The passage is cut off at the end, but the key dilemma is complete.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Look for the two main possibilities: motion is either impossible by itself or not. Then the sub-branches.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Trace the implications of each branch
Follow the logic: if impossible by itself, motion can never exist. If impossible by another, then that other is either necessary by itself or dependent, leading to regress.
The argument aims to show that the opponent's position is untenable.
4
Understand the refutation strategy
Imam Razi claims that one single argument undermines all six of the opponent's proofs. This is a powerful counter-argument.
This lesson focuses on the logical structure, not the specific six proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن الجسم لو كان أزليا، لكان إما أن يكون ساكنا وإما أن يكون متحركًا. فلم قلتم : إنه يمتنع كون الجسم في الأزل متحركا ؟ والدلائل السنة التي عَوّلْتُم عليها في بيان امتناع كون الجسم متحركًا في الأزل هي بأسرها معارضة بوجه واحد. أن وجود الحركة وتأثير المؤثر في إيجاد الحركة في الأزل وإما أن يكون ممتنعا وهو لعينه ،ولذاته وإما أن لا يكون ممتنعا لعينه ولذاته. فإن كان ممتنعا لذاته وجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع قط؛ لأن ما الذات لا يزول فوجب أن لا توجد الحركة أصلاً . وأما إن كان وجود الحركة الأزلية وإيجاد الحركة في الأزل ليس ممتنعا لذاته فإما أن يكون ممتنعا لغيره، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان ممتنعًا لغيره، فلذلك المانع إن كان واجبًا لذاته امتنع زواله. وكان يجب امتناع زوال ذلك الامتناع، وإن كان واجبًا أيضًا لغيره كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول. فيلزم التسلسل . وأما إن كان وجود الحركة الأزلية غير ممتنعة لذاته ولا
We concede: if the body were eternal, it would be either stationary or moving. But why do you say that it is impossible for the body to be moving in eternity? The six proofs upon which you relied to show the impossibility of the body's being moving in eternity are all opposed by one single point: that the existence of motion and the effect of the agent in bringing about motion in eternity either is impossible in itself and by its essence, or is not impossible in itself and by its essence. If it is impossible by itself, it must never cease to be impossible, because what is impossible by itself cannot cease, so motion must not exist at all. And if the existence of eternal motion and bringing about motion in eternity is not impossible by itself, then it is either impossible due to something else, or not. If it is impossible due to something else, then that preventer, if it is necessary by itself, its removal is impossible, and the removal of that impossibility must be impossible; and if it is also necessary by something else, the same discussion applies, leading to infinite regress. And if the existence of eternal motion is neither impossible by itself nor
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage engages with the opponent's claim that a body, if eternal, would have to be either at rest or in motion, but they deny the possibility of eternal motion. The author argues that the opponent's six proofs can all be countered by a single argument. He presents a dilemma: motion in eternity is either intrinsically impossible or not. If it is intrinsically impossible, then motion could never exist, but it does exist, so it cannot be intrinsically impossible. If it is not intrinsically impossible, then any impossibility must come from an external factor. That external factor either is necessary by itself or dependent on something else. If necessary by itself, it cannot be removed, so the impossibility remains. If dependent, the same issue arises, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the opponent's position is undermined. The text cuts off before the final alternative.

Correction: Imam Razi argues that even if motion is an accident, its impossibility cannot be intrinsic; otherwise, motion could never exist. The argument focuses on the logical necessity of the impossibility, not the nature of accidents.

Why Space Cannot Be a Dimension
Two proofs refuting the view that space is a dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments refuting the claim that space (makan) is a dimension (bu'd), and why overlapping dimensions is impossible.

Core Idea
Space cannot be a dimension—both independence and dependence on matter lead to contradiction.

This passage from Imam Razi's al-Arba'in presents two powerful arguments against identifying space with dimension. The first argument uses a dilemma about the dimension's relationship to matter; the second shows that if space were a dimension, it would force impossible overlapping dimensions. These arguments are foundational for understanding the classical kalam concept of space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi number the proofs as 'second aspect' and 'third aspect'.
2
Understand the second proof's dilemma
The second proof presents a dilemma about the dimension's relationship to matter. Work through each horn of the dilemma and see why both lead to impossibility.
If dimension is independent, it cannot indwell in matter; if dependent, it cannot be abstracted.
3
Explore the third proof's overlap argument
The third proof shows that if space and its occupant are both dimensions, they would overlap, leading to the absurdity of one cubit being two cubits. Understand why this is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان أنه يمتنع كون المكان بعدا : أن البعد كان من حيث إنه هو هو غنيا عن المادة امتنع حلوله في المادة. فكان يجب أن لا يحل البعد في المادة أصلا وإن كان مفتقرًا إلى المادة، امتنع كونه مجردًا عن المادة. الوجه الثالث : هو أن المكان لو كان عبارة عن البعد والمتمكن له بعد آخر يلزم منه تداخل البعدين وهو محال . أمّا :أولاً : فلأنه يقتضي الجمع بين المثلين. وهو محال . وأما ثانيا فلأن البعدين طرفي الإناء إذا كان ذراعًا واحدا، فلو كان هناك بعدان أحدهما بعد المكان والثاني بعد المتمكن لزم القول بكون الذراع الواحد ذراعين وذلك محال فثبت بهذه الوجوه : فساد القول بأن المكان هو البعد.
The second aspect in explaining that it is impossible for space to be a dimension: that if the dimension is, in terms of its essence, independent of matter, then its indwelling in matter is impossible. So it must be that the dimension should not indwell in matter at all. And if it is dependent on matter, then its being abstracted from matter is impossible. The third aspect: that if space were a dimension, and the occupant (mutamakkin) has another dimension, then the overlapping of the two dimensions would follow, which is impossible. First, because it necessitates combining two similars, which is impossible. Second, because the two dimensions are the two ends of the vessel when it is one cubit. If there were two dimensions—one the dimension of space and the other the dimension of the occupant—then it would necessitate saying that one cubit is two cubits, which is impossible. Thus, by these aspects, the corruption of the statement that space is the dimension is established.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that space (makan) is a dimension (bu'd). It presents two proofs (the second and third aspects; the first is missing from this window). The second proof argues that if a dimension is by its essence independent of matter, it cannot reside in matter; if it is dependent on matter, it cannot be abstracted from it—either way, the view fails. The third proof shows that if space were a dimension and the body occupying it also had a dimension, the two dimensions would overlap, which is absurd. Overlap would require one cubit to be two cubits, an impossibility. The conclusion is that the claim that space is a dimension is false.

Key Terms

بعد (bu'd)
متمكن (mutamakkin)
تداخل البعدين (tadakhul al-bu'dayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space is a dimension that bodies occupy, and this is a coherent concept in classical kalam.
Correction: Classical kalam, as represented by Imam Razi, argues that space cannot be a dimension because it leads to contradictions such as overlapping dimensions and logical absurdities.

Checkpoint

A) The dimension would be independent of matter.
B) Two dimensions would overlap, making one cubit two cubits.
C) The dimension would be dependent on matter.
D) Motion would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The passage proves that space is not a dimension, using two irrefutable arguments: a dilemma about matter-dependence and the impossibility of overlapping dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection and Answer on Motion in Eternity
Objection that motion cannot occur in eternity and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that motion cannot occur in eternity is refuted by analyzing whether the concept of eternity is necessary or contingent.

Core Idea
Eternity does not inherently prevent motion; it depends on necessity.

This lesson examines a classic objection in kalam: if eternity means no beginning, can motion occur in it? The objection says no, because motion requires being preceded by something else. The answer dismantles this by analyzing the nature of the concept of eternity itself, showing that the objection leads to an infinite regress unless we admit a Necessary Being. This passage is a fine example of reasoning by conditional analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'It is not said...' and understand the claim that eternity prevents motion due to priority.
Note the key terms: 'azal (eternity), haraka (motion), mabsooq (preceded).'
2
Understand the answer's structure
The answer uses a conditional: if the meaning of eternity is necessary by itself, its removal is impossible; if contingent, it depends on something else, leading to a regress.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Follow the chain of reasoning
Trace the argument: from the impossibility of removal of the necessary, to the impossibility of removal of the concept of eternity, to the impossibility that motion is impossible in eternity.
The conclusion is that since motion does occur, the objection fails.
4
Reflect on the logical method
Note how the argument avoids infinite regress by terminating at the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud).
This is a foundational move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : المانع من حصول الحركة هو تحقيق مسمى الأزل؛ لأن الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، والأزل ينافي المسبوقية بالغير فالجمع بينهما محال. لأنا نقول : مسمى الأزل حيث ثبت وتحقق إن كان واجب الثبوت لذاته امتنع زواله قط، فكان يجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع أبدا، وإن لم يكن واجب الثبوت لذاته، كان ثبوته لأجل شيء آخر. والكلام في ذلك الثاني، كما في الأول. ولا يتسلسل، بل ينتهي بالآخرة إلى واجب الوجود لذاته. وحينئذ يلزم من امتناع زوال الواجب لذاته امتناع زوال مسمى الأزل، ويلزم من امتناع زواله أن لا يزول قط امتناع حدوث الحركة. ولما كان ذلك باطلاً، علمنا أن الحركة لا يمتنع حصولها في الأزل.
It is not said: The preventer of motion occurring is the realization of the meaning of eternity; because motion requires being preceded by another, and eternity negates being preceded by another, so combining them is impossible. We say: The meaning of eternity, when it is established and realized, if it is necessary in existence (wajib al-thubut) by itself, its removal is impossible at all, so it would be necessary that that impossibility never ceases; and if it is not necessary in existence by itself, its existence is due to something else, and the discussion concerning that second is as in the first, and there is no infinite regress, but it terminates ultimately to the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud) by itself. Thus, from the impossibility of removal of the necessary by itself, there follows the impossibility of removal of the meaning of eternity, and from the impossibility of its removal, the impossibility of occurrent motion never ceases. And since that is false, we know that motion is not impossible to occur in eternity.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objection states that motion cannot occur in eternity because motion requires being preceded by something else, whereas eternity is unbeginning and thus negates priority. The answer argues that the concept of eternity, if it is necessary by itself, cannot be removed; but if it is contingent, it depends on something else, leading to the Necessary Existence. The conclusion is that since the impossibility of motion in eternity would be eternal and thus unremovable, but we know motion does occur (as change is observed), the objection fails.

Key Terms
أزل
واجب الثبوت لذاته
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity itself, as a concept, inherently prevents any motion or change from occurring.
Correction: The passage shows that the preventer depends on whether the concept of eternity is necessary or contingent; if it is necessary, it would eternally prevent motion, but since motion occurs, the concept cannot be necessary in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the response, if the meaning of eternity is necessary by itself, what follows?
A) It can be removed.
B) Its removal is impossible.
C) Motion becomes possible.
D) Eternity is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The objection that eternity prevents motion is refuted by showing it would require the concept of eternity to be necessary, which would make the impossibility of motion eternal, contradicting observed motion.

Three Proofs that Rest is Not a Positive Attribute
Three proofs that alternation of states does not imply positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments from Imam al-Razi that demonstrate why alternation between states does not entail that each state is a positive attribute, specifically addressing the case of rest (sukun) as a divine attribute.

Core Idea
Alternation of states does not prove either state is real — change can be in relations, not in entities.

This lesson addresses a key objection in kalam: if a body alternates between motion and rest, does that prove rest is a positive attribute? Imam al-Razi responds with three powerful proofs, each showing that change from one state to another does not necessitate that either state is a positive attribute. These proofs use examples from impossibility/possibility, God's knowledge, and the nature of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opening lines where the opponent argues that since a body alternates between motion and rest, both must be positive attributes. Note the initial response: we do not concede that rest is positive.
The opponent's proof relies on the idea that alternation requires both states to be real.
2
Examine the first proof
Focus on the first proof using impossibility and possibility. See how change from impossibility to possibility does not make either a positive attribute, and why impossibility cannot be positive.
This proof shows that even non-entities can change without being positive.
3
Examine the second proof
Read the second proof about God's knowledge. It argues that God's knowledge of an event changes from non-occurrence to occurrence, but that does not imply a new attribute in God.
This proof uses an example from divine attributes to show change without new positive attribute.
4
Examine the third proof
Read the third proof about a body becoming a locus for an accident. It shows that if being a locus were a positive attribute, it would lead to infinite regress.
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that alternation implies both states are real. The three proofs collectively refute this by showing that change can occur in non-entities or relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركًا في الأزل. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ساكنا ؟ قوله : (لأن السكون أمر ثبوتي والثابت الأزلي يمتنع زواله) قلنا : لا نسلم أن السكون أمر ثبوتي. وقوله : (الدليل عليه أن الجسم يتحرك بعد أن كان ساكنا، وبالعكس. وذلك يوجب كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتيًا). نقول : لا نسلم أن تبدل إحدى الحالتين بالأخرى، يوجب كون إحدى الحالتين ثبوتية والذي يدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن الحوادث عندكم ممتنعة الحدوث في الأزل، ثم انقلبت جائزة الحدوث فيما لا يزال فههنا تبدل الامتناع بالصحة، مع أنه لا يصح أن يكون الامتناع أمرًا ثبوتيًا. وتكون الصحة أمرًا ثبوتيًا. أمَّا أن الامتناع لا يكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا فلأن الامتناع لو كان أمرًا ثبوتيّا، لكان الموصوف به ثبوتيًا، فيلزم أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت ثابتًا، وهو محال . وأما أن الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا. فلذلك لأنه لو كان أمرًا ثبوتيًا لا متنع كونه واجبًا لذاته، بل يكون ممكنا لذاته . فيكون إمكانه زائدا عليه. ويلزم التسلسل. والوجه الثاني : في بيان أن تبدل إحدى الحالتين بالأخرى، لا يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ،ثبوتيًا، ولا تجدد أمر في المحل : ذلك لأنه قبل حصول الحادث المعين، لا يصدق أن الله تعالى عالم بحصوله في هذا الوقت لأنه لو صدق عليه أنه عالم بحصوله في ذلك الوقت مع أنه غير حاصل في ذلك الوقت، لكان ذلك جهلاً. وهو على الله تعالى محال . ثم إنه إذا حصل ذلك الحادث، فإنه يصدق على الله تعالى أنه عالم بحصوله في ذلك الوقت. فإذن صدق على الله تعالى أنه ما كان عالما بحصوله في ذلك الوقت، ثم صار عالمًا بحصوله في ذلك الوقت. فلو اقتضى صدق هذه القضية، حدوث أمر وتجدد وصف لزم حدوث شيء في ذات الله وذلك عندكم محال. الوجه الثالث : أن الجسم قبل حدوث العرض المعين فيه ما كان محلا لذلك العرض، ثم إذا حصل ذلك العرض فقد صار محلّا لذلك العرض فيلزم أن يكون كونه محلا لذلك العرض عرضًا ،آخر ثم إنه على هذا التقدير يصير محلا لذلك العرض الآخر، بعد أنه ما كان كذلك. فيلزم أن يكون كونه محلا لتلك المحلية عرضا آخر ويلزم التسلسل.
We concede that the body cannot be moving in eternity. Why is it not permissible for it to be at rest? He said: 'Because rest is a positive attribute (amr thubuti) and the eternal positive attribute cannot cease.' We said: We do not concede that rest is a positive attribute. And his saying: 'The proof for it is that the body moves after having been at rest, and vice versa, and that necessitates that one of the two states is a positive attribute.' We say: We do not concede that the alternation of one state with another necessitates that one of the two states is positive. What indicates this are aspects (wujuh): First: According to you, events are impossible to occur in eternity, then they become permissible to occur in the future; so here there is a change from impossibility (imtina') to possibility (siha), even though impossibility cannot be a positive attribute, and possibility cannot be a positive attribute. As for impossibility not being a positive attribute, if it were positive, the thing described by it would be positive, leading to the impossible being existent, which is absurd. And as for possibility, if it were positive, it would become necessary by itself, but it is possible by itself, so its possibility would be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. Second aspect: The alternation of one state to another does not necessitate that one state is positive, nor that something new occurs in the subject (mahall); because before the occurrence of a specific event, it is not true that God knows its occurrence at that time, because if it were true that He knows its occurrence at that time while it is not occurring, that would be ignorance (jahl), which is impossible for God. Then when that event occurs, it is true of God that He knows its occurrence at that time. So it becomes true of God that He was not knowing its occurrence at that time, then He became knowing its occurrence at that time. If the truth of this proposition necessitated the occurrence of something and a new attribute, it would necessitate something occurring in God's essence, which according to you is impossible. Third aspect: The body before the occurrence of a specific accident (arad) in it was not a locus (mahall) for that accident; then when that accident occurs, it becomes a locus for that accident. So it would follow that its being a locus for that accident is another accident, and then it would become a locus for that other accident, and so on, leading to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a detailed response to the claim that rest is a positive attribute. The opponent argues that because a body alternates between motion and rest, both must be positive. The response denies this and provides three proofs. The first proof uses the example of impossibility and possibility themselves changing, which are not positive. The second proof uses God's knowledge: if change in knowledge required a new attribute, it would imply change in God. The third proof uses the concept of a body being a locus for accidents, leading to infinite regress if each state is a separate attribute.

Key Terms

أمر ثبوتي
تبدل
امتناع
إمكان
جهل

عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body alternates between motion and rest, then both motion and rest must be real positive attributes.
Correction: Alternation does not imply positivity; as shown by the change from impossibility to possibility, which are not positive.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof's example to show that alternation does not imply positive attributes?
A) Change from motion to rest.
B) Change from impossibility to possibility.
C) Change in God's knowledge.
D) Change in locus.

Key Takeaway
Alternation of states does not prove either state is a positive attribute; change can be in non-entities like possibility or relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this truth reshape your understanding of His omniscience and your personal accountability before Him? Will you let this deepen your reverence for His perfect knowledge, or will you continue to think of Him as changing like a created being?
?
The third proof shows that if every change required a new attribute, infinite regress would follow. This underscores Allah's absolute simplicity. In what areas of your life do you still attribute complexity or parts to Allah, even subtly? What practical step can you take today to purify your tawhid from such subtle associations?
?
The first proof uses the concept that impossibility cannot be a positive attribute. How often do you treat your own limitations or sins as fixed, unchangeable realities? Does this lesson challenge you to see that even your 'impossible' states can change because they are not positive entities? Distinction Between Accident and Its Locus
Proof that being a locus is distinct from the accident itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why being a locus (mahall) is not identical to the accident itself, based on the argument from the possibility of knowledge with doubt.

Core Idea
An accident and its location are not the same; we can know one while doubting the other.

This passage continues the discussion on the third proof (from multiplicity). An objector claims that being a locus is merely the accident's occurrence in the body. Imam al-Razi responds by showing that we can know the accident's essence while doubting whether it is in the body, proving they are distinct attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that being a locus is just the accident's occurrence. Grasp why someone might think the relation is not additional.
The objector is trying to reduce the relation to the accident itself.
2
Grasp the response's key argument
The response uses the argument from knowledge and doubt: we can know the accident's essence while doubting its location. This proves the two are distinct.
Knowledge of one without the other implies difference in reality.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that being a locus and being an accident are two attributes additional to the essences of the body and the accident.
This supports the third proof that accidents do not subsist in accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال إنه لا معنى لكونه محلا لذلك العرض، وكون ذلك العرض حالاً إلا مجرد حصول ذلك العرض فيه. لأنا نقول : ليس كون الجسم محلّا لذلك العرض، وكون ذلك العرض حالا في الجسم، هو نفس ذلك العرض ولذاته ؛ لأنه يمكن العلم بذات ذلك العرض، وذات ذلك الشك في كون ذلك العرض حالا في ذلك الجسم، وكون ذلك الجسم محلا لذلك العرض والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم. فعلمنا أن المحلية والحالية صفتان مغايرتان لذات ذلك العرض، ولذات ذلك الجسم.
It is not said: There is no meaning to being a locus (mahall) for that accident (arad), and that accident being an accident (hal), except the mere occurrence of that accident in it. For we say: The body being a locus for that accident, and that accident being an accident in the body, is not the same as the essence (dhat) of that accident; because it is possible to know the essence of that accident, and to doubt whether that accident is an accident in that body, and whether that body is a locus for that accident, and what is known is different (mughayir) from what is not known. So we know that being a locus (mahalliya) and being an accident (haliya) are two attributes different from the essence of that accident and from the essence of that body.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is a response to an objection against the third proof. The objector claims that 'being a locus' is nothing but the accident itself. The response argues that we can know the essence of the accident while doubting its being located, indicating that the two are distinct. Thus, the relation of being a locus is an additional attribute.

Key Terms
محل
حال
ذات
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that an accident's inherence is identical to the accident itself.
Correction: The passage shows that we can know the accident's essence while doubting its location, proving they are distinct.

Checkpoint
How does the response prove that being a locus is distinct from the accident?
A) By infinite regress.
B) By possibility of knowing one while doubting the other.
C) By divine knowledge.
D) By change of state.

Key Takeaway
The accident and its locus are distinct because we can know the accident's essence without knowing its location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know the essence of a truth—say, that Allah alone is worthy of worship—yet how many times have you doubted its reality by acting as if something else holds your heart? Will you today identify one such doubt and replace it with the attribute of certainty?
?
If you can grasp a theological concept but remain uncertain of its place in your own life, what does that reveal about your connection to the truth? Is your knowledge merely an accident without a locus in your soul?
?
When you learn that an accident and its locus are distinct, are you willing to examine whether your faith has truly settled in your heart as a permanent attribute, or is it still floating as an abstract idea? What concrete step will you take to make it dwell?

Motion as Transition: Refuting Rest as Non-Existence
Objection that rest is non-existence and response defining motion as transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is defined as transition (intiqal) rather than merely being in a location, and how this refutes the objection that rest is non-existence.

Core Idea
Motion is the transition itself, not its result.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection that motion might be a positive attribute while rest is its absence. He clarifies a subtle point: motion is not simply 'being in a location' after moving, but rather the process of moving between locations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the kalam view of motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and concession
Notice how the opponent concedes that one state must be positive but questions why motion cannot be positive and rest its absence.
2
Examine the opponent's definition
The opponent equates motion with 'being in a location' (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz) and reduces the difference to an accidental attribute. Identify this claim.
3
Analyze the response
Razi rejects that definition. He argues that motion is the transition from one location to another, which is prior to and ends at the second location. Understand the proof: when you arrive, motion ceases.
The key is that the end of a thing is not the thing itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن صدق قولنا لم يكن ثم كان يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتيًا . فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الحركة أمرًا ثبوتيًا، وأن يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة عما شأنه أن يتحرك ؟ قوله : الحركة والكون كل واحد منهما عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز، ولا تفاوت بينهما إلا في وصف عرضي. قلنا : لا نسلم أن الحركة عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز الثاني. والدليل عليه أنه متى حصل في الحيز الثاني فقد انقطعت الحركة وانتهت ونهاية الشيء لا تكون نفس ذلك الشيء، بل الحركة عبارة عن كونه مستقلا عن الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني. وذلك الانتقال أمر متقدم على الحصول في الحيز الثاني.
We concede: The truth of our statement 'it was not then it was' necessitates that one of the two states is a positive attribute. Why is it not permissible that motion be a positive attribute, and that rest be the non-existence (adam) of motion in that which is liable to move? He said: Motion and being-in-space (al-kawn) each are expressions of occurring in a location (hayyiz), and there is no difference between them except in an accidental attribute. We said: We do not concede that motion is an expression of occurring in the second location. The proof for it: When it occurs in the second location, motion has ceased and ended, and the end of a thing is not the thing itself. Rather, motion is an expression of his being on the way (mustaqillan) from the first location to the second location, and that transition (intiqal) is prior to occurring in the second location.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is an objection proposing that motion is positive and rest is negative. The opponent quotes a definition equating motion with being in a location. The response rejects that motion is merely the result, arguing that motion is the transition itself, which is prior to the arrival.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
عدم
حيز
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is simply the state of being in a different location.
Correction: Motion is the transition between locations, not the state of being in a location. Arrival ends motion.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is motion?
A) Being in a location.
B) The transition from one location to another.
C) Non-existence of rest.
D) An accidental attribute.

Key Takeaway
Motion is the transition from one location to another, not the result of arrival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your spiritual journey—your transition from sin to obedience—do you define it by the final state (the completed prayer, the avoided sin) or by the ongoing movement of your heart? How would your relationship with Allah change if you valued the transition itself, even when you haven't 'arrived'? What step of transition are you avoiding today because you're too focused on the endpoint?
?
This passage teaches that the end of a thing is not the thing itself. In your repentance (tawbah), have you ever felt that the act of turning is only valid once you feel 'arrived'? How does that mindset hinder your hope in Allah's mercy? Will you commit to seeing each moment of turning as the essence of repentance, regardless of perfection?

Refutation of Infinite Divisibility
Razi refutes the claim of infinite divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses necessary knowledge to refute the argument that spatial objects are infinitely divisible.

Core Idea
Necessary truths about bodies refute false inferences about infinite division.

This lesson presents a brief but powerful objection-and-answer from Imam Razi. He encounters a claim that the existence of two sides in a body implies infinite divisibility. His response hinges on a self-evident truth about the directedness of bodies, showing how necessary knowledge can stop skeptical arguments in their tracks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the objection and Razi's reply.
The Arabic contains a potential OCR artifact ('السماء') which may be a misreading.
2
Identify the objection and the response
The objector claims that if every body has two sides, then it is infinitely divisible. Razi responds by pointing out a necessary truth about bodies.
The response is that it is known by necessity that each side faces a different direction.
3
Understand the role of necessary knowledge
Razi argues that the premise (bodies have distinct sides) is so evident that it cannot be denied. This necessary knowledge overrides the skeptical implication of infinite divisibility.
Necessary truths are self-evident and foundational in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this response is effective. The objector's argument is dismissed because it tries to derive a problematic conclusion from an undeniable fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لو افترض في كل متحيز جانبان للزم أن يكون كل متحيز قابلا للقسمة، لا إلى نهاية. السماء قلنا : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن كل متحيز فإن الجانب الذي منه يلي غير الجانب الذي يلي الأرض وما كان معلوم الثبوت بالضرورة لا يمكن إنكاره وبالله التوفيق.
His statement: 'If every spatial object is assumed to have two sides, it would necessarily follow that every spatial object is divisible without end.' [We said: By the heavens], we said: It is known by necessity that every spatial object has a side that faces one direction different from the side that faces the earth, and what is known necessarily cannot be denied, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if every spatial object (mutahayyiz) has two sides, then it must be infinitely divisible. Razi responds that it is a necessary truth that every spatial object has distinct sides (e.g., one side faces up, another faces the earth). This necessary knowledge cannot be denied. The objection is thus dismissed because the necessary character of the knowledge precludes the skeptical implication. The argument is brief: the premise is so evident that the alleged consequence is irrelevant.

Key Terms
متحيز
جانبان
القسمة لا إلى نهاية
معلوم بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body has two sides, it must be infinitely divisible.
Correction: Razi shows that the necessary truth about bodies having distinct sides refutes that inference because the premise is undeniable.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the claim that every spatial object is infinitely divisible?
A) By appealing to necessary knowledge that each side faces a different direction.
B) By proving that infinite divisibility is impossible.
C) By denying that bodies have two sides.
D) By stating that the argument is irrelevant to bodies.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes infinite divisibility by appealing to necessary knowledge about the directedness of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Razi says that what is known by necessity cannot be denied. Have you ever found yourself doubting something you know is absolutely true—like your own existence or the reality of the world around you—because of a clever argument? The objector used a logical implication to try to force an unwelcome conclusion. This week, when you encounter such situations, practice holding firm to necessary truths and critically examine the premises of the argument.

Razi's response relies on a deeply held conviction about the nature of physical reality. What is one necessary truth about yourself—something you know beyond doubt—that you have been neglecting in your daily actions? The Parallel Lines Proof for the Finitude of Bodies
Razi presents the first part of his second proof for the finitude of bodies using a parallel lines argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the interaction between a finite and an infinite line leads to a logical contradiction, proving that bodies cannot be infinite.

Core Idea
If bodies were infinite, parallel lines would force a contradiction.

Imam Razi presents a clever geometric argument to show that bodies must have finite boundaries. The proof considers two parallel lines—one finite and one hypothetically infinite—and examines what happens when one line tilts to align with the other. The resulting logical impossibility demonstrates that the assumption of infinite bodies is false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the goal
Razi aims to prove that all bodies are finite in magnitude, which is a key step in establishing that they are created (and therefore require a Creator).
2
Set up the thought experiment
Imagine two parallel lines: one finite and one infinite. This is a conceptual scenario used to test the possibility of infinite bodies.
The lines represent dimensions of a hypothetical body.
3
Track the alignment
If the finite line is rotated out of parallelism to align with the infinite line, there must be a first point on the infinite line where alignment occurs.
This follows because the rotation is continuous.
4
Spot the contradiction
In the finite line, any point we choose as the first alignment point would already have been preceded by alignment with a point above it (since the line is continuous). Thus, the infinite line both must have a first point and cannot have one.
This is a logical absurdity.
5
Conclude the finitude
The only way to avoid the contradiction is to reject the initial assumption that bodies could be infinite. Therefore, bodies are finite in magnitude.
The proof is complete for finitude; later steps will show finitude implies origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني: في إثبات حدود الأجسام : فنقول : الأجسام متناهية المقدار، وكل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فهو محدث، فالأجسام محدثة. والذي يدل على أن الأجسام متناهية في المقدار وجهان: الوجه الأول: الأجسام لو كانت غير متناهية أمكن أن يفرض فيها خطان متوازيان أحدهما غير متناه والآخر متناه، فإذا زال الخط المتناهي عن الموازاة إلى المسامتة فلا بد وأن يحدث في الخط الذي هو غير متناه نقطة أول نقطة هي المسامتة، لكن ذلك محال في الخط الذي هو متناه؛ لأن كل نقطة فرضناها فيه وجعلناها أول نقطة المسامتة كانت المسامتة مع النقطة التي فوقها، حاصلة قبل المسامتة معها، فإذن يلزم أن يحصل في الخط الغير المتناهي نقطة أول نقطة هي المسامتة، وأن لا يحصل وهو محال وهذا المحال إنما يلزم من فرضنا ذلك الخط غير متناه، وإذا فرض الخط غير متناه وجب أن يكون محالا باطلا.
The second proof: on establishing the limits of bodies. We say: Bodies are finite in magnitude, and everything finite in magnitude is originated, so bodies are originated. That which indicates that bodies are finite in magnitude are two aspects: The first aspect: If bodies were infinite, it would be possible to assume in them two parallel lines, one infinite and the other finite. Then when the finite line moves from parallelism to alignment, there must occur in the infinite line a first point that is the point of alignment. But that is impossible in the finite line, because every point we assume in it and make it the first point of alignment, the alignment with the point above it would have occurred before the alignment with it. Therefore, it follows that in the infinite line a first point of alignment occurs and does not occur, which is absurd. This absurdity follows only from our assumption that the line is infinite. If the line is assumed infinite, it must be absurd and false.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the finitude of bodies. It is part of the second main proof (برهان ثاني). Razi first states the intended conclusion: bodies are finite in magnitude and therefore originated (later proofs will complete the argument). He then gives two proofs; this is the first (الوجه الأول). The proof works by considering two parallel lines, one finite and one infinite. When the finite line is rotated out of parallelism to align with a point on the infinite line, there must be a first point on the infinite line where alignment occurs. However, in the finite line, any point assumed to be the first point of alignment would have been preceded by an alignment with a point above it (because the finite line is continuous). This yields a contradiction: the infinite line must have a first point of alignment, but it cannot have one. The only resolution is that the assumption of an infinite line is false. Hence, bodies cannot be infinite in size.

Key Terms
متناهي المقدار
غير متناه
خطان متوازيان
مسامتة
أول نقطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite bodies are conceivable and therefore possible.
Correction: This proof shows that the concept of an infinite body leads to a logical contradiction (the parallel lines argument), so infinite bodies are impossible.

Checkpoint
What contradiction arises if we assume an infinite line in the parallel line argument?
A) The infinite line would both have and not have a first point of alignment.
B) The finite line would become infinite.
C) The lines would never be parallel.
D) The point of alignment would be undefined.

Key Takeaway
The parallel lines argument proves that bodies cannot be infinite in magnitude, as it leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof concludes that bodies are finite and thus originated. If you truly believe this, how does it change the way you live your life today, knowing that everything around you, including yourself, has a finite existence and a beginning?
?
Razi uses reason and geometry to prove a fundamental truth about reality. Are you willing to let logic and evidence challenge your comfortable assumptions? What is one belief about your life's 'infinite' nature (e.g., unlimited time, endless chances) that you need to confront with the conclusion of this proof?
?
The parallel lines argument reveals a contradiction in assuming infinite extension. In your own spiritual journey, is there a contradiction you have been ignoring—perhaps between believing the universe has a beginning and living as if there is no accountability? How will you resolve it today?

Second Proof for Finitude of Bodies: The Line Argument
Razi presents the second proof using two infinite lines with a finite difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that bodies are finite, using the comparison of two infinite lines with a finite difference and the application of one-to-one correspondence.

Core Idea
A finite difference between two lines forces both to be finite.

Razi presents a sophisticated argument against the possibility of infinite bodies. He imagines an infinite line and places two points within it, creating two lines that differ by a finite amount. By comparing the number of units in these lines, he shows that if one line is deficient, it must terminate, making the other line also finite. This proof reinforces the Kalam principle that all bodies are finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the precise mathematical language Razi uses.
2
Visualize the construction
Draw an infinite line AB. Place two points J and D in the middle. Identify the lines JA, DA, and JD. Note that JD is finite (one span). Observe that line JA is shorter than DA by exactly JD.
This visualization is key to understanding the argument.
3
Follow the proof's logic
Razi considers two possibilities: either the number of units in JA equals that in DA, or it is less. Reject the first as impossible. For the second, he argues that the deficiency (one span) must come from the infinite side, not the finite side, because on the finite side the units are matched one-to-one.
The one-to-one matching on the finite side forces the difference to appear on the infinite side.
4
Appreciate the key insight
Once the deficient line terminates, the excess line is exactly one span longer, so it also terminates. Thus both lines are finite, contradicting the assumption that they were infinite.
This reductio ad absurdum proves that no line can be infinite.
5
Connect to the larger context
This is the second proof for the finitude of bodies. Together with the first proof (which is not shown here), Razi establishes that every body is limited in size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : في بيان أن الأجسام يجب أن تكون متناهية لو كانت الأجسام غير متناهية أمكننا أن نفرض فيها خطا غير متناه وليكن ذلك الخط خط (اب) ويمكننا أن نفرض في وسط هذا الخط نقطتين : إحداهما نقطة (ج) والأخرى نقطة (د) المقدمة الثالثة فنقول : خط (ج) من جانب (ج) متناه، ومن جانب (۱) غير متناه . وخط د ا أيضا كذلك، ولكن خط (ج) (ا) أقل من خط (د (ا) بمقدار خط (جد) وليكن ذلك المقدار شبرا واحدا. وإذا تلخص هذا فنقول : عدد الأشياء الموجودة في الخط الناقص، وإن كانت مساوية لعدد الأشياء الموجودة في الخط الزائد كان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره، وهذا محال، وإن كانت أنقص منه فذلك التفاوت إما أن يظهر من الجانب المتناهي أو من الجانب الغير المتناهي، والأول محال؛ لأنا فرضنا التطبيق بحسب مراتب الأعداد في هذا الجانب المتناهي، فوجب أن يظهر التفاوت من الجانب الآخر. ومعنى فرض التطبيق بحسب مراتب الأعداد أن الشبر الأول هذه الجملة من مقابل بالشبر الأول من تلك الجملة الثانية والشبر الثاني من هذه الجملة مقابل بالشبر الثاني من تلك الجملة ولتكن هذه الدقيقة معلومة حتى يتم البرهان، فإذا حصل التطابق من هذا الجانب وجب أن لا يظهر التفاضل إلا أن لا يظهر التفاضل إلا من الجانب الآخر، وحينئذ تنقطع الجملة الناقصة من ذلك الجانب والزائد زاد عليه بشبر واحد، فتكون الجملة الزائدة أيضا متناهية، وهو المقصود. قثبت بهذين البرهانين أن كل جسم فهو متناء في المقدار.
And the second aspect: on demonstrating that bodies must be finite. If bodies were infinite, we could assume in them an infinite line, let that line be line AB, and we could assume in the middle of this line two points: one point J and another point D. Third preamble: We say: Line JA from side J is finite, and from side A is infinite. Line DA also is likewise, but line JA is less than line DA by the amount of line JD, and let that amount be one span. If this is summarized, we say: The number of things existing in the deficient line, if it equals the number of things existing in the excess line, then the thing with its other would be like it without its other, and this is impossible; and if it is less than it, then that difference either appears from the finite side or from the infinite side. The first is impossible because we assumed the application according to the orders of numbers on this finite side, so the difference must appear from the other side. The meaning of assuming the application according to orders of numbers is that the first span of this set is matched with the first span of that second set, and the second span of this set is matched with the second span of that set. Let this subtle point be known so the proof is complete. If matching occurs from this side, it is necessary that superiority does not appear except from the other side. Then the deficient set terminates from that side, and the excess is increased by one span, so the excess set is also finite, which is the intended conclusion. Thus, by these two proofs it is established that every body is finite in magnitude.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (الوجه الثاني) for the finitude of bodies. Razi sets up an infinite line AB with two points J and D in its middle. From J to one side (say toward A) the line is finite, from J to the other side (beyond D) it is infinite; similarly for D. The segment JD is finite, say one span. The line JA is shorter than DA by one span. Razi then argues about the number of 'things' (probably units of length) in the deficient line (JA) versus the excess line (DA). If the numbers were equal, a contradiction would arise: a thing with its other would be like it without its other (reductio). If the number in JA is less, that deficiency must come from the infinite side of the lines (since on the finite side the spans are matched one-to-one). This forces the deficient line to terminate, and since the excess line extends one span beyond the deficient, the excess line also becomes finite. This proves that both lines are finite, contradicting the assumption of an infinite line. Therefore, bodies must be finite. The paragraph concludes that both proofs together establish that every body is finite in magnitude.

Key Terms
غير متناهٍ
متناهٍ
شبر
تطبيق
مراتب الأعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an infinite line can be a proper superset of another infinite line, with the shorter line still being infinite.
Correction: Razi's proof shows that if the difference is finite, the shorter line must terminate, and thus both lines are finite. An infinite line cannot be a proper superset of another infinite line if the difference is finite.

Checkpoint
What does the difference of one span between two assumed infinite lines lead to?
A) The lines become parallel.
B) The deficient line terminates, forcing both lines to be finite.
C) The infinite line becomes finite only on one side.
D) The number of points becomes equal.

Key Takeaway
This proof demonstrates that a finite difference between two lines leads to the finitude of both, thus all bodies must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi’s proof forces you to accept that every physical thing in your life—your body, your possessions, your world—has a finite limit. If that is true, then what in your life are you treating as if it is infinite or limitless? What would it mean to truly acknowledge the finitude of everything you own?
?
The absurdity in the proof is that a part cannot equal the whole. In your pursuit of spiritual growth, have you ever convinced yourself that a small, finite deed (like a single prayer or charity) is 'enough' on its own, without relying on Allah's infinite mercy? How does accepting the finitude of your actions change your need for Him?
?
The difference of one span forced the entire line to be finite. Consider this: one small, finite act of repentance can change your eternal trajectory. Are you holding back from that one span of change because you think your sins are infinite? How does this proof challenge your view of your own limits and Allah's forgiveness?

Two Arguments That Possibility Is Not an Attribute
Razi gives the first two arguments against possibility being an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility cannot be an existent attribute based on the arguments of inherence and priority.

Core Idea
If possibility were existent, it would have to inhere in a non-existent or violate causal priority.

Imam Razi begins his refutation with two quick arguments. The first shows that an attribute cannot exist without a subject, and the second shows that possibility is conceptually prior to existence, so it cannot be an existent entity. These arguments establish the absurdity of treating possibility as a real property.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the structure: a general claim followed by two numbered proofs.
The first proof is about inherence, the second about priority.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that if possibility were existent, it must inhere either in the possible non-existent thing or elsewhere. Both options are impossible, so possibility cannot be an attribute.
The key point is that an attribute cannot inhere in a non-existent.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof uses the priority of possibility over existence. If possibility were existent, then the quiddity would have the existence of something else before its own existence, which is absurd.
This shows a contradiction in temporal or conceptual order.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both proofs lead to the same conclusion: possibility is not an existent attribute but a conceptual relation.
Think about why this matters for kalam discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة، ويدل عليه وجوه.
أحدها: أنكم سلمتم أن الشيء قبل حدوثه ممكن الوجود، فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لكان إما أن تكون قائمة به أو بغيره لا جائز أن تكون قائمة به، والإ لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، ولا جائز أن تقوم بغيره ؛ لأن صفة الشيء لا يعقل قيامها بغيره، كما أن الحركة التي يكون الجسم موصوفًا بها يمتنع أن تكون قائمة بغير ذلك الجسم.
وثانيها : أن اتصاف الماهية بإمكان الوجود سابق على اتصافها بالوجود فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لزم أن يكون اتصاف الماهية بوجود شيء آخر سابقا على اتصافها بوجود نفسها.
وثالثها: أن الإمكان لو كان موجودًا لكان مساويًا لسائر الموجودات في الوجود، ومخالفًا لها في الماهية المخصوصة، فإذن ماهيته غير وجوده، فاتصاف ماهيته بوجوده إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان موجودًا واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن تكون الصفة المفتقرة لذاتها إلى ما يكون ممكنا لذاته وتكون واجبة لذاتها، وهو محال، وإن كان ذلك الاتصاف على سبيل الإمكان لزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان آخر، ويفضي إلى التسلسل.
ورابعها : أن العقول الفعالة والأرواح البشرية والهيولى كلها ممكنة، فيلزم افتقارها إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لا يقال : الممكن إنما يفتقر إلى الهيولى إذا كان محدثًا لأنه قبل حدوثه غير موجود، فلا بد له من شيء آخر يكون موجودًا قبل حدوثه، حتى يكون ذلك الشيء محلا لذلك الإمكان، أما العقل الفعال والهيولى، لما كانا موجودين أزلا ولأبدًا كان إمكانه قائما به فلا يفتقر إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لأنا نقول : ثبوت الإمكان للماهية الممكنة يكون على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي، فإذا كان شرط قيام الإمكان به كونه في نفسه موجودًا وثبوت الإمكان له أمر واجب لذاته، وما كان شرطًا لما كان واجبًا لذاته أولى بأن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون ثبوت الوجوب لهذه الأشياء واجبًا بالذات فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجبًا لذاته، وهو محال.
فثبت بهذه الدلائل أن الإمكان لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة، وبالله التوفيق.
And the answer to the third doubt: That possibility (al-imkan) cannot be an existent attribute; and evidences indicate it.
One: You have conceded that a thing before its occurrence is possible of existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, it would either inhere in it or in something else. It cannot inhere in it, because that would entail an existent inhering in a non-existent; and it cannot inhere in something else, because an attribute of a thing cannot be conceived as inhering in another, just as the motion by which a body is described cannot inhere in another body.
Two: The description of the quiddity (al-mahiyyah) by possibility of existence is prior to its description by existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, then the description of the quiddity by the existence of something else would be prior to its description by its own existence.
Three: If possibility were existent, it would be equal to other existents in existence and different from them in specific quiddity. Thus its quiddity is other than its existence. If its description of its quiddity by its existence is by way of necessity, then it would be existent necessary by itself, so the attribute that is dependent by its essence on something that is possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible. If that description is by way of possibility, then possibility would have another possibility, leading to infinite regress.
Four: The active intellects, human souls, and prime matter (al-hayula) are all possible, so it would follow that they need another prime matter, which is false.
It cannot be said: The possible only needs prime matter if it is originated; because before its occurrence it is non-existent, so there must be another thing existent before its occurrence to be a locus for that possibility. As for the active intellect and prime matter, since they are existent eternally and perpetually, their possibility subsists in themselves, so they do not need another prime matter. That is false.
For we say: The subsistence of possibility for the possible quiddity is by way of essential necessity. If the condition for the subsistence of possibility in it is its being existent in itself, and the subsistence of possibility for it is a necessary matter by itself, then whatever is a condition for that which is necessary by itself is more deserving of being necessary by itself. Thus the subsistence of necessity for these things would be necessary by itself, so the possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible.
So it is established by these evidences that possibility cannot be conceived as an existent attribute. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of the third doubt that claims possibility (imkan) is an existent attribute. The author gives four proofs, then addresses a potential counter-objection.
Proof 1: If possibility were an existent attribute, it must inhere either in the possible thing (before it exists) or elsewhere. Inherence in the non-existent is absurd; inherence elsewhere is impossible because an attribute belongs to its subject.
Proof 2: The priority of possibility to existence: if possibility were existent, then the quiddity would be described by the existence of something else (the possibility) before its own existence, which is absurd.
Proof 3: If possibility were existent, it would have a quiddity distinct from its existence. Its existence would then either be necessary (making the possible necessary) or possible (making another possibility for it, causing infinite regress).
Proof 4: If possibility were an attribute, then possible beings like active intellects and souls would need a locus (hayula) for that attribute, requiring another hayula, ad infinitum.
Then an objector says that for eternal possible beings, possibility subsists in themselves without needing an external locus. Razi rebuts: The subsistence of possibility in such beings is essential (necessary), and if that requires the being itself to be existent, then the being would be self-necessary, contradicting its possibility. Thus, possibility cannot be an existent attribute.

Key Terms
الإمكان
صفة موجودة
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real attribute that exists in the world like other properties.
Correction: The passage shows possibility is a conceptual relation, not an existent entity, because it cannot inhere in a non-existent and it is prior to existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can't possibility inhere in the possible thing before it exists?
A) Because the possible thing is non-existent and an attribute cannot inhere in a non-existent
B) Because possibility is not an attribute
C) Because the possible thing already has an attribute
D) Because inherence is impossible

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not an existent attribute because it cannot inhere in a non-existent and its priority over existence makes it impossible to be a real property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first argument shows that an existent attribute cannot inhere in a non-existent. In your daily life, you often make plans for future events that do not yet exist. On what do you base your certainty that these events are possible? If possibility is not a real property, what does that mean for the foundation of your hopes and fears? Are you placing your trust in a mere mental concept rather than in the One who alone can bring things into existence? Commit to examining your reliance on future outcomes and redirect your trust to Allah's power.
?
The second argument reveals that if possibility were existent, it would have to precede the thing's own existence, leading to a contradiction. Consider an aspect of your character you hope to change—like patience or humility. Before it exists in you, you consider it 'possible' for you to acquire it. But according to this argument, that possibility is not a real attribute. So what is the basis for your efforts? Are you striving based on the mere possibility or on something else? Let this lesson transform your striving into a reliance on Allah's grace to actualize good qualities in you.

The Third Proof: Infinite Regress
Razi gives the third proof: if possibility were existent, it leads to infinite regress or contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that if possibility were existent, it would lead to an infinite regress or a necessary possible.

Core Idea
If possibility were existent, its own existence would require another possibility, leading to infinite regress.

The third proof is a classic kalam argument: if possibility were an existent entity, it would have a quiddity distinct from its existence. Its existence would either be necessary or possible, both leading to contradictions. This shows that possibility cannot be an existent attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a single, self-contained proof.
It uses the distinction between quiddity and existence.
2
Understand the dilemma
If possibility is existent, it has a quiddity and existence. The existence of its quiddity is either necessary or possible. If necessary, then a possible thing becomes necessary; if possible, then that possible would need another possibility, ad infinitum.
Both options are impossible, so the premise must be false.
3
Analyze the necessary case
If the existence of possibility's quiddity is necessary, then the attribute would be necessary by itself, contradicting its role as an attribute of a possible entity.
This would make the possible thing necessary, which is absurd.
4
Analyze the possible case
If the existence is possible, then that possibility would itself require another possibility, leading to an infinite chain of possibilities.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is invalid in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة، ويدل عليه وجوه.
أحدها: أنكم سلمتم أن الشيء قبل حدوثه ممكن الوجود، فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لكان إما أن تكون قائمة به أو بغيره لا جائز أن تكون قائمة به، والإ لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، ولا جائز أن تقوم بغيره ؛ لأن صفة الشيء لا يعقل قيامها بغيره، كما أن الحركة التي يكون الجسم موصوفًا بها يمتنع أن تكون قائمة بغير ذلك الجسم.
وثانيها : أن اتصاف الماهية بإمكان الوجود سابق على اتصافها بالوجود فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لزم أن يكون اتصاف الماهية بوجود شيء آخر سابقا على اتصافها بوجود نفسها.
وثالثها: أن الإمكان لو كان موجودًا لكان مساويًا لسائر الموجودات في الوجود، ومخالفًا لها في الماهية المخصوصة، فإذن ماهيته غير وجوده، فاتصاف ماهيته بوجوده إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان موجودًا واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن تكون الصفة المفتقرة لذاتها إلى ما يكون ممكنا لذاته وتكون واجبة لذاتها، وهو محال، وإن كان ذلك الاتصاف على سبيل الإمكان لزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان آخر، ويفضي إلى التسلسل.
ورابعها : أن العقول الفعالة والأرواح البشرية والهيولى كلها ممكنة، فيلزم افتقارها إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لا يقال : الممكن إنما يفتقر إلى الهيولى إذا كان محدثًا لأنه قبل حدوثه غير موجود، فلا بد له من شيء آخر يكون موجودًا قبل حدوثه، حتى يكون ذلك الشيء محلا لذلك الإمكان، أما العقل الفعال والهيولى، لما كانا موجودين أزلا ولأبدًا كان إمكانه قائما به فلا يفتقر إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لأنا نقول : ثبوت الإمكان للماهية الممكنة يكون على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي، فإذا كان شرط قيام الإمكان به كونه في نفسه موجودًا وثبوت الإمكان له أمر واجب لذاته، وما كان شرطًا لما كان واجبًا لذاته أولى بأن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون ثبوت الوجوب لهذه الأشياء واجبًا بالذات فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجبًا لذاته، وهو محال.
فثبت بهذه الدلائل أن الإمكان لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة، وبالله التوفيق.
And the answer to the third doubt: That possibility (al-imkan) cannot be an existent attribute; and evidences indicate it.
One: You have conceded that a thing before its occurrence is possible of existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, it would either inhere in it or in something else. It cannot inhere in it, because that would entail an existent inhering in a non-existent; and it cannot inhere in something else, because an attribute of a thing cannot be conceived as inhering in another, just as the motion by which a body is described cannot inhere in another body.
Two: The description of the quiddity (al-mahiyyah) by possibility of existence is prior to its description by existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, then the description of the quiddity by the existence of something else would be prior to its description by its own existence.
Three: If possibility were existent, it would be equal to other existents in existence and different from them in specific quiddity. Thus its quiddity is other than its existence. If its description of its quiddity by its existence is by way of necessity, then it would be existent necessary by itself, so the attribute that is dependent by its essence on something that is possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible. If that description is by way of possibility, then possibility would have another possibility, leading to infinite regress.
Four: The active intellects, human souls, and prime matter (al-hayula) are all possible, so it would follow that they need another prime matter, which is false.
It cannot be said: The possible only needs prime matter if it is originated; because before its occurrence it is non-existent, so there must be another thing existent before its occurrence to be a locus for that possibility. As for the active intellect and prime matter, since they are existent eternally and perpetually, their possibility subsists in themselves, so they do not need another prime matter. That is false.
For we say: The subsistence of possibility for the possible quiddity is by way of essential necessity. If the condition for the subsistence of possibility in it is its being existent in itself, and the subsistence of possibility for it is a necessary matter by itself, then whatever is a condition for that which is necessary by itself is more deserving of being necessary by itself. Thus the subsistence of necessity for these things would be necessary by itself, so the possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible.
So it is established by these evidences that possibility cannot be conceived as an existent attribute. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of the third doubt that claims possibility (imkan) is an existent attribute. The author gives four proofs, then addresses a potential counter-objection.
Proof 1: If possibility were an existent attribute, it must inhere either in the possible thing (before it exists) or elsewhere. Inherence in the non-existent is absurd; inherence elsewhere is impossible because an attribute belongs to its subject.
Proof 2: The priority of possibility to existence: if possibility were existent, then the quiddity would be described by the existence of something else (the possibility) before its own existence, which is absurd.
Proof 3: If possibility were existent, it would have a quiddity distinct from its existence. Its existence would then either be necessary (making the possible necessary) or possible (making another possibility for it, causing infinite regress).
Proof 4: If possibility were an attribute, then possible beings like active intellects and souls would need a locus (hayula) for that attribute, requiring another hayula, ad infinitum.
Then an objector says that for eternal possible beings, possibility subsists in themselves without needing an external locus. Razi rebuts: The subsistence of possibility in such beings is essential (necessary), and if that requires the being itself to be existent, then the being would be self-necessary, contradicting its possibility. Thus, possibility cannot be an existent attribute.

الوجود
الوجوب الذاتي

The Fourth Proof and Objection-Answer
Razi gives the fourth proof and then refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof and how Razi refutes an objection by showing it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Eternal possible beings cannot be their own locus for possibility, and the objection's reasoning collapses into self-necessity.

The fourth proof argues that if possibility were an attribute, even eternal possible beings like active intellects would need a locus, leading to an infinite regress. An objector suggests that eternal beings have possibility subsisting in themselves, but Razi shows this implies they are necessary by themselves, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: proof, objection, answer.
This is the final part of the refutation.
2
Understand the fourth proof
The fourth proof says that active intellects, human souls, and prime matter are all possible. If possibility were an attribute, they would each need a prime matter (hayula) to inhere in, but they themselves are prime matter or similar, leading to an infinite need for loci.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection
The objector says that for eternal beings, their possibility is self-subsisting because they are eternally existent, so they don't need an external locus.
The objector tries to limit the need for a locus to originated things only.
4
Analyze Razi's reply
Razi argues that if possibility is self-subsisting for these beings, then the subsistence of possibility in them is essential (necessary). If the condition for that subsistence is the being's existence, then the being's own necessity would be required, making the possible necessary — a contradiction.
This shows the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة، ويدل عليه وجوه.
أحدها: أنكم سلمتم أن الشيء قبل حدوثه ممكن الوجود، فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لكان إما أن تكون قائمة به أو بغيره لا جائز أن تكون قائمة به، والإ لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، ولا جائز أن تقوم بغيره ؛ لأن صفة الشيء لا يعقل قيامها بغيره، كما أن الحركة التي يكون الجسم موصوفًا بها يمتنع أن تكون قائمة بغير ذلك الجسم.
وثانيها : أن اتصاف الماهية بإمكان الوجود سابق على اتصافها بالوجود فلو كان الإمكان صفة موجودة لزم أن يكون اتصاف الماهية بوجود شيء آخر سابقا على اتصافها بوجود نفسها.
وثالثها: أن الإمكان لو كان موجودًا لكان مساويًا لسائر الموجودات في الوجود، ومخالفًا لها في الماهية المخصوصة، فإذن ماهيته غير وجوده، فاتصاف ماهيته بوجوده إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان موجودًا واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن تكون الصفة المفتقرة لذاتها إلى ما يكون ممكنا لذاته وتكون واجبة لذاتها، وهو محال، وإن كان ذلك الاتصاف على سبيل الإمكان لزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان آخر، ويفضي إلى التسلسل.
ورابعها : أن العقول الفعالة والأرواح البشرية والهيولى كلها ممكنة، فيلزم افتقارها إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لا يقال : الممكن إنما يفتقر إلى الهيولى إذا كان محدثًا لأنه قبل حدوثه غير موجود، فلا بد له من شيء آخر يكون موجودًا قبل حدوثه، حتى يكون ذلك الشيء محلا لذلك الإمكان، أما العقل الفعال والهيولى، لما كانا موجودين أزلا ولأبدًا كان إمكانه قائما به فلا يفتقر إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل.
لأنا نقول : ثبوت الإمكان للماهية الممكنة يكون على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي، فإذا كان شرط قيام الإمكان به كونه في نفسه موجودًا وثبوت الإمكان له أمر واجب لذاته، وما كان شرطًا لما كان واجبًا لذاته أولى بأن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون ثبوت الوجوب لهذه الأشياء واجبًا بالذات فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجبًا لذاته، وهو محال.
فثبت بهذه الدلائل أن الإمكان لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة، وبالله التوفيق.
And the answer to the third doubt: That possibility (al-imkan) cannot be an existent attribute; and evidences indicate it.
One: You have conceded that a thing before its occurrence is possible of existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, it would either inhere in it or in something else. It cannot inhere in it, because that would entail an existent inhering in a non-existent; and it cannot inhere in something else, because an attribute of a thing cannot be conceived as inhering in another, just as the motion by which a body is described cannot inhere in another body.
Two: The description of the quiddity (al-mahiyyah) by possibility of existence is prior to its description by existence. If possibility were an existent attribute, then the description of the quiddity by the existence of something else would be prior to its description by its own existence.
Three: If possibility were existent, it would be equal to other existents in existence and different from them in specific quiddity. Thus its quiddity is other than its existence. If its description of its quiddity by its existence is by way of necessity, then it would be existent necessary by itself, so the attribute that is dependent by its essence on something that is possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible. If that description is by way of possibility, then possibility would have another possibility, leading to infinite regress.
Four: The active intellects, human souls, and prime matter (al-hayula) are all possible, so it would follow that they need another prime matter, which is false.
It cannot be said: The possible only needs prime matter if it is originated; because before its occurrence it is non-existent, so there must be another thing existent before its occurrence to be a locus for that possibility. As for the active intellect and prime matter, since they are existent eternally and perpetually, their possibility subsists in themselves, so they do not need another prime matter. That is false.
For we say: The subsistence of possibility for the possible quiddity is by way of essential necessity. If the condition for the subsistence of possibility in it is its being existent in itself, and the subsistence of possibility for it is a necessary matter by itself, then whatever is a condition for that which is necessary by itself is more deserving of being necessary by itself. Thus the subsistence of necessity for these things would be necessary by itself, so the possible by itself would be necessary by itself, which is impossible.
So it is established by these evidences that possibility cannot be conceived as an existent attribute. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of the third doubt that claims possibility (imkan) is an existent attribute. The author gives four proofs, then addresses a potential counter-objection.
Proof 1: If possibility were an existent attribute, it must inhere either in the possible thing (before it exists) or elsewhere. Inherence in the non-existent is absurd; inherence elsewhere is impossible because an attribute belongs to its subject.
Proof 2: The priority of possibility to existence: if possibility were existent, then the quiddity would be described by the existence of something else (the possibility) before its own existence, which is absurd.
Proof 3: If possibility were existent, it would have a quiddity distinct from its existence. Its existence would then either be necessary (making the possible necessary) or possible (making another possibility for it, causing infinite regress).
Proof 4: If possibility were an attribute, then possible beings like active intellects and souls would need a locus (hayula) for that attribute, requiring another hayula, ad infinitum.
Then an objector says that for eternal possible beings, possibility subsists in themselves without needing an external locus. Razi rebuts: The subsistence of possibility in such beings is essential (necessary), and if that requires the being itself to be existent, then the being would be self-necessary, contradicting its possibility. Thus, possibility cannot be an existent attribute.
