Three Objections Against Possibility (Imkan)
Imam Razi enumerates three objections against the conceivability of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections that deny the conceivability of possibility (imkan) and how they challenge the notion of contingent existence.

Core Idea
Possibility itself is under attack: three arguments claim it is unintelligible.

Imam Razi now presents three counter-arguments that aim to show that the very concept of possibility is contradictory. These are not his own view but objections he must refute. Understanding them is crucial for grasping the full dialectic of the kalam proofs for the necessary existence of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and note the three distinct proofs (الحجة الأولى, الثانية, الثالثة). Each tries to disprove the conceivability of possibility.
These are presented as objections to be answered later.
2
Analyze the first objection
Examine how the first objection splits into two cases: existence identical to essence vs. existence distinct from essence, and why both allegedly make possibility unintelligible.
Focus on the logical moves: on identity view, possibility requires non-identity; on distinction view, the subject of possibility becomes problematic.
3
Reflect on the second and third objections
Consider the second objection's dilemma: possibility itself must be existent or non-existent, both dismissed. Then the third objection about the status of non-existents and the impossibility of attributing possibility to essence or existence.
Notice how these objections rely on prior debates (e.g., whether non-existent is a thing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن كل محدث ممكن لذاته لكنه معارض بحجج دالة على أن القول بوجود شيء ممكن الوجود محال الحجة الأولى: أنا إما أن نقول : الوجود نفس الماهية وإما أن نقول: الوجود غير الماهية وعلى كل واحد من القولين فالقول بالإمكان والجواز غير معقول، أما على القول بأن الوجود نفس الماهية فنقول : الإمكان معقول؛ وذلك لأن الشيء الموصوف بالإمكان هو الذي يجوز أن يكون موجودًا ويجوز أن يكون معدوما، والحكم بجواز الوجود والعدم على تلك الماهية يستدعي كون الماهية مقارنة للوجود، ومقارنة للعدم ومتى كان الأمر كذلك امتنع أن يقال : الوجود هو نفس الماهية، وأما على القول بأن الوجود غير الماهية فلأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الماهية أو الوجود أو كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، والكل محال، وإما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان والماهية؛ لأن كل ما كان ممكن الثبوت لم يمتنع تقرره مع عدم ذلك الشيء، فلو قلنا : السواد يمكن أن يكون سوادا كأن معناه السواد يمكن أن يحكم عليه بأنه سواد ومعلوم أن ذلك محال ; لأنه يقتضي أن يكون حال كونه سوادا محكوما عليه بأنه غير سواد، وذلك محال، وإما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الوجود; لأنه يرجع حاصله إلى أن الوجود يمكن أن لا يكون وجودًا وهذا محال باطل أيضاً، وإما أنه يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ; لأن الذي ذكرناه في الماهية وفي الوجود عائد بعينه في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، فثبت أن القول بإمكان الوجود غير معقول سواء قلنا : الوجود عين الماهية أو قلنا : إنه غيرها.
الحجة الثانية : أنا لو فرضنا شيئًا من الأشياء ممكن الوجود لكان ذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون موجودًا أو معدومًا، لا جائز أن يكون موجودًا للوجوه الكثيرة التي ذكرناها في الجواب عن شبه الفلاسفة في قدم الأجسام، ولا جائز أن يكون معدوماً ; لأن الشيء بتقدير أن يكون ممكن الوجود كان موصوفًا في نفسه بالإمكان، وذلك الإمكان رافع للامتناع والامتناع عدم ورفع العدم ثبوت، فوجب أن يكون الإمكان أمرًا ثبوتيًا، ولما كان القول بكون الشيء ممكن الوجود يقتضي أن يكون الإمكان إما أن يكون وصفا سلبيًا أو وصفًا وجوديًا، وكان القسمان باطلين فكان القول يكون الشيء ممكن الوجود باطلا.
الحجة الثالثة: أنا إذا قلنا الشيء يجوز أن يكون موجودا، ويجوز أن يكون معدوما يقتضي تقرر ذلك الشيء حال وصفه بكونه معدوما، وهذا يقتضي كون المعدوم شيئًا، وأنتم لا تقولون به. وأيضا : القائل بأن المعدوم شيء لا يمكنه القول بالإمكان; وذلك لأن الإمكان على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون وصفًا للماهية أو وصفًا للوجود ولا جائز أن يكون وصفًا للماهية ; لأن الماهية يستحيل عليها الانقلاب والتغير، فلا يعقل كون الإمكان وصفًا لها، ولا جائز أن يكون وصفًا للوجود لأن الوجود قبل تجدده وحدوثه ما كان ثابتا ولا متعيناً والإمكان عندكم ثابت قبل الحدوث، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القول بجعل الإمكان صفة للوجود.
We grant that what you mentioned indicates that every originated thing is possible in itself, but it is opposed by proofs indicating that the statement that something is possible of existence is impossible. The first proof: We either say that existence is identical to essence, or we say that existence is other than essence. On each of the two views, the statement of possibility and permissibility is unintelligible. As for the view that existence is identical to essence, we say: possibility is unintelligible; because the thing described by possibility is that which may be existent and may be non-existent, and the judgment of permissibility of existence and non-existence upon that essence requires that the essence be associated with existence and associated with non-existence, and when this is the case, it is impossible to say that existence is identical to essence. As for the view that existence is other than essence, because on this supposition, either that which is described by possibility is the essence, or existence, or the essence being described by existence, and all are impossible. And it is impermissible that the described by possibility be the essence; because everything whose establishment is possible, its establishment is not impossible with the absence of that thing. So if we say 'blackness can be blackness,' its meaning is that blackness can be judged as blackness, and it is known that this is impossible; because it requires that while it is blackness it is judged as non-blackness, and that is impossible. And it is impermissible that the described by possibility be existence; because its conclusion reduces to that existence can be non-existence, and this is also false and absurd. And it is permissible that the described by possibility be the essence qualified by existence; because what we mentioned concerning essence and concerning existence returns exactly in the essence's being qualified by existence. So it is established that the statement of possibility of existence is unintelligible whether we say existence is identical to essence or that it is other than it.
The second proof: If we suppose something to be possible of existence, then that possibility must either be existent or non-existent. It cannot be existent for the many reasons we mentioned in the answer to the philosophers' doubts concerning the eternity of bodies, and it cannot be non-existent; because if a thing is possible of existence, it is described in itself by possibility, and that possibility removes impossibility, and impossibility is non-existence, and the removal of non-existence is a positive [attribute]. So possibility must be a positive thing. And since the statement that something is possible of existence requires that possibility be either a negative attribute or a positive attribute, and both divisions are false, then the statement that something is possible of existence is false.
The third proof: If we say 'something may be existent and may be non-existent,' it requires the subsistence of that thing at the time of its being described as non-existent, and this requires that non-existent is a thing, and you do not believe that. Also, the one who says that non-existent is a thing cannot assert possibility; because on this supposition, possibility is either a description of the essence or a description of existence. It cannot be a description of the essence; because the essence cannot undergo transformation and change, so possibility cannot be conceived as a description of it. And it cannot be a description of existence; because existence before its renewal and occurrence was not established nor determinate, while possibility according to you is established before occurrence. If this is so, then the statement of making possibility a description of existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the conceivability of possibility (imkan). The author grants the previous arguments but then counters them. The first objection analyzes possibility in relation to the debate on whether existence is identical to essence or distinct. It argues that on either view, possibility is unintelligible because it leads to contradictions. The second objection considers whether possibility itself is existent or non-existent, and finds both options problematic, concluding that the very concept of something being possible is false. The third objection argues that saying 'something may be existent and may be non-existent' requires the non-existent to be a thing (which some deny), and even if one accepts that, possibility cannot be attributed to essence or existence coherently. These objections collectively aim to undermine the notion of possibility that the author's earlier proofs sought to establish.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
جواز (jawaz)
سلب (salb)
ثبوت (thubut)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections represent Imam Razi's own final position.
Correction: These are objections he presents to refute; they are not his own views but part of a dialectical argument.

Checkpoint
What do the three objections collectively argue against?
A) The necessity of existence
B) The conceivability of possibility
C) The existence of an agent
D) The temporality of the world

Key Takeaway
The three objections aim to show that possibility (imkan) is not a coherent concept, setting the stage for Razi's refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility itself were not real, then every choice you make would be an illusion. Think of a decision you made today—how does the reality of that choice depend on the concept of possibility? What does that say about your accountability to Allah?
?
The first objection argues that possibility leads to contradictions. Yet, you experience possibilities daily. Do you ever reflect that your ability to choose between good and evil is a gift from Allah that requires a real possibility? How does this shape your gratitude and fear of Him?
?
The second objection claims that possibility must be either existent or non-existent, both absurd. But in your own life, do you treat Allah's possibilities for you as mere abstractions, or do you see them as real opportunities for repentance and growth? What change must you make today to honor the reality of possibility in your relationship with Allah?

Defining Possibility to Resolve Objections
Razi defines possibility and shows its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defines possibility (imkan) to resolve earlier objections and why a preponderator is necessary.

Core Idea
Possibility is not about essence but about a thing's capacity to continue or change.

Imam Razi now responds to the objections raised earlier by clarifying exactly what he means by 'possibility'. He shows that the objections miss the mark because they rely on a different understanding of the term. Once his definition is established, the logical need for a preponderator becomes clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: إمكان, استمرار, بقاء, مرجح.
The Arabic may seem dense; the translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the core definition
Locate how Razi defines 'imkan'. Notice that it is about continuation or change, not about existence per se.
This is the key to resolving the objections.
3
Trace the logical implication
Follow the reasoning: both remaining and not remaining are possible → no inherent preponderance → need for a preponderator. Also note the new question at the end.
This sets up the next part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : مرادنا من الإمكان هو كون الشيء بحيث يجوز أن يستمر على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك، ويجوز أن لا يبقى على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك، وإذا ظهر مرادنا من لفظ الإمكان زالت الشبهات المذكورة.
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : بقاؤه على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك غير ممتنع وعدم بقائه أيضًا على ما كان عليه قبل ذلك غير ممتنع أيضاً، ولما جاز الأمران امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر، إلا لمرجح ، إلا أنه يبقى عليه أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون محدث الأجسام محدثًا، وإن كان قديمًا ولكنه جائز الوجود لذاته، إلا أن
The answer: Our intended meaning of possibility is that the thing is such that it may continue as it was before, and it may not remain as it was before. And when our intended meaning of the term ‘possibility’ becomes clear, the aforementioned doubts disappear. And when you understand this, we say: Its remaining as it was before is not impossible, and its not remaining as it was before is also not impossible. And since both are permissible, the preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. However, it remains to be said: Why is it not permissible that the originator of bodies is originated, even if it is eternal but possible of existence in itself, except that…
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage contains the author's response to the three objections. First, he clarifies his definition of possibility (imkan): it is the property of a thing that it may continue in its present state or may not continue. It is not about essence or existence in the abstract, but about persistence or change. Once this definition is clear, the previous objections dissolve because they were based on a different understanding. Then the author draws a conclusion: if a thing can remain the same or change, neither is impossible, and a preponderator (murajjih) is needed to determine which occurs. The passage ends with a new question: why cannot the originator of bodies be originated itself, even if it is eternal but possible in itself? This seems to be a further point of discussion, possibly leading to the next section.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
استمرار (istimrar)
بقاء (baqa')
مرجح (murajjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possibility refers to the essence of a thing or its existence.
Correction: Razi specifically defines possibility as the thing's capacity to continue or not continue in its present state, not about its essence or existence.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi's definition of possibility resolve the earlier objections?
A) By asserting that possibility is a positive attribute
B) By defining possibility as the ability to continue or change, not as a relation of essence and existence
C) By denying that possibility exists
D) By accepting that possibility is unintelligible

Key Takeaway
Razi's clarification of possibility as a thing's capacity for continuation or change refutes earlier objections and establishes the need for a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your current state—faith, health, or circumstances—will continue indefinitely without needing a preponderator? What would it mean for you to recognize that every moment of your existence depends on God's continuous will?

Razi's definition of possibility as 'may continue or not' forces you to see that nothing in creation is self-sustaining. In what area of your life do you act as if things could remain as they are without God's support? Name one habit you will change today to acknowledge your total dependence on Allah.
?
If the objections to this argument were resolved simply by clarifying the definition, ask yourself: Is there a belief about Allah that you reject because you have misunderstood the terms? What is one theological concept you have struggled with, and how can you ensure you are using the correct definitions from scholars before objecting?

The Objection and the Principle of Intrinsic Difference
The author presents the opponent's objection and states his general principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's objection and the author's fundamental principle that difference does not always require an additional cause.

Core Idea
Not all difference needs an extra cause; some things differ by themselves.

The author faces a sharp objection: if Allah is said to differ by His 'specific essence', does not the word 'specific' imply an added cause? Before diving into the proofs, he first establishes the key principle that difference does not always require an additional attribute. This lesson presents that objection and the principle that will be defended by three arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's claim that the author's position is contradictory. Notice how the opponent uses the phrase 'specific essence' to argue for an additional concept.
2
Identify the author's principle
After stating the objection, the author gives a brief response: difference does not always require an additional matter. Underline this principle.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Consider that if difference always required an external cause, it would lead to infinite regress. This sets the stage for the upcoming proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الخصم ربما قال في هذا الموضع: إن كلامكم في هذا الموضع متناقض ؛ لأنكم تقولون إنه تعالى يخالف خلقه لذاته المخصوصة فقولكم : المخصوصة" يدل على المفهوم الزائد على الذات فكان هذا اعترافًا بأن المخالفة إنما وقعت بأمر زائد على الذات فثبت أن قولكم بأنه تعالى يخالف سائر الذوات لذاته المخصوصة، لا لأمر زائد على الذات كلام متناقض واعلم أنا قبل الخوض في الدليل نذكر ما يزيل هذا الإشكال، فنقول : إنه لا يجب في كل شيء يخالف شيئًا آخر أن تكون مخالفته له لأجل أمر زائد عليه، والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن الشيئين المختلفين لو كان اختلافهما لأجل اختصاصهما بأمر زائد لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون مخالفًا للآخر أو لا يكون، فإن لم يكن مخالفا للآخر امتنع كونه سببًا لأن يصير غيره مخالفًا للآخر، وإن كان مخالفا للآخر وجب أن تكون مخالفته لأجل شيء آخر ، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان، فثبت أنه لا بد من الانتهاء إلى شيئين يختلفان لنفسهما لا لأمر زائد. الوجه الثاني : وهو أن الذاتين إذا كانتا متساويتين من كل الوجوه ثم قامت بكل واحدة منهما صفة مخالفة للصفة القائمة بالذات الأخرى، فعند هذا الفرق بقيت الذاتان كما كانتا متساويتين والصفات كما كانتا ،مختلفتين وما كانتا متساويتين لا تنقلبان البتة ،مختلفتين وما كانتا مختلفتين لا تنقلبان البتة متساويتين، وعند هذا يظهر أن المختلفتين لا يعقلان إلا أن يكونا مختلفتين لذاتيهما، وأما فرض أمرين يختلفان أمر زائد على ذاتيهما فذلك غير معقول. الوجه الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا ذاتا وقامت بها صفة فالذات من حيث إنها هي بد وأن تكون مخالفة لتلك الصفة وإلا لم تكن إحداهما بأن تكون موصوفة والأخرى بأن تكون صفة أولى من العكس، وإذا كانت تلك الذات مخالفة لتلك الصفة كانت مخالفة تلك الذات لتلك الصفة لنفس الذات لا لأمر زائد. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأمور يخالف بعضها بعضا لأنفسها وذواتها، حيث هي هي لا باعتبار صفة قائمة بها.
And know that the opponent might say in this place: Your statement in this place is contradictory; because you say that He (Exalted) differs from His creation by His specific essence, so your saying 'specific' indicates an additional concept beyond the essence, so this is an admission that the difference occurred by an additional matter beyond the essence. Thus it is established that your statement that He (Exalted) is different from other essences by His specific essence, not by an additional matter, is contradictory. And know that before entering into the proof we will mention what removes this difficulty. So we say: It is not necessary in every thing that differs from another thing that its difference from it be due to an additional matter. What indicates this are [several] ways: The first way: that if the difference of two different things were due to their being particularized with an additional matter, then that additional matter would either be different from the other or not. If it is not different from the other, it is impossible for it to be a cause for something else becoming different from the other. If it is different from the other, then its difference must be due to another matter, and that would necessitate either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both of which are impossible. Thus it is established that one must end at two things that differ by themselves, not due to an additional matter. The second way: that if two essences are equal in every aspect, and then an attribute different from the attribute subsisting in the other essence subsists in each of them, then with this distinction the two essences remain equal as they were, and the two attributes remain different as they were; what was equal never becomes different, and what was different never becomes equal. And from this it appears that two different things cannot be conceived except as being different in their essences. As for supposing two things that differ due to an additional matter beyond their essences, that is inconceivable. The third way: that if we suppose an essence and an attribute subsists in it, then the essence in so far as it is itself must be different from that attribute, otherwise neither one would be more entitled to be described and the other to be a description. And if that essence is different from that attribute, then the difference of that essence from that attribute is by the essence itself, not due to an additional matter. So it is established by these ways that one must admit things that differ from each other by themselves and their essences, where they are what they are, not in consideration of an attribute subsisting in them.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponent argues that the phrase 'specific essence' implies an additional concept, making the author's view contradictory. The author responds by stating that difference does not always require an additional matter. He then gives three arguments (wujooh) to prove that some differences are intrinsic. The first argument uses a regress/circularity argument: if difference were due to an additional matter, that matter would itself need a cause for its difference, leading to infinite regress or circularity; hence we must stop at two things that differ in themselves. The second argument considers two equal essences that acquire different attributes: the essences remain equal and the attributes remain different, showing that the difference is not in the essences themselves but in the attributes; this demonstrates that if they were truly different in themselves, they would be different without attributes. The third argument shows that an essence and its attribute are different in themselves, not due to an additional matter. These three ways establish that some things differ intrinsically.

Key Terms
الخصم (opponent)
المفهوم الزائد (additional concept)
الذات (essence)
المخالفة (difference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's main argument?
A) The author's position is contradictory because 'specific' implies an added cause.
B) Allah cannot differ from creation.
C) Difference always requires an attribute.
D) The author is inconsistent in using Arabic.

Key Takeaway
Difference does not always require an additional cause; the opponent's reliance on the word 'specific' is not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is different from His creation,' do you truly believe that difference is by His very essence, or do you subconsciously imagine an extra quality that separates Him? This lesson demands you examine your mental image of Allah—are you adding something to His essence without realizing it? Commit to purifying your belief so that you affirm His uniqueness without attaching any mental additions.
?
The opponent's objection seems logical: if something is 'specific,' it must have an extra feature. Have you ever accepted an argument against your faith simply because it sounded logical, without questioning its hidden assumptions? What assumptions in your own reasoning about Allah might be leading you astray? This week, identify one assumption you hold about Allah and test it against the principle that not every difference needs an extra cause.
?
If difference can be intrinsic, then your own worth is not defined by what you add (achievements, status) but by who you are in Allah's sight. Are you trying to prove your validity by piling on accomplishments, while ignoring the intrinsic value Allah gave you? How would your life change if you stopped seeking validation from extras and rested in your essence as His servant? Write one concrete step to shift your focus from adding to being.

First Argument: The Regress/Circularity Proof
The author presents the first proof against the necessity of an additional cause for difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that refutes the need for an additional cause of difference by showing it leads to infinite regress or circularity.

Core Idea
If difference needed a cause, that cause would itself need a cause, leading to absurdity.

The author now presents his first of three arguments. It is a classic reductio: if every difference required an additional cause, then that cause would itself differ from its effect, requiring yet another cause, and so on. This leads to an unacceptable infinite regress or circularity. The only way to stop is to accept that some things differ in themselves.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
The argument assumes that if two things differ due to an additional matter, that matter must either be different or not different from the other.
2
Trace the two branches
If the additional matter is not different, it cannot cause difference; if it is different, then its own difference requires another cause, leading to regress or circularity.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The only escape is to end at two things that differ by themselves, not by an additional matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الخصم ربما قال في هذا الموضع: إن كلامكم في هذا الموضع متناقض ؛ لأنكم تقولون إنه تعالى يخالف خلقه لذاته المخصوصة فقولكم : المخصوصة" يدل على المفهوم الزائد على الذات فكان هذا اعترافًا بأن المخالفة إنما وقعت بأمر زائد على الذات فثبت أن قولكم بأنه تعالى يخالف سائر الذوات لذاته المخصوصة، لا لأمر زائد على الذات كلام متناقض واعلم أنا قبل الخوض في الدليل نذكر ما يزيل هذا الإشكال، فنقول : إنه لا يجب في كل شيء يخالف شيئًا آخر أن تكون مخالفته له لأجل أمر زائد عليه، والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن الشيئين المختلفين لو كان اختلافهما لأجل اختصاصهما بأمر زائد لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون مخالفًا للآخر أو لا يكون، فإن لم يكن مخالفا للآخر امتنع كونه سببًا لأن يصير غيره مخالفًا للآخر، وإن كان مخالفا للآخر وجب أن تكون مخالفته لأجل شيء آخر ، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان، فثبت أنه لا بد من الانتهاء إلى شيئين يختلفان لنفسهما لا لأمر زائد. الوجه الثاني : وهو أن الذاتين إذا كانتا متساويتين من كل الوجوه ثم قامت بكل واحدة منهما صفة مخالفة للصفة القائمة بالذات الأخرى، فعند هذا الفرق بقيت الذاتان كما كانتا متساويتين والصفات كما كانتا ،مختلفتين وما كانتا متساويتين لا تنقلبان البتة ،مختلفتين وما كانتا مختلفتين لا تنقلبان البتة متساويتين، وعند هذا يظهر أن المختلفتين لا يعقلان إلا أن يكونا مختلفتين لذاتيهما، وأما فرض أمرين يختلفان أمر زائد على ذاتيهما فذلك غير معقول. الوجه الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا ذاتا وقامت بها صفة فالذات من حيث إنها هي بد وأن تكون مخالفة لتلك الصفة وإلا لم تكن إحداهما بأن تكون موصوفة والأخرى بأن تكون صفة أولى من العكس، وإذا كانت تلك الذات مخالفة لتلك الصفة كانت مخالفة تلك الذات لتلك الصفة لنفس الذات لا لأمر زائد. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأمور يخالف بعضها بعضا لأنفسها وذواتها، حيث هي هي لا باعتبار صفة قائمة بها.
And know that the opponent might say in this place: Your statement in this place is contradictory; because you say that He (Exalted) differs from His creation by His specific essence, so your saying 'specific' indicates an additional concept beyond the essence, so this is an admission that the difference occurred by an additional matter beyond the essence. Thus it is established that your statement that He (Exalted) is different from other essences by His specific essence, not by an additional matter, is contradictory. And know that before entering into the proof we will mention what removes this difficulty. So we say: It is not necessary in every thing that differs from another thing that its difference from it be due to an additional matter. What indicates this are [several] ways: The first way: that if the difference of two different things were due to their being particularized with an additional matter, then that additional matter would either be different from the other or not. If it is not different from the other, it is impossible for it to be a cause for something else becoming different from the other. If it is different from the other, then its difference must be due to another matter, and that would necessitate either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both of which are impossible. Thus it is established that one must end at two things that differ by themselves, not due to an additional matter. The second way: that if two essences are equal in every aspect, and then an attribute different from the attribute subsisting in the other essence subsists in each of them, then with this distinction the two essences remain equal as they were, and the two attributes remain different as they were; what was equal never becomes different, and what was different never becomes equal. And from this it appears that two different things cannot be conceived except as being different in their essences. As for supposing two things that differ due to an additional matter beyond their essences, that is inconceivable. The third way: that if we suppose an essence and an attribute subsists in it, then the essence in so far as it is itself must be different from that attribute, otherwise neither one would be more entitled to be described and the other to be a description. And if that essence is different from that attribute, then the difference of that essence from that attribute is by the essence itself, not due to an additional matter. So it is established by these ways that one must admit things that differ from each other by themselves and their essences, where they are what they are, not in consideration of an attribute subsisting in them.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponent argues that the phrase 'specific essence' implies an additional concept, making the author's view contradictory. The author responds by stating that difference does not always require an additional matter. He then gives three arguments (wujooh) to prove that some differences are intrinsic. The first argument uses a regress/circularity argument: if difference were due to an additional matter, that matter would itself need a cause for its difference, leading to infinite regress or circularity; hence we must stop at two things that differ in themselves. The second argument considers two equal essences that acquire different attributes: the essences remain equal and the attributes remain different, showing that the difference is not in the essences themselves but in the attributes; this demonstrates that if they were truly different in themselves, they would be different without attributes. The third argument shows that an essence and its attribute are different in themselves, not due to an additional matter. These three ways establish that some things differ intrinsically.

Key Terms
التسلسل (infinite regress)
الدور (circularity)
المخالفة (difference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what happens if the additional matter is itself different from the other thing?
A) It can still be the cause of difference.
B) It leads to infinite regress or circularity.
C) It automatically makes the things equal.
D) It proves the additional matter is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
If every difference required a cause, we would get an infinite regress; therefore, some differences must be intrinsic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own thinking, where do you tend to attribute the differences between people (e.g., intelligence, status, faith) to external factors rather than to their intrinsic natures? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that habit?
?
Consider an area of your life where you keep asking 'why' (e.g., why do I have this weakness?). Do you ever find yourself in an endless regress of explanations? What would it mean, by contrast, to accept that some things are simply true about you without further cause?
?
This argument says that difference must ultimately be intrinsic. How does that affect the way you view the differences between yourself and others? Will you now look for the intrinsic worth in others instead of attributing differences to mere circumstances?

Second Argument: Equal Essences with Different Attributes
The author presents the second proof using the example of equal essences with differing attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that shows difference cannot be reduced to attributes, because equal essences remain equal even when they have different attributes.

Core Idea
Two equal essences stay equal; only their attributes differ, so difference is in the attributes, not in the essences.

The second argument takes a different approach. Suppose we have two essences that are exactly alike in every way. Then each acquires a different attribute. What happens? The essences remain equal, only the attributes differ. The author argues that if the essences themselves were to become different, that would require a change in their essence, but the attributes alone changed. Therefore, the difference is not intrinsic to the essences but to the attributes. This actually supports the idea that some differences are intrinsic, because the essences themselves do not differ—they remain equal. But the author's point is that if we try to explain all difference by attributes, we end up with a scenario where essences remain equal despite different attributes, showing that difference is not reducible to attributes alone.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Imagine two essences that are exactly equal in every aspect.
2
Add different attributes
Suppose each essence has a different attribute. What happens?
3
Observe the result
The essences stay equal; the attributes stay different. The essences do not become different just because their attributes differ.
4
Draw the conclusion
This shows that if two things are truly different, they must be different in essence, not just by an attribute.

A) The attributes become equal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that your good deeds make you essentially different from others? This lesson shows that attributes do not change essence. What does this mean for your reliance on your own attributes (knowledge, piety) versus your reliance on your essence created by Allah? Can you truly change your essence? Take a moment to repent of pride in attributes that are not truly yours.
?
If difference is essential, then consider your relationship with Allah: you are His creation, He is your Creator. Do you live as if that essential difference is the foundation of your worship? Or do you try to reduce the gap through spiritual achievements? Commit to one act of humility that acknowledges your essence as dependent.
?
The argument says two things cannot differ by additional matter beyond essences. Apply this to your sins: they are accidents, not your essence. Are you holding onto shame from past sins as if they define you? Realize your essence is what Allah created, and He can forgive. Will you let go of that false identity and turn to Him?

Third Argument: Essence vs Attribute Intrinsic Difference
The author presents the third proof that an essence and its attribute are intrinsically different.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that an essence and its attribute must be different in themselves, not due to an additional matter.

Core Idea
An essence and its attribute are intrinsically different; otherwise neither would be more deserving of being the attribute.

The third argument is elegantly simple. Consider any essence and an attribute that subsists in it. The essence, in itself, must be different from the attribute. Why? Because if they were not different, it would be arbitrary which one is the subject and which one is the predicate. This intrinsic difference is not caused by an additional attribute; it is immediate. Thus, we have an example of two things that differ by themselves.

Guided Path
1
Consider an essence and its attribute
Think of an essence (like a body) and an attribute (like blackness) that inheres in it.
2
Ask: Are they different?
Intuitively, the essence and the attribute are not the same thing. They are distinct.
3
Reason why they must differ in themselves
If they were not different, there would be no reason why one is the subject and the other the property. Their difference is intrinsic.
4
Apply the conclusion
This shows that intrinsic difference exists and does not require an additional cause.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that your essence and your attributes are distinct. If you confuse your identity (essence) with your actions or qualities (attributes), how might that lead to misplaced self-worth or despair? In what specific area of your life do you need to separate who you are from what you do, and what step will you take today to act on that distinction?
?
Consider an attribute you struggle with, such as anger or impatience. This lesson proves that attribute is not part of your core self. How does that change your responsibility to manage it? Will you now view it as something external that you can detach from, rather than an inescapable part of you?
?
If everything that describes you (knowledge, power, flaws) is distinct from your essence, then what is your essence before Allah? Does this thought make you more humble or more hopeful? Write one concrete change in your daily worship that this awareness will bring.

The Negative Concept and Equality of Essences: Razi's Critique
Razi discusses the negative concept and argues against equality of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument against the claim that essences are equal because they share a negative concept, and his alternative view that different essences can share common necessary attributes.

Core Idea
Sharing a negative concept does not make essences equal; different things can share common attributes.

Razi addresses a key debate in kalam: does the fact that a negative concept applies to all essences imply that the essences themselves are equal? He argues against this, showing that the opponents' reasoning leads to an unacceptable conclusion. This passage clarifies the relationship between essences and their necessary attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the negative concept
Read the opening sentence where Razi states that the negative concept is common to all essences. Note that this is not disputed; the real dispute is about what follows from it.
The negative concept here is likely 'being self-subsistent' or similar.
2
Analyze the opponents' argument
Identify the opponents' claim: essences are equal in themselves, then each has a necessary attribute. Razi points out that this implies equal things have different necessary attributes, which is rationally unacceptable.
Razi says 'that is not acceptable to the intellect.'
3
Grasp Razi's alternative
Razi's view: essences are different in themselves, but they share a common necessary attribute (being self-subsistent). Thus different things can have equal necessary attributes, which is rationally possible. He gives examples: different things share in being different, opposites share in being opposite.
This resolves the apparent problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة المفهوم السلبي أمرًا مشتركا فيه بين الذوات كلها، وإنما النزاع في أن تلك الحقائق المحكوم عليها بهذا القيد السلبي هل هي متساوية من حيث إنها هي؟ ودليلكم لا يفيد ذلك، واعلم أن الخصوم زعموا أن الذوات متساوية في تمام كونها ذوات، ثم أثبتوا لكل واحد من تلك الذواءة، صفة لازمة، فحاصل قولهم يرجع إلى أن الأشياء المتساوية في تمام الماهية يلزمها لوازم مختلفة، وذلك مما لا يقبله العقل. أما على ما قلنا فإنا زعمنا أن الذوات والحقائق مختلفة في أنفسها إلا أنه يلزمها لازم مشترك، وهو كونها أمورًا قائمة بأنفسها غنية عن محال تحل فيها، فحاصل قولنا يرجع إلى أن الأشياء المختلفة يلزمها لوازم متساوية . وهذا غير ممتنع في العقول ؛ إذ المختلفات متشاركة في كونها مختلفة والمتضادات متساوية في كونها متضادة.
The fifth issue: The negative concept is a common thing among all essences. But the dispute is about whether those realities that are judged by this negative condition are equal inasmuch as they are themselves? Your proof does not indicate that. Know that the opponents claim that essences are equal in their complete being essences, then they posit for each of those essences a necessary attribute. The upshot of their statement is that things that are equal in complete quiddity entail different necessary attributes, and that is not acceptable to the intellect. As for what we have said, we claim that essences and realities are different in themselves, except that a common necessary attribute entails them, namely being self-subsistent things, independent of any place in which they inhere. So the upshot of our statement is that different things entail equal necessary attributes. And this is not impossible in intellects, for different things share in being different, and opposites are equal in being opposites.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the fifth issue concerning the negative concept (المفهوم السلبي). Razi first states that the negative concept is common to all essences. The real debate, he explains, is whether the realities that are qualified by this negative condition are themselves equal. He then critiques the opponents' position: they claim essences are equal in being essences, then add necessary attributes to each, which would mean that equal things have different necessary attributes—an unacceptable conclusion for the intellect. Razi then presents his own view: essences are inherently different, but they share a common necessary attribute (being self-subsistent). Therefore, different things can have equal necessary attributes, and this is not rationally impossible. He illustrates this with examples: different things share in being different, and opposites share in being opposite.

Key Terms
المفهوم السلبي
الذوات
اللوازم
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a negative concept is common to all essences, then the essences themselves must be equal.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the commonality of the negative concept does not necessitate equality of the essences; essences can be different yet share a common necessary attribute.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say is the true point of dispute in the fifth issue?
A) Whether the negative concept is common to all essences
B) Whether the essences qualified by the negative condition are equal in themselves
C) Whether necessary attributes can be different for equal essences
D) Whether different things can share a common attribute

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that sharing a negative attribute does not necessitate equality of essences; rather, different essences can share common necessary attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says that different things can share a common necessary attribute without being equal. Think of a belief you hold that you share with many others. Does that shared belief make your essence the same as theirs? What does this imply about your unique responsibility before Allah?
?
The opponents mistakenly thought that a common negative concept forces equality of essences. Have you ever equated yourself with someone else because you share a trait (like being Muslim), leading you to overlook your own distinct duties? How might this lesson correct that error?
?
Razi illustrates that opposites share the attribute of being opposite. Consider two conflicting desires within your own heart. How does recognizing their shared nature (both being desires) help you see your own essence as distinct and accountable for choosing between them?

Fourth Proof: Logical Necessity Requires Distinction
Razi uses logic of predication to show necessity qualifies relation, not divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the logic of predication to show that necessity qualifies the relation (copula), not the divine essence, thereby establishing the distinction between essence and existence in God.

Core Idea
Necessity is a quality of the relation, not the subject.

This lesson presents Razi’s fourth proof, which draws on logical principles to argue that the necessity of existence must be a quality of the relation between subject and predicate, not of the subject itself. By analyzing the statement 'The Creator must exist,' Razi shows that for necessity to be intelligibly attributed, the Creator’s reality must be distinct from His existence. This proof is foundational for understanding the divine attributes in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical framework
Learn that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are qualities of the relation (copula) between subject and predicate, not of the subject itself. This is established in logic.
2
Examine the example
Analyze the example 'Man must be an animal.' Identify subject (man), predicate (animal), copula (subsistence of animal for man), and necessity as a quality of that copula.
3
Apply to the Creator
Apply the same logic to 'The Creator must exist.' Here the subject is the Creator, predicate is existence, and necessity qualifies the attribution relation (copula).
4
Grasp the conclusion
Realize that for the attribution of necessity to be meaningful, the subject (Creator's reality) must be distinct from the predicate (existence). Thus, essence and existence are distinct in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : ثبت في علم المنطق أن الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان كيفيات لنسب المحمولات إلى الموضوعات مثلاً إذا قلنا : " الإنسان يجب أن يكون حيوانا. فالإنسان هو الموضوع، والحيوان هو المحمول، وثبوت الحيوان للإنسان هو النسبة، المسماة بالرابطة، ثم هذه النسبة موصوفة بالوجوب، وهذا الوجوب كيفية لهذه وهي النسبة، وهذا كلام حق معقول. إذا عرفت هذا فن قول : إذا قلنا : " يجب أن يكون الباري تعالى موجوداً فالباري هو الموضوع ، والموجود هو المحمول، وإسناد الموجود إلى تلك الحقيقة هو الرابطة، والوجوب كيفية لهذه النسبة ،والرابطة وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يصر إثبات وجوب الوجود في حق الباري تعالى معقولاً ، إلا إذا قلنا إن حقيقته مغايرة لوجوده.
The fourth proof: It is established in logic that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are qualities of the relations of predicates to subjects. For example, when we say: 'Man must be an animal.' Man is the subject, animal is the predicate, and the subsistence of animal for man is the relation, called the copula. Then this relation is described with necessity, and this necessity is a quality of this relation. And this is correct and intelligible speech. When you know this, we say: When we say: 'The Creator (may He be exalted) must exist', the Creator is the subject, existence is the predicate, and the attribution of existence to that reality is the copula, and necessity is a quality of this relation. And if this is the case, then proving the necessity of existence with respect to the Creator (may He be exalted) is not intelligible unless we say that His reality is other than His existence.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الرابعة) based on logical analysis. It establishes that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are qualities (كيفيات) of the relation between predicate and subject (نسب المحمولات إلى الموضوعات), not of the subject itself. Using the example 'Man must be an animal', the copula رابطة is the relation, and necessity describes it. Applying this to 'The Creator must exist', the subject is the Creator, the predicate is existence, and necessity qualifies the attribution relation. For this attribution to be meaningful, the subject (Creator) must be distinct from the predicate (existence), just as in the human case. Therefore, God's reality (حقيقة) must be other than His existence.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان
كيفيات
نسب
المحمولات
الموضوعات
الرابطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think necessity qualifies the subject itself (e.g., God's essence is necessary).
Correction: The proof shows that necessity actually qualifies the relation (copula) between subject and predicate, not the subject. Thus, necessity of existence is a relational property, not an essential one.

Checkpoint
In the logical analysis, what is necessity a quality of?
A) The subject
B) The predicate
C) The relation (copula)
D) The essence

Key Takeaway
Necessity is always a quality of the relation (copula), not of the subject, so for God, necessity of existence implies a real distinction between essence and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'Allah is Necessary' without thinking. After studying this proof, are you aware that the necessity you affirm is a quality of the relation between God and existence, not a property of God's essence itself? How does this realization change how you silently regard God's nature?
?
If necessity qualifies the relation, not the subject, then your own existence is purely contingent—a gift, not a given. Are you living today as if you are self-sufficient? In what specific decision or habit will you consciously acknowledge that you could cease to exist at any moment?
?
The proof concludes that God's reality is other than His existence. Does this truth make you pause in awe of the divine mystery, or do you find it unsettling? How will you cultivate a deeper reverence for the unsearchable reality of God beyond what your mind can grasp?

The Subtle Distinction Between Qadir and Mujib
Razi summarizes the entire discussion on the distinction between a voluntary agent and a necessitating cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the distinction between a voluntary agent (qadir) and a necessitating cause (mujib) in Islamic theology, and why Imam Razi considers it one of the subtlest intellectual investigations.

Core Idea
The qadir-mujib distinction is one of the most subtle topics in theology.

Imam Razi concludes a profound discussion by highlighting that the difference between a capable agent and a necessitator is among the most delicate intellectual investigations. This lesson unpacks why this distinction matters and why it requires careful thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice the concluding tone: 'So this is the totality...'
Razi is summarizing, not introducing new arguments.
2
Consider why Razi calls this 'subtlest'
Reflect on the meaning of 'adhaqq' (أذق). Why would distinguishing a voluntary agent from a necessitator be especially subtle?
Think about the implications for divine will and causality.
3
Reflect on the importance of the distinction
Ask yourself: How does this distinction affect our understanding of God's actions? Why would Islamic theologians care deeply about this?
This connects to debates about free will and determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مجموع الكلام في الفرق بين القادر وبين الموجب، وهو من أذق المباحث العقلية.
So this is the totality of the discourse on the distinction between the capable agent (qadir) and the necessitator (mujib), and it is among the subtlest intellectual investigations.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This sentence serves as a conclusion and transition. Razi states that what has been presented covers the entire discussion on the difference between a voluntary agent (qadir) and a necessitating cause (mujib). He notes that this topic is one of the subtlest (adhaqq) intellectual investigations, highlighting its difficulty and importance.

Mental Statement is Distinct from Knowledge and Will
Al-Razi proves that mental judgment is not knowledge, belief, power, or will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mental judgment (al-hukm al-dhihni) is not knowledge or belief, nor power or will, and how al-Razi proves this using the example of mentally asserting a known falsehood.

Core Idea
You can mentally say something false while knowing it's false — that shows mental judgment is not knowledge.

Al-Razi tackles a subtle distinction: the mental statement we form when we say something in our mind is not the same as knowing, believing, willing, or having power. This lesson walks through his proof, which relies on a simple but powerful example: you can mentally assert a false proposition while simultaneously knowing it is false. This reveals that the mental judgment is a distinct entity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial claim
Al-Razi first notes that spoken statements indicate a mental judgment, which is clearly not power or will. Recognize that this sets the stage for the real question: could it be knowledge or belief?
2
Grasp the confusion
The only confusion is whether the mental judgment is knowledge or belief. Al-Razi aims to prove it is not.
3
Follow the core proof
He gives an example: while knowing the world is not eternal, you can mentally say 'the world is eternal'. The mental judgment occurs, but no knowledge or belief of that falsehood exists. This proves distinctness.
The key is that the mind can construct false propositions even when the truth is known.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objector might say the mental judgment is just a supposition (farḍ) or estimation (taqdir). Al-Razi replies that even if so, it is still not knowledge, belief, power, or will, so the point stands.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The conclusion is that mental request is distinct from will and mental judgment distinct from knowledge and belief. This is a novel contribution according to al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر الذهني فنقول : لا شك أن قولنا باللسان: قام زيد، وضرب عمرو، يدل على حكم ذهني وإسناد عقلي، وهذا الحكم الذهني والإسناد العقلي ظاهر أنه ليس من جنس القدرة والإرادة، إنما الذي يقع فيه الاشتباه أن يقال: إن هذا الحكم الذهني هو الاعتقاد أو العلم، فإذا بينا بالبرهان أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ظهر أن الخبر القائم بالنفس معنى مغاير للعلوم والاعتقادات ومغاير للقدر والإرادات وذلك هو المراد من الخبر القائم بالنفس. وإنما قلنا: إن هذا الحكم الذهني ليس من جنس العلوم والاعتقادات، وذلك لأني حال ما أكون عالمًا بأن العالم ليس بقديم يمكنني أن أقول في الذهن : العالم قديم، وذلك لأن الذهن كما يمكنه تركيب القضايا الصادقة فكذلك يمكنه تركيب القضايا الكاذبة، والقضايا الكاذبة الذهنية يكون ذلك الحكم الكاذب فيها حاصلا في الذهن، والعلم بها والاعتقاد فيها غير حاصل فههنا الكلام في القضايا الكاذبة التي يكون كذبها معلوما، حصل الحكم الذهني، ولم يحصل العلم والاعتقاد، وهذا يدل قطعًا على أن الحكم الذهني مغاير للعلم والاعتقاد. فإن قلنا : هذا الحكم الذهني عبارة عن فرض يفرضه الذهن، وتقدير بقدره. قلنا : هب أن الأمر على ما قلتم إلا أن هذا الفرض وهذا التقدير ليس من باب العلوم والاعتقادات ولا من باب القدر والإرادات فكان معنى مغايرا لها وهو المطلوب، وذلك لا يختلف بأن سميتموه فرضًا وتقديرًا، أو لا تسمونه بذلك، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الطلب الذهني مغاير للإرادة وأن الحكم الذهني مغاير للعلم والاعتقاد. ومن أنصف علم أن هذا التنقرير والتلخيص لم يتيسر ممن تقدمنا .
As for the mental statement (al-khabar al-dhihni), we say: There is no doubt that our saying with the tongue: 'Zayd stood' and 'Amr struck' indicates a mental judgment (hukm dhihni) and a rational attribution (isnad aqli). This mental judgment and rational attribution are clearly not of the kind of power (qudra) and will (irada). The only point of confusion is to say: This mental judgment is belief (i'tiqad) or knowledge (ilm). If we demonstrate by proof that it is not so, it becomes clear that the statement subsisting in the soul is a meaning distinct from knowledges and beliefs and distinct from power and wills; that is what is intended by the statement subsisting in the soul. And we say: This mental judgment is not of the kind of knowledges and beliefs, because when I am knowing that the world is not eternal, I can say in the mind: 'The world is eternal.' This is because the mind can construct true propositions just as it can construct false propositions. In false mental propositions, that false judgment occurs in the mind, but knowledge and belief about them do not occur. Here we are speaking of false propositions whose falsehood is known; the mental judgment occurs, yet knowledge and belief do not occur. This conclusively indicates that the mental judgment is distinct from knowledge and belief. If it is said: This mental judgment is a supposition (farḍ) that the mind supposes and an estimation (taqdir) it estimates, we say: Grant that it is as you say, but this supposition and estimation are not of the class of knowledges and beliefs nor of the class of power and wills; so it is a meaning distinct from them, which is the desired conclusion. That does not change whether you call it supposition and estimation or not. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the mental request is distinct from will and that the mental judgment is distinct from knowledge and belief. And whoever is fair knows that this exposition and summary has not been facilitated for anyone before us.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the mental statement (khabar dhihni) is distinct from knowledge, belief, power, and will. Al-Razi first notes that spoken statements indicate a mental judgment and rational attribution, which are clearly not power or will. The confusion lies in thinking this mental judgment is belief or knowledge. He then proves it is not knowledge: one can mentally assert a false proposition (e.g., 'the world is eternal') while knowing it is false; thus the mental judgment occurs without knowledge or belief. He then addresses an objection that it is merely supposition or estimation, answering that even if so, it is still not of the class of knowledge, belief, power, or will. The conclusion is that mental request is distinct from will, and mental judgment distinct from knowledge and belief.

Key Terms
الخبر الذهني (al-khabar al-dhihni)
الحكم الذهني (al-hukm al-dhihni)
العلم (al-ilm)
الاعتقاد (al-i'tiqad)
الإرادة (al-irada)
القدرة (al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mental judgment is the same as knowledge or belief because both involve the mind.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that one can have a mental judgment (e.g., 'the world is eternal') while simultaneously knowing it is false, which proves the mental judgment is not knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that the mental judgment is not knowledge?
A) By showing that knowledge is always true while mental judgments can be false.
B) By giving the example of mentally asserting a falsehood while knowing it is false.
C) By arguing that the mind cannot construct false propositions.
D) By stating that mental judgments are not spoken aloud.

Key Takeaway
The mental statement is a distinct mental entity, not reducible to knowledge, belief, power, or will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally entertain a falsehood that you know is false, like 'the world is eternal', does that disturb you? What does it reveal about the control you have over your own thoughts? How can you train your mind to only entertain propositions that are true?
?
If mental judgments are distinct from will, then some thoughts arise in your mind without your direct volition. How does this affect your sense of responsibility for your inner speech? Are you accountable for thoughts you did not will? How can you cultivate a mindset that aligns your mental statements with what you truly know and believe?
?
Al-Razi's proof shows that knowledge and mental judgment are not the same. Have you ever acted on a mental judgment that you knew was false? What was the consequence? How can you ensure that your actions follow from true knowledge and not mere supposition?

The Meaning of 'Nazar Ila': Vision or Physical Gaze?
Razi presents the debate on whether 'nazar ila' denotes vision or physical gaze.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the Arabic expression 'nazar ila' denotes actual vision or the physical act of directing the eye, and the evidence advanced for the vision interpretation.

Core Idea
The term 'nazar ila' is contested: does it mean seeing or just looking?

In this chapter, Razi examines a key theological-linguistic question: Does the phrase 'nazar ila' (to look at) refer to vision (ru'ya) or the physical act of turning the eye? The answer has implications for understanding verses about seeing God. Razi presents both sides and then gives the arguments of those who say it means vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very structured argumentative style.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage begins by stating two views: some Ash'aris claim 'nazar ila' is for vision; some Ash'ari verifiers and most Mu'tazila say it means facing or turning the pupil.
This is a lexical dispute with theological consequences.
3
Examine the Quranic arguments
Two verses are cited: 7:143 (Moses) and 88:17 (camels). For each, understand how the argument connects the meaning of nazar to vision rather than physical eye movement.
The Moses verse has two aspects: avoiding direction and volition-sequencing.
4
Consider the poetic evidence
Al-Nabigha and another poet use 'nazar' in ways that imply vision, such as making it an exception from 'ru'ya' or describing it as what decides the lover.
Poetry is used to support lexical usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع: في بيان أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى هل هو موضوع للرؤية أم لا؟ وقد اختلف الناس فيه، فزعم بعض أصحابنا أنه للرؤية، وأنكر ذلك بعض المحققين منا وجمهور المعتزلة وقالوا : لفظ النظر موضوع إما لكون الحدقة مقابلة للمرئي، كما يقال جبلان متناظران أي متقابلان وإما لتقليب الحدقة السليمة نحو المرئي، طلبا للرؤية. واحتج من قال بأن لفظ النظر المقرون بـ" إلى " للرؤية بالقرآن والشعر. أما القرآن فآيتان الأولى : قوله تعالى : رَبِّ أَرني أنظر إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] والاستدلال به من وجهين : الأول: أنه لو كان النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي لكان معنى الآية : أرني حتى أقلب حدقتي إلى جهتك، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام قد أثبت الجهة الله تعالى، وذلك باطل. الثاني : أنه رتب النظر على الإرادة والمرتب على الإرادة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة فيدل هذا على أن النظر هو الرؤية. الآية الثانية: قوله تعالى : أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِفَتْ [الغاشية: ١٧] والذي يفيد معرفة كيفية الخلقة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة. وأما الشعر : فقول النابغة : وما رأيته إلا نظرة عرضت وم الإمارة والمأمور معذور استثنى النظر عن الرؤية، فوجب أن يكون النظر من جنس الرؤية. وقال الآخر : نظرت إلى من حسن الله وجهه فيا نظرة كادت على وامق تقضي ومعلوم أن الذي يقضي على الوامق هو رؤية المعشوق، لا تقليب الحدقة نحوه. وأما الذين أنكروا كون النظر المقرون بـ" إلى " مفيدا للرؤية، فقد احتجوا بخمسة وعشرين وجها
Chapter Four: On explaining whether the word 'nazar' (looking) combined with the letter 'ila' (to) is used for vision or not. People have differed on it. Some of our companions claim that it is for vision, while some of the verifiers among us and the majority of the Mu'tazila deny that and say: The word 'nazar' is used either for the pupil facing the seen object, as it is said 'two mountains are mutanadhiran' meaning 'facing each other', or for turning the healthy pupil towards the seen object in search of vision. And the one who said that the word 'nazar' combined with 'ila' is for vision argued by the Quran and poetry. As for the Quran, there are two verses. The first is His statement, the Most High: 'My Lord, show me, that I may look at You' (7:143). There are two aspects of inference from it. First: if 'nazar' were an expression for turning the pupil towards the direction of the seen object, then the meaning of the verse would be: 'Show me so that I may turn my pupil towards Your direction', and this would imply that Moses, peace be upon him, affirmed a direction for God Most High, and that is false. Second: he made 'nazar' consequent upon volition, and what is consequent upon volition is vision, not turning the pupil, so this indicates that 'nazar' is vision. The second verse is His statement, the Most High: 'Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?' (88:17). And what benefits knowledge of the manner of creation is vision, not turning the pupil. As for poetry: the saying of al-Nabigha: 'I did not see him except a glance that occurred...' he made an exception of 'nazar' from 'ru'ya', so it is necessary that 'nazar' be of the same kind as vision. And another said: 'I looked at the one whose face God beautified, O a glance that almost...' and it is known that what decides the lover is the vision of the beloved, not turning the pupil towards him. As for those who denied that 'nazar' combined with 'ila' indicates vision, they have argued with twenty-five proofs...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Four, which investigates whether the expression 'nazar ila' (to look at) is lexically designated for actual vision (ru'ya) or for the physical act of directing the eye. Razi reports two positions: (1) Some Ash'aris claim it means vision. (2) Some Ash'ari verifiers and most Mu'tazila say it means either the pupil facing the object or turning the healthy pupil towards it in quest of vision. The proponents of the vision view then present evidence. From the Quran: First, the verse of Moses (7:143) — if 'nazar' meant turning the pupil, then Moses would be asking to turn his pupil towards God, implying a direction for God, which is false; moreover, 'nazar' is made dependent on volition (arada: 'arani'), and what is dependent on volition is vision, not a physical act. Second, the verse about camels (88:17) — knowing how they are created comes from vision, not simply turning the eye. From poetry: Al-Nabigha uses 'nazar' as an exception from 'ru'ya', so 'nazar' must be a kind of vision. Another poet uses 'nazara ila' and says it almost decides the lover, which can only be through vision. The passage ends by mentioning that the opponents have twenty-five proofs, but they are not listed here, only the beginning of their argument is referenced.

B) The verse about camels shows that nazar must be vision because it results in knowledge of their creation.
C) Al-Nabigha's poetry uses nazar as a synonym for ru'ya.
D) The verb 'nazara' is always followed by 'ila' in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The expression 'nazar ila' is argued by some to mean vision, based on Quranic and poetic evidence, but the debate is not settled here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Moses' cry, 'My Lord, show me, that I may look at You,' do you feel the same desperation to see Allah? Or have you become satisfied with just 'turning your pupil'—going through outward acts of worship without the inward vision? What would it take for you to sincerely desire that vision today?
?
The Mu'tazila say that 'nazar' is merely physically turning the eye in search of vision. Where in your life have you substituted the search for Allah with mere mechanical worship? Your prayer, your fasting—are they just turning a pupil, or are they truly seeking vision of the Divine? Make a concrete commitment to change one act today.
?
Al-Nabigha's poetry contrasts 'nazar' and 'ru'ya', showing they are of the same kind. When you experience a moment of clarity about Allah (e.g., from reflecting on creation), do you treat it as mere intellectual 'looking' or as true vision that should transform you? What will you do to make such 'nazar' lead to deeper 'ru'ya'?

The Dispute over 'Nazar Ila' and Vision
Razi introduces the dispute over 'nazar ila' and presents two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the semantic dispute over the phrase 'nazar ila' and its theological significance for discussions about seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'nazar ila' is contested—does it imply vision or just physical direction?

Razi opens the nineteenth issue by presenting a key linguistic dispute with theological implications. Before we can debate whether humans can see Allah, we must agree on what it means to 'look at' something. This passage sets up the two opposing views: some say 'nazar ila' directly means vision, while others argue it only describes the physical act of turning the eye.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Notice how Razi frames the issue: he states the question, then reports two opinions. Identify the key phrase 'النظر المقرون بحرف إلى'.
The passage is a clear statement of a linguistic dispute.
2
Identify the two opposing views
One group claims 'nazar ila' is for vision (ru'ya). The other group denies this and gives alternative meanings. Who are these groups?
The first group is 'some of our companions' (Ash'aris); the second includes 'some of our investigators' and the majority of the Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's linguistic argument
They propose two possible meanings for 'nazar': either the pupil facing the object, or turning the pupil toward it seeking vision. Note the example of two mountains facing each other.
This shows 'nazar' is about physical orientation, not necessarily perception.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
This debate directly affects the interpretation of Qur'anic verses about 'looking at Allah' (e.g., 75:22-23). If 'nazar' implies vision, then seeing Allah is possible; if not, it remains an open question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر: الفصل الرابع: في بيان أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى هل هو موضوع للرؤية أم لا؟ وقد اختلف الناس فيه، فزعم بعض أصحابنا أنه للرؤية، وأنكر ذلك بعض المحققين منا وجمهور المعتزلة وقالوا : لفظ النظر موضوع إما لكون الحدقة مقابلة للمرئي، كما يقال جبلان متناظران أي متقابلان وإما لتقليب الحدقة السليمة نحو المرئي، طلبا للرؤية.
The nineteenth issue: The fourth chapter: Concerning the clarification of whether 'nazar' (looking) combined with the preposition 'ila' (at) is semantically designated for vision (ru'ya) or not. People have differed regarding it. Some of our companions (Ash'aris) claimed that it is for vision, and some of our investigators (muhaqqiqun) and the majority of the Mu'tazila denied that, saying: The word 'nazar' is semantically designated either for the pupil facing the visible object, as it is said 'two mountains facing each other' (mutanāẓirān), i.e., facing each other, or for turning the healthy pupil toward the visible object, seeking vision.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage introduces the nineteenth issue (al-mas'ala al-tāsi'a 'ashar) and the fourth chapter (al-faṣl al-rābi‘) concerning whether the phrase 'nazar ilā' (looking at) is semantically designated for vision (ru'ya) or not. It states that people have differed on this: some of our (Ash‘ari) scholars claim it is for ru'ya, while some of our investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) and the majority of the Mu‘tazila deny this, stating that the word 'nazar' is either for the pupil facing the visible object (as in 'two mountains facing each other') or for turning the healthy pupil toward the visible object in seeking vision.

Key Terms
النظر
الرؤية
الحدقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'nazar' in Qur'anic verses (e.g., 'nazar ila Allah') necessarily means seeing with the eyes.
Correction: The passage shows that linguistic analysis reveals 'nazar' primarily denotes physical orientation or direction of the eye, not the actual vision itself, as argued by the Mu'tazila and some Ash'ari investigators.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what is the meaning of 'nazar'?
A) It means vision (ru'ya) directly.
B) It means the pupil facing the visible object or turning the pupil toward it seeking vision.
C) It means intellectual contemplation.
D) It means the act of pointing.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'nazar ila' is linguistically contested—whether it directly denotes vision or merely physical orientation—and this dispute is foundational for theological debates about the beatific vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a theological belief you hold firmly—how much of it rests on your assumption that a certain Arabic word means exactly what you think it means? If that word were open to a different interpretation, would your belief still stand? What will you do this week to test your assumptions about key terms in the Quran?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that 'nazar ila' does not guarantee vision; it is only a physical orientation seeking vision. When you consider your own longing to see Allah, is that hope built on the certainty of the word 'look' or on a deeper trust in Allah's promise? If the linguistic evidence left room for doubt, would your hope falter? How can you anchor your hope beyond the meaning of a single word?
?

Are you willing to embrace uncertainty where scholars themselves differed, or will you seek a firm conviction through further study?
