Quranic Proofs that 'Nazar Ila' Means Vision
Razi presents two Quranic verses with logical arguments for 'nazar ila' meaning vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses two Quranic verses to argue that the phrase 'nazar ila' refers to actual vision (ru'ya), not merely physical eye movement.

Core Idea
Quranic usage proves 'nazar ila' is about seeing, not just turning the eye.

Razi begins his proof by turning to the Quran, the primary source of linguistic authority in Islamic theology. He cites two verses that, according to the proponents of vision, demonstrate that 'nazar ila' is used for actual seeing, not mere physical direction of the eye. This lesson unpacks those arguments and their logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses and arguments
First read the Arabic passage containing the two verses and the two respects from the first verse. Then read the translation. Understand the structure: one verse with two logical points, another verse with one point.
2
Examine the first argument from verse 1
The first argument from Quran 7:143 shows that if 'nazar' meant turning the pupil, Moses would be attributing direction to God, which is impossible. Consider why this is a valid reductio ad absurdum.
This is a key theological point against anthropomorphism.
3
Examine the second argument from verse 1
The second argument notes that Moses asked to see upon his desire; what is desired is actual vision, not pupil movement. So 'nazar' here must mean vision.
4
Examine the argument from verse 2
The second verse (88:17) says 'Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?' Knowledge of creation requires vision, not just directing the eye. Therefore 'nazar' here means vision.
This verse is about ordinary physical sight, but it still uses 'nazar ila' for actual vision.
5
Reflect on the cumulative case
Consider how both verses together build a case that 'nazar ila' denotes vision in both divine and ordinary contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال بأن لفظ النظر المقرون بـ "إلى" للرؤية بالقرآن والشعر. أما القرآن فآيتان: الأولى: قوله تعالى: رَبِّ أَرني أنظر إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: 143] والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: أنه لو كان النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي لكان معنى الآية: أرني حتى أقلب حدقتي إلى جهتك، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام قد أثبت الجهة لله تعالى، وذلك باطل. الثاني: أنه رتب النظر على الإرادة والمرتب على الإرادة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة فيدل هذا على أن النظر هو الرؤية. الآية الثانية: قوله تعالى: أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ [الغاشية: 17] والذي يفيد معرفة كيفية الخلقة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة. وأما الشعر: فقول النابغة: وما رأيته إلا نظرة عرضت والإمارة والمأمور معذور استثنى النظر عن الرؤية، فوجب أن يكون النظر من جنس الرؤية. وقال الآخر: نظرت إلى من حسن الله وجهه فيا نظرة كادت على وامق تقضي ومعلوم أن الذي يقضي على الوامق هو رؤية المعشوق، لا تقليب الحدقة نحوه.
And the one who said that the word 'nazar' combined with 'ila' is for vision (ru'ya) adduced proof from the Qur'an and poetry. As for the Qur'an, there are two verses: The first: His statement exalted: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You (anẓuru ilayk)' [al-A'raf 7:143]. Deriving evidence from it is in two respects: First: if 'nazar' were an expression for turning the pupil toward the direction of the visible object, the meaning of the verse would be: 'Show me so that I may turn my pupil toward Your direction,' and this would require that Moses (peace be upon him) affirmed direction (jiha) for God exalted, and that is invalid. Second: he [Moses] ordered looking (nazar) upon desire (irāda), and what is ordered upon desire is vision (ru'ya), not turning the pupil; so this indicates that 'nazar' is vision. The second verse: His statement exalted: 'Do they not look at (yanẓurūn ilā) the camels, how they are created' [al-Ghashiya 88:17]; and that which benefits knowledge of how creation is is vision, not turning the pupil. As for poetry: the statement of al-Nabigha: 'I did not see him except a look that appeared / and the command and the commanded are excused' – he excluded 'nazar' from 'ru'ya', so it is necessary that 'nazar' be of the same genus as 'ru'ya'. And another said: 'I looked at (naẓartu ilā) the one whose face God beautified / and what a look that nearly passed sentence on the passionate lover' – and it is known that that which passes sentence on the passionate lover is the vision of the beloved, not turning the pupil toward him.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the proof from those who argue that the phrase 'nazar ilā' (looking at) is designated for vision (ru'ya). The argument uses two Qur'anic verses and two poetic examples. First, from Qur'an 7:143, where Moses asks to see God: 'Show me, that I may look at You.' The argument proceeds in two respects: (a) if 'nazar' meant turning the pupil, the request would be absurd because it would imply God has a direction; (b) Moses ordered looking upon desire, and what is ordered upon desire is actual vision, not pupil movement. Second, from Qur'an 88:17: 'Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?' – knowing the creation requires vision, not just directing the eye. Then two poetic examples: al-Nabigha's line distinguishes 'nazar' from 'ru'ya', implying they are of the same genus since one excludes the other; the second poem describes a look that nearly kills the lover, which clearly refers to vision of the beloved, not just pupil movement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume 'nazar ila' simply means to direct one's gaze without necessarily seeing.
Correction: Razi shows that Quranic usage equates 'nazar ila' with actual vision (ru'ya), as evidenced by the logical implications of the verses.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two arguments from Quran 7:143 used to prove that 'nazar ila' means vision?
A) If 'nazar' meant turning the pupil, Moses would be affirming a direction for God.
B) 'Nazar' cannot be commanded.
C) The verse is metaphorical.
D) Moses was not serious.

Key Takeaway
The Quran uses 'nazar ila' to mean actual vision, not merely eye movement, as shown by the nonsense of implying direction for God and the need for vision to know creation.

?
Moses asked to see God out of desire, not mere curiosity. Is your desire to see God (in the Hereafter) a true longing that drives your actions today? If not, what is blocking that desire?
?
Razi uses logic to show that 'nazar ila' cannot mean physical eye movement because that would imply direction for God. Are there assumptions in your daily life where you unintentionally attribute direction or limitation to God? How can you correct that?

Poetic Proofs that 'Nazar Ila' Means Vision
Razi presents two poetic citations as linguistic evidence for 'nazar ila' meaning vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses two examples from pre-Islamic poetry to support the claim that 'nazar ila' refers to vision (ru'ya).

Core Idea
Poetic usage confirms that 'nazar ila' means real seeing, not just eye movement.

In addition to the Quran, Razi invokes the authority of pre-Islamic Arabic poetry (al-Nabigha and a second poet) to demonstrate natural linguistic usage. These examples show that native speakers understood 'nazar ila' as vision. This lesson examines how exclusion and consequence in poetry prove the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic examples and analysis
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Nabigha's line and the second line. Notice how Razi uses them as evidence.
2
Understand al-Nabigha's argument
Al-Nabigha says 'I did not see him except a look that appeared' – the word 'except' (istithna) excludes 'nazar' from 'ru'ya', which only works if they are of the same category. That implies 'nazar' falls under 'ru'ya' as a type.
This is an argument from exclusion (istithna).
3
Understand the second poet's argument
The second poet describes a look (nazra) that nearly killed the passionate lover. The effect (near death) is caused by seeing the beloved, not by merely turning the pupil. So 'nazar' must refer to the actual sight.
This is an argument from consequence.
4
Reflect on the combined evidence
Consider how both poetic examples converge on the same point: 'nazar ila' means vision in natural Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال بأن لفظ النظر المقرون بـ "إلى" للرؤية بالقرآن والشعر. أما القرآن فآيتان: الأولى: قوله تعالى: رَبِّ أَرني أنظر إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: 143] والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: أنه لو كان النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي لكان معنى الآية: أرني حتى أقلب حدقتي إلى جهتك، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام قد أثبت الجهة لله تعالى، وذلك باطل. الثاني: أنه رتب النظر على الإرادة والمرتب على الإرادة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة فيدل هذا على أن النظر هو الرؤية. الآية الثانية: قوله تعالى: أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ [الغاشية: 17] والذي يفيد معرفة كيفية الخلقة هو الرؤية لا تقليب الحدقة. وأما الشعر: فقول النابغة: وما رأيته إلا نظرة عرضت والإمارة والمأمور معذور استثنى النظر عن الرؤية، فوجب أن يكون النظر من جنس الرؤية. وقال الآخر: نظرت إلى من حسن الله وجهه فيا نظرة كادت على وامق تقضي ومعلوم أن الذي يقضي على الوامق هو رؤية المعشوق، لا تقليب الحدقة نحوه.
And the one who said that the word 'nazar' combined with 'ila' is for vision (ru'ya) adduced proof from the Qur'an and poetry. As for the Qur'an, there are two verses: The first: His statement exalted: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You (anẓuru ilayk)' [al-A'raf 7:143]. Deriving evidence from it is in two respects: First: if 'nazar' were an expression for turning the pupil toward the direction of the visible object, the meaning of the verse would be: 'Show me so that I may turn my pupil toward Your direction,' and this would require that Moses (peace be upon him) affirmed direction (jiha) for God exalted, and that is invalid. Second: he [Moses] ordered looking (nazar) upon desire (irāda), and what is ordered upon desire is vision (ru'ya), not turning the pupil; so this indicates that 'nazar' is vision. The second verse: His statement exalted: 'Do they not look at (yanẓurūn ilā) the camels, how they are created' [al-Ghashiya 88:17]; and that which benefits knowledge of how creation is is vision, not turning the pupil. As for poetry: the statement of al-Nabigha: 'I did not see him except a look that appeared / and the command and the commanded are excused' – he excluded 'nazar' from 'ru'ya', so it is necessary that 'nazar' be of the same genus as 'ru'ya'. Commentary
This passage presents the proof from those who argue that the phrase 'nazar ilā' (looking at) is designated for vision (ru'ya). The argument uses two Qur'anic verses and two poetic examples. First, from Qur'an 7:143, where Moses asks to see God: 'Show me, that I may look at You.' The argument proceeds in two respects: (a) if 'nazar' meant turning the pupil, the request would be absurd because it would imply God has a direction; (b) Moses ordered looking upon desire, and what is ordered upon desire is actual vision, not pupil movement. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think poetic usage is figurative and not literal.
Correction: Razi uses poetry as evidence of the literal linguistic usage, arguing that native speakers employ 'nazar ila' for genuine vision.

Checkpoint

B) 'Nazar' and 'ru'ya' are the same genus.
C) The poet never saw him.
D) The line is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Pre-Islamic poetry confirms that 'nazar ila' means actual vision, as shown by exclusion (istithna) and the effect of seeing the beloved.

First Objection from Quran 7:198 and Its Refutation
Razi presents an objection using Quran 7:198 and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes an objection that uses Quran 7:198 to argue that 'nazar' is different from vision.

Core Idea
The same verse can be used for opposite claims; context interprets key terms.

In this passage, Razi presents the first objection against the view that 'nazar' (looking) means vision (ru'ya). The objector cites Quran 7:198, where God says of the idols, 'You see them looking at you while they do not see.' The objector uses two arguments to show that 'nazar' cannot be vision. Razi then anticipates a counter-argument and provides a refutation by explaining that 'nazar' in this verse means 'facing' rather than vision. This lesson walks you through the objection, its two lines of reasoning, and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage along with its full translation. Notice how the objection is structured: it first states the verse, then gives two arguments (wajh).
Pay attention to the key terms: النظر, الإبصار, الرؤية.
2
Identify the two arguments in the objection
The objector derives evidence in two respects. First, the verse affirms 'nazar' and negates 'ibsar', showing they are different. Second, God sees their looking, but vision itself is not seen, so looking cannot be vision.
The second argument relies on the claim that 'ru'ya' is not seen.
3
Understand the anticipated counter-argument and response
The objector anticipates a response: the verse refers to idols, which cannot turn pupils, so 'nazar' cannot mean turning the pupil. Razi's answer: here 'nazar' means 'facing' (taqabul), like two mountains facing each other.
This shows that the same word can have different meanings in different contexts.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: How does this refutation preserve the possibility that 'nazar' can mean vision in other contexts? The answer: the verse is not about vision at all; it's about physical orientation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: وَتَرَاهُمْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَيْكَ وَهُمْ لَا يُبْصِرُونَ [الأعراف: 198] وجه الاستدلال من وجهين: الأول: أنه أثبت النظر وبقي الإبصار، وهذا يدل على أن النظر غير الإبصار. الثاني: أنه تعالى حكم بأنه يرى نظرهم إليه ولا شك أن الرؤية لا ترى، ولما كان النظر مرتئيا والرؤية غير مرئية وجب أن لا يكون النظر هو الرؤية. فإن قيل: إنه تعالى أثبت النظر للأصنام بقوله تعالى: وَتَرَاهُمْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: 198] ولا شك أنه لم يحصل للأصنام تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فوجب أن لا يكون النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة. قلنا: النظر هنا مفسر بالتقابل، يقال: جبلان متناظران، أي متقابلان، وهذا المعنى كان حاصلا للأصنام.
The first proof: His statement exalted: 'And you see them looking at you while they do not see (yubṣirūn)' [al-A'raf 7:198]. The manner of deriving evidence is in two respects: First: He affirmed looking (nazar) and negated seeing (ibṣār), and this indicates that looking is other than seeing. Second: He exalted decreed that He sees their looking at Him, and there is no doubt that vision (ru'ya) is not seen (lā turā). Since looking is seen (murtā'an? or mar'iyyan?) and vision is not seen, it is necessary that looking is not vision. If it is said: He exalted affirmed looking for the idols by His statement: 'And you see them looking at you' [al-A'raf 7:198], and there is no doubt that for the idols there did not occur turning of the pupil toward the direction of the visible object, so it is necessary that looking is not an expression for turning the pupil. We say: Looking here is interpreted as facing (taqābul); it is said: 'two mountains facing each other (mutanāẓirān)', i.e., facing each other, and this meaning was present for the idols.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) against the view that 'nazar' means vision. The objector uses Quran 7:198, which says 'You see them looking at you while they do not see.' The objection proceeds in two respects: (1) The verse affirms 'nazar' and negates 'ibṣār', showing they are different. (2) God sees their looking, but vision (ru'ya) itself cannot be seen; since looking is seen and vision is not, looking cannot be vision. The objector then anticipates a counter: one might argue that the verse refers to idols who cannot turn pupils, so 'nazar' cannot mean pupil movement. The answer (qulnā) is: here 'nazar' is interpreted as 'facing' (taqābul), as in 'two mountains facing each other,' and this meaning (the idols being positioned facing the Prophet) is applicable to the idols.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'nazar' in Quran 7:198 refers to vision or physical looking.
Correction: Razi explains that in this context 'nazar' means 'facing' (taqābul), as in two mountains facing each other.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in this passage, how is 'nazar' interpreted in Quran 7:198 when applied to idols?
A) As vision (ru'ya).
B) As turning the pupil (taqlīb al-ḥadaqa).
C) As facing (taqābul).
D) As metaphorical knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that 'nazar' in Quran 7:198 means 'facing', not vision, so the verse does not challenge the equation of nazar with vision in other contexts.

The Second Objection from Quran 3:77 and Its Refutation
Razi presents the second objection from Quran 3:77 and refutes it in detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the metaphorical interpretation of 'nazar' as turning the pupil to reconcile Quran 3:77 with God's vision.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'nazar' in the Quran must be compatible with God's attributes.

Razi addresses a key challenge: Quran 3:77 says God does not look at the disbelievers, yet He sees them. This apparent contradiction is resolved by showing that 'nazar' here is metaphorical, meaning something other than vision. The passage presents the objection, a counter-objection, and a detailed refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the flow: objection, counter-objection, and refutation.
Pay attention to the key terms: nazar, ru'ya, taqlib al-hadaqa, rahma, majaz.
2
Identify the objection and counter-objection
The objection uses Quran 3:77 to argue that nazar is different from ru'ya. The counter-objection says that if nazar means turning the pupil, it would be absurd for God.
The counter-objection is a potential problem for the objector's own argument.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
Razi argues that interpreting nazar as turning the pupil allows a metaphorical reading (abandonment of mercy), while interpreting it as vision does not. He provides two reasons: the natural link between turning the pupil and love/mercy, and the fact that both are voluntary acts.
This shows the strength of metaphorical interpretation over literal.
4
Reflect on the role of metaphor
Consider why metaphor (majaz) is a valid tool in kalam. The passage shows how linguistic analysis can resolve theological issues.
Metaphor must be based on a real connection (mulazama or mushābaha) between the literal and figurative meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قوله تعالى في صفة الكفار: وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ [آل عمران: 77] نفي كونه تعالى ناظرا إليهم، ولا شك أنه كان يراهم فيلزم أن يكون النظر غير الرؤية. وإن قيل: لو كان النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة لكان معنى الآية أنه تعالى لا يقلب حدقته إليهم، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل. قلنا: لو جعلنا النظر حقيقة في تقليب الحدقة أمكن حمل قوله تعالى: وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ [آل عمران: 77] على ترك الرحمة، أما لو جعلناه حقيقة في الرؤية لا يمكن حمله على ترك الرحمة، فكان الأول أولى. والذي يدل على ما قلناه إن تقليب الحدقة إلى جانب الشيء لا يختاره الإنسان إلا إذا أحبه ومتى أحبه فإنه يرحمه ظاهرا، فحصل بين تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة الشيء وبين إيصال الرحمة إليه هذه الملازمة. وأيضا تقليب الحدقة جهة الشيء وإيصال الرحمة إليه فعلان اختياريان، فحصل بينهما هذه المشابهة والملازمة وكل واحد منهما سبب مستقل لحسن المجاز، أما لو جعلنا النظر عبارة عن الرؤية لم يحصل بينه وبين الرحمة لا الملازمة ولا المشاركة أما الملازمة فلأنه ليس كل ما يراه الإنسان أحبه بل الذي يختاره الإنسان فإنه يحبه، لكن اختبار الرؤية ليس إلا تقليب الحدقة نحوه، فثبت أن حمل النظر على تقليب الحدقة أولى.
The second proof: His statement exalted describing the disbelievers: 'And He does not look at them (walā yanẓuru ilayhim)' [Al 'Imran 3:77] – negating that He exalted is a looker at them, and there is no doubt that He was seeing them, so it follows that looking (nazar) is other than vision (ru'ya). And if it is said: if looking (nazar) were an expression for turning the pupil, the meaning of the verse would be that He exalted does not turn His pupil toward them, and it is known that that is false. We say: if we make 'nazar' a reality (ḥaqīqa) in turning the pupil, it would be possible to interpret His statement exalted 'And He does not look at them' as abandonment of mercy (tark al-rahma); but if we make it a reality in vision, it cannot be interpreted as abandonment of mercy, so the first is more appropriate. And what indicates what we said is that turning the pupil toward something is not chosen by a person unless he loves it, and whenever he loves it, he has mercy on it manifestly; so there occurs between turning the pupil toward the direction of the thing and conveying mercy to it this concomitance (mulāzama). Also, turning the pupil toward the direction of the thing and conveying mercy to it are two voluntary acts, so between them there occurs this similarity (mushābaha) and concomitance, and each one of them is an independent cause for the appropriateness of metaphor (majāz). However, if we make 'nazar' an expression for vision, there does not occur between it and mercy either concomitance or participation; as for concomitance, because not everything a person sees he loves; rather, what he chooses he loves. But the choice (ikhtiyār) of vision is nothing but turning the pupil toward it. So it is established that interpreting 'nazar' as turning the pupil is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage gives the second objection (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) from Quran 3:77, which says that God does not look at the disbelievers. The objector argues: God certainly sees them, so 'looking' here cannot mean vision; it must be different. Then an objection within the objection: if 'nazar' means turning the pupil, then the verse would absurdly mean God does not turn His pupil toward them. The answer refutes this by showing that interpreting 'nazar' as turning the pupil allows a metaphorical interpretation: the verse means God abandons mercy toward them. This works because turning the pupil toward something is a voluntary act associated with love and mercy, making it a good metaphor for mercy. If 'nazar' meant vision, no such metaphorical connection to mercy exists, because seeing is not necessarily tied to love. So the interpretation of 'nazar' as turning the pupil is more appropriate.

Key Terms
الحجة
النظر
الرؤية
تقليب الحدقة
الرحمة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If 'nazar' means turning the pupil, then Quran 3:77 would describe God as having a physical eye that turns, which is anthropomorphic.
Correction: Razi shows that 'nazar' here is used metaphorically to mean abandonment of mercy, not a physical action. The literal meaning of turning the pupil is the basis for the metaphor, not a literal description of God.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that interpreting 'nazar' as turning the pupil is better in the context of Quran 3:77?
A) Because it avoids anthropomorphism.
B) Because it allows the verse to be interpreted metaphorically as abandonment of mercy.
C) Because turning the pupil is the literal meaning.
D) Because vision cannot be negated of God.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that a metaphorical interpretation of 'nazar' as turning the pupil best reconciles Quran 3:77 with God's attribute of vision.

First Proof from Quran: Nazar Is Not Ru'ya
Razi presents first Quranic proof that nazar is not ru'ya with two aspects and objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 7:198 to argue that nazar (looking) is distinct from ru'ya (vision), and how he responds to a counterargument about idol worship.

Core Idea
The Quran itself distinguishes nazar from ru'ya in a single verse.

Imam Razi begins his theological demonstration by turning to the Quran. He selects a verse from Surah Al-A'raf to argue that looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) are not the same thing. The verse provides two logical angles, and then he anticipates and answers an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the verse from Al-A'raf: 'And you see them looking at you while they do not see.' This is the foundation of the proof.
The verse uses two different words: 'yandhurūna' (looking) and 'yubṣirūna' (seeing).
2
Identify the first deductive aspect
The first aspect: the verse affirms looking (nazar) and denies seeing (ibsār) for the same subject (the idols). This shows they are not the same because they can be separated.
3
Identify the second deductive aspect
The second aspect: Allah sees their looking, but the act of seeing (ru'ya) is not itself seen. Since looking is seen and ru'ya is not, they cannot be identical.
This is a logical distinction based on visibility.
4
Consider the objection and reply
An objector says: if looking is eye movement, then idols did not have that, so looking cannot mean eye movement. Razi replies: here looking means mutual facing (taqābul), which applies to idols.
The reply redefines nazar in this context.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Understand why this distinction matters: if nazar and ru'ya were the same, the verse would be contradictory. The proof upholds that Allah is not seen in this world (a key Ash'ari position).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَنَرَنهُمْ يَنْظُرُونَ إِلَيْكَ وَهُمْ لَا يُبْصِرُونَ) [الأعراف: ١٩٨] وجه الاستدلال من وجهين : الأول : أنه أثبت النظر وبقي الإبصار، وهذا يدل على أن النظر غير الإبصار. الثاني : أنه تعالى حكم بأنه يرى نظرهم إليه ولَا شك أن الرؤية لا ترى، ولما كان النظر مرتبًا، والرؤية غير مرئية وجب أن لا يكون النظر هو الرؤية. فإن قيل: إنه تعالى أثبت النظر للأصنام بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَتَرَاهُمْ يَنْظُرُونَ إِلَيْكَ) [الأعراف: ۱۹۸] ولا شك أنه لم يحصل للأصنام تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فوجب أن لا يكون النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة. قلنا : النظر هنا مفسر بالتقابل ، يقال : جبلان متناظران، أي متقابلان، وهذا المعنى كان حاصلا للأصنام.
The first proof: His saying, exalted: 'And you see them looking at you while they do not see.' The aspect of deduction is in two ways: First: He affirmed looking (nazar) and negated seeing (ibsār), and this indicates that looking is other than seeing. Second: He, exalted, judged that He sees their looking at Him, and there is no doubt that vision (ru'ya) is not seen. And since looking is seen and vision is not seen, it is necessary that looking is not vision. If it is said: He, exalted, affirmed looking for the idols by His saying: 'And you see them looking at you' and there is no doubt that the idols did not have the turning of the eye-pupil toward the direction of the seen object, so it must be that looking is not the turning of the eye-pupil. We say: Looking here is interpreted as facing (taqābul). It is said: two mountains are 'mutanāzirān', i.e., facing each other, and this meaning was present for the idols.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran distinguishing nazar from ru'ya. The author presents two deductive aspects from the verse. First, the verse affirms nazar and denies ibsār for the same subject, showing they are not identical. Second, Allah sees their nazar, but ru'ya itself is unseeable, so they differ. An objection follows: Allah affirmed nazar for idols without eye movement, so nazar cannot mean eye-pupil turning. The reply: nazar here means mutual facing (taqābul), as in two mountains facing each other, which applies to idols.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
إبصار (ibsār)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlīb al-ḥadaqa)
تقابل (taqābul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar (looking) and ru'ya (vision) are synonymous and refer to the same act of seeing.
Correction: The Quranic verse distinguishes them: the idols are described as 'looking' but not 'seeing', showing they are different. Additionally, Allah sees their looking, but vision itself cannot be seen, further proving distinction.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse demonstrates that nazar and ru'ya are not identical, and nazar can mean mutual facing rather than physical eye movement.

Second Quranic Proof: Nazar ≠ Ru'ya
Razi presents a second Quranic proof that nazar ≠ ru'ya, with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 3:77 to argue that nazar (looking) is distinct from ru'ya (vision), and why he interprets nazar as turning the eye-pupil to allow a metaphorical reading about mercy.

Core Idea
Looking (nazar) is not seeing (ru'ya); it is a voluntary act that implies care.

In this lesson, we encounter Imam Razi's second qur'anic argument for distinguishing nazar from ru'ya. He cites the verse where Allah says He does not look at the disbelievers, even though He undoubtedly sees them. This sparks a debate about the meaning of 'nazar' and leads to a rich discussion about metaphor and divine mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the core argument
Start by reading the verse (Al Imran 3:77) and Razi's initial inference: since Allah sees but does not look, nazar and ru'ya must differ.
Notice that the verse negates looking, not seeing.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if nazar means turning the eye-pupil, the verse would be absurdly anthropomorphic. Grasp why that objection arises.
3
Follow Razi's reply
Razi replies by arguing that interpreting nazar as pupil-turning allows the verse to be read metaphorically as 'withholding mercy'. This is plausible because turning the pupil is voluntary and linked to love and mercy.
He contrasts this with interpreting nazar as ru'ya, which would make the verse about mere sight and hard to interpret as mercy.
4
Analyze the correlation (mulāzama)
Examine the reasoning: turning the pupil toward something is a voluntary act that shows love, and love leads to mercy. This correlation supports the metaphorical use.
Razi emphasizes that both actions are voluntary, strengthening the metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قوله تعالى في صفة الكفار : وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ ﴾ [آل عِمرَان : ٧٧] تقي كونه تعالى ناظرا إليهم، ولا شك أنه كان يراهم فيلزم أن يكون النظر غير الرؤية. وإن قيل : لو كان النظر عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة لكان معنى الآية أنه تعالى لا يقلب حدقته إليهم، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل. قلنا : لو جعلنا النظر حقيقة في تقليب الحدقة أمكن حمل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا ينظر إليهم [آل عمران : ۷۷] على ترك الرحمة، أما لو جعلناه حقيقة في الرؤية لا يمكن حمله على ترك الرحمة، فكان الأول أولى. والذي يدل على ما قلناه إن تقليب الحدقة إلى جانب الشيء لا يختاره الإنسان إلا إذا أحبه ومتى أحبه فإنه يرحمه ظاهراً، فحصل بين تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة الشيء وبين إيصال الرحمة إليه هذه الملازمة. وأيضًا تقليب الحدقة جهة الشيء وإيصال الرحمة إليه فعلان اختياريان، فحصل بينهما هذه المشابهة والملازمة وكل واحد منهما سبب مستقل لحسن المجاز، أما لو جعلنا النظر عبارة عن الرؤية لم يحصل بينه وبين الرحمة لا الملازمة ولا المشاركة أما الملازمة فلأنه ليس كل ما يراه الإنسان أحبه بل الذي يختاره الإنسان فإنه يحبه، لكن اختيار الرؤية ليس إلا تقليب الحدقة نحوه، فثبت أن حمل النظر على تقليب الحدقة أولى.
The second proof: His saying, exalted, describing the disbelievers: 'And He does not look at them' [Al Imran 3:77] restricts His being a looker at them, and there is no doubt that He used to see them, so it follows that looking is other than vision. And if it is said: If looking were the turning of the eye-pupil, the meaning of the verse would be that He does not turn His pupil toward them, and it is known that that is false. We say: If we consider looking as a reality in turning the eye-pupil, it is possible to interpret His saying 'He does not look at them' as abandonment of mercy, whereas if we consider it a reality in vision, it cannot be interpreted as abandonment of mercy, so the first is more appropriate. And what indicates what we said is that turning the eye-pupil toward a thing is not chosen by a person except if he loves it, and whenever he loves it he shows mercy to it outwardly, so this correlation (mulāzama) occurs between turning the eye-pupil toward a thing and conveying mercy to it. Also, turning the eye-pupil toward a thing and conveying mercy to it are two voluntary acts, so this similarity and correlation occur between them, and each one is an independent cause for the goodness of metaphor. As for if we consider looking to be the same as vision, no correlation or participation occurs between it and mercy. As for the correlation, because not everything a person sees does he love, rather what he chooses does he love, but the choice of vision is nothing but turning the eye-pupil toward it. So it is established that interpreting looking as turning the eye-pupil is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a second Quranic proof using the verse that Allah does not look at the disbelievers. Since He sees them (ru'ya), but does not look (nazar), they must differ. An objection argues that if nazar means eye-pupil turning, the verse would be absurd. The author replies by interpreting nazar as a basis for metaphorical abandonment of mercy. A detailed analysis follows: turning the pupil correlates with love and mercy, both being voluntary acts, so a metaphor is valid. If nazar were vision, no such correlation exists. This supports interpreting nazar as pupil turning.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlīb al-ḥadaqa)
ملازمة (mulāzama)
مجاز (majāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He does not look at them' refers to physical sight, meaning Allah does not see them.
Correction: The verse is about mercy, not physical seeing. Allah sees all, but He does not look with care toward disbelievers, i.e., He withholds mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is interpreting nazar as turning the eye-pupil better than interpreting it as vision?
A) Because it avoids attributing a pupil to Allah.
B) Because it allows the verse to be understood as a metaphor for withholding mercy.
C) Because it makes the verse apply to all people.
D) Because it makes nazar identical to ru'ya.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that nazar (looking) is a voluntary act of attention distinct from ru'ya (vision), enabling a metaphorical interpretation of divine mercy in Quran 3:77.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fourth Proof: Looking Without Seeing
Imam Razi presents a linguistic proof that nazar can occur without ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic proof from common speech that looking (nazar) is distinct from seeing (ru'ya), and why this distinction is foundational for the Ash'ari position on vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Looking and seeing are not the same: you can look without seeing.

Imam Razi now presents a concise but powerful linguistic proof. He points to ordinary language: when someone says 'I looked at the crescent but did not see it,' they affirm the act of looking while denying seeing. This everyday expression, he argues, shows that nazar and ru'ya are two separate realities. This distinction is crucial for later discussions about whether Allah can be seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how simple and direct the proof is—it uses a common saying.
The proof relies on ordinary language, not complex theology.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The speaker affirmatively says 'I looked' (nazartu) and then negates seeing (lam ara). This shows that looking is affirmed while seeing is denied, proving they are distinct.
3
Connect to broader debates
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter? In kalam, it supports the idea that one can 'look' for Allah (through nazar) without necessarily seeing Him, unless He grants vision.
This proof is often used in arguments about ru'yat Allah (vision of Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المألة التاسعة عشر
يقال : نظرت إلى الهلال فلم أره، أثبت النظر مع عدم الرؤية.
The nineteenth issue. It is said: I looked (nazartu) at the crescent but did not see (ara) it. This affirms looking (nazar) in the absence of seeing (ru'ya).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic proof. The statement 'I looked at the crescent but did not see it' shows that nazar can occur without ru'ya, proving they are not identical. The speaker affirms nazar while negating ru'ya, so the two must be different acts.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
هلال (hilāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That looking and seeing are the same.
B) That looking is possible without seeing.
C) That seeing requires looking.
D) That the crescent is invisible.

Key Takeaway
Looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) are distinct acts; one can look without seeing, as proven by everyday Arabic speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek Allah through prayer, study, or reflection, do you sometimes feel you 'see' nothing in return? Does the fact that looking (nazar) is distinct from seeing (ru'ya) change your expectation? Are you willing to keep looking even when the crescent remains unseen?

Think of a time you gave up seeking a truth because you didn't 'see' results quickly. How does this lesson's demonstration that looking can be valid without seeing call you to re-evaluate your persistence in the path of faith?

The Visibility of Looking vs. Seeing
Imam Razi presents a linguistic proof for the distinction between nazar and ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for distinguishing nazar (looking) from ru'ya (seeing) based on the property of being visible.

Core Idea
Looking is visible; seeing is not — thus they are different.

Imam Razi now presents a simple but powerful linguistic proof. By examining everyday language, he shows that we can observe someone's act of looking, but we never observe the act of seeing itself. This observable difference reveals that nazar and ru'ya are not the same reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note the rhetorical question and its conclusion.
The passage uses a common expression: 'Do you not see how so-and-so looks...'
2
Identify the two premises
The passage states that looking (nazar) is visible (we see someone looking) and that seeing (ru'ya) is not visible (we see objects, not the act of seeing). Recognize these as the key premises.
3
Trace the conclusion
Since nazar is visible and ru'ya is not, they must be different realities. Understand how this linguistic observation leads to a metaphysical distinction.
This is a valid argument from different properties to different essences.
4
Contrast the two terms
Reflect on the difference between nazar as a physical/visible act and ru'ya as an invisible mental or spiritual perception.
This proof is one of several arguments for their distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامة : يقال : أما ترى كيف ينظر فلان إلى الهلال؟ وكيف ينظر فلان إلى فلان؟ وهذا يدل على أن النظر مرئي، ولا شك أن الرؤية غير مرئية، فوجب أن يكون النظر مغايرا للرؤية.
The fifth proof: It is said: Do you not see how so-and-so looks at the crescent? And how so-and-so looks at so-and-so? This indicates that looking (nazar) is visible, and there is no doubt that seeing (ru'ya) is not visible, so it is necessary that looking is different from seeing.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof based on common speech. When we ask 'Do you not see how he looks?' we are observing the act of looking itself, meaning nazar is visible and perceptible to others. In contrast, ru'ya (vision) is not itself seen; we see objects, not the act of seeing. Therefore, nazar and ru'ya must be different realities.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
مرئي (mar'ī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since both nazar and ru'ya are acts of perception, they are identical.
Correction: The proof shows that nazar is visibly observable, while ru'ya is not, so they must be distinct.

Checkpoint
What characteristic of nazar does the fifth proof highlight?
A) It is invisible.
B) It is voluntary.
C) It is visible.
D) It is identical to ru'ya.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that nazar (looking) and ru'ya (seeing) are distinct realities because one is visible and the other is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the act of looking is visible, then your pursuit of truth is observable to others. Does that thought comfort you or convict you? In what way does your outward 'looking' reflect—or fail to reflect—an inner desire for truth? Write down one change you will make so that your looking aligns with what you claim to seek.

Sixth Proof: Goal and Means Are Different
Imam Razi presents the sixth proof that nazar leads to ru'ya but is not ru'ya itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relationship between looking (nazar) and seeing (ru'ya) is one of means to end, and therefore they are distinct.

Core Idea
The means is never the same as the goal.

This passage presents a simple yet powerful proof from everyday language. When we say 'I kept looking until I saw,' we instinctively distinguish between the ongoing process of looking and the final moment of seeing. Imam Razi uses this linguistic observation to argue that nazar and ru'ya are different realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. Notice the structure 'ما زلت أنظر حتى رأيته' ('I kept looking until I saw it'). This temporal progression is key to the argument.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify 'نظر' (nazar) and 'رؤية' (ru'ya) in the sentence. Also note 'غاية' (ghāya) meaning 'goal' or 'end.'
These three terms form the logical core of the proof.
3
Understand the logical principle
The proof relies on the principle that the goal (ghāya) of something is different from that thing itself. Understand why this is a self-evident truth: the path to a destination is not the destination.
This principle is not argued for; it is presented as obvious.
4
Apply the principle to nazar and ru'ya
Apply the principle to the example. If nazar is the means (the looking) and ru'ya is the goal (the seeing), then they must be different. Recognize that the proof is valid only if ru'ya is indeed the goal of nazar.
Imam Razi assumes this relationship is uncontroversial.
5
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Consider the mistaken belief that the phrase implies identity. Note that the grammar of 'until' (حتى) indicates a transition from one state to another, not identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : يقال : ما زلت أنظر إلى الهلال حتى رأيته، ولا شك أن غاية الشيء غير ذلك الشيء.
The sixth proof: It is said: I kept looking at the crescent until I saw it, and there is no doubt that the goal (ghāya) of a thing is other than that thing.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof from temporal progression. The statement 'I kept looking until I saw' treats nazar as the continuous process and ru'ya as the endpoint. Since a goal (ghāya) is distinct from the path leading to it, nazar and ru'ya must be different.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
غاية (ghāya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'I kept looking until I saw it' might be interpreted as equating looking with seeing or implying they occur simultaneously.
Correction: The grammar shows a progression: the looking precedes the seeing, and the word 'until' indicates a transition from one state to another, not identity. The goal is separate from the means.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the sixth proof rely on?
A) That a goal is identical to the means.
B) That a goal is different from the means.
C) That looking and seeing occur simultaneously.
D) That seeing is a type of looking.

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof uses the self-evident truth that the goal of a process is distinct from the process itself to show that nazar (looking) and ru'ya (seeing) are different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a religious practice you do regularly. Is it possible that you have made the act itself (like reading Qur'an without reflection) your goal, rather than using it as a means to see Allah's guidance? How would your practice change if you truly saw it as a means to a distinct end? Identify one change you will make this week.
?
In your journey of faith, are you often 'looking' at the signs of Allah without ever 'seeing' His presence in your life? What is one barrier that keeps your looking from becoming seeing? What concrete step can you take today to move from nazar to ru'ya?

Poetic Proof for Distinction of Looking and Seeing
Imam Razi cites a poetic line showing that looking precedes seeing, proving they are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a poetic verse can serve as a rational proof in Kalam, specifically demonstrating the distinction between looking and seeing.

Core Idea
The particle 'fa' proves that looking and seeing are two different acts.

Imam Razi presents a seventh proof using a line of poetry. The poet says 'I looked... then I saw,' using the Arabic letter 'fa' to indicate immediate succession. This grammatical feature implies that looking and seeing are not the same act, because if they were identical, the sequence would be meaningless. This lesson will unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse and its English translation carefully. Notice the sequence of actions: looking before seeing.
The verse is a complete unit of evidence.
2
Identify the key particle
Find the connecting particle between 'nazartu' and 'abṣartu'. It is the letter 'fa', which in Arabic grammar indicates immediate succession (ta'qīb).
This particle is the crux of the proof.
3
Understand the logical inference
Why does 'fa' imply that looking and seeing are distinct? If they were the same act, you cannot have one after the other. The sequence only makes sense if they are different.
Imam Razi concludes that ibsār is other than nazar.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how Kalam uses linguistic analysis as a rational proof. Poetry is a source of natural Arabic usage, not mere decoration.
This shows the interdisciplinary nature of classical theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قول الشاعر :
نظرت إليها من وراء خصاص فأبصرت وجهًا داعيًا لمعاصي رتب الإبصار على النظر بفاء التعقيب وهذا يدل على أن الإبصار غير النظر.
The seventh proof: The saying of the poet: 'I looked at her from behind a screen, / Then I saw a face inviting to sins.' He ordered seeing (ibsār) after looking (nazar) with the letter fā' of succession (ta'qīb), and this indicates that seeing (ibsār) is other than looking (nazar).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a literary proof. The poet uses 'nazartu' then 'fa-abṣartu' — the 'fa' indicates immediate succession (ta'qīb). If nazar and ibsār were the same, such sequential ordering would be meaningless. Therefore, they must be distinct acts.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
إبصار (ibsār)
فاء التعقيب (fā' al-ta'qīb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Poetry cannot be used as evidence in theological arguments.
Correction: Classical scholars used poetry as linguistic evidence because it reflects the natural usage of the Arabic language, and thus can serve as a proof for conceptual distinctions.

Checkpoint
What grammatical particle in the poetic verse proves that looking and seeing are different?
A) The preposition 'ila'.
B) The letter 'fa' indicating succession.
C) The use of 'waw' for conjunction.
D) The dual form.

Key Takeaway
The use of 'fa' in the poetic verse demonstrates that looking and seeing are distinct acts, proving that language itself can serve as a rational proof in Kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at something, do you ever pause to consider the difference between just glancing and truly seeing? How does this verse challenge you to be more intentional about what you allow your eyes to dwell on?
?
The poet saw a face that invited to sins. Reflect: In your daily life, when you see something that tempts you, is it preceded by a deliberate look? What responsibility do you have for the 'looking' that leads to 'seeing'?
?
Imam Razi uses a line of poetry to prove a theological point. Are you attentive to the language you use every day as a reflection of deeper truths? How can you train yourself to notice such subtleties in the Qur'an and hadith?

Eighth Proof: Imperative Construction
Razi presents a linguistic proof that nazar and ru'ya are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic imperative construction 'look until you see' proves that nazar (looking) and ru'ya (seeing) are distinct concepts in kalam.

Core Idea
A tautological command proves linguistic distinction.

Imam Razi uses a simple linguistic observation to strike down the claim that nazar and ru'ya are the same. If they were identical, saying 'look until you see' would be as absurd as saying 'see until you see.' This lesson walks through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the command structure: 'unzur... ḥattā tarā'.
The phrase 'ḥattā tarā' indicates purpose or result.
2
Identify the argument
Imam Razi argues that if nazar and ru'ya were the same, the sentence would reduce to 'see until you see', which is nonsensical. Therefore they must be different.
3
Understand the linguistic principle
The key is that 'ḥattā' marks a goal distinct from the initial action. This proves nazar is a means to an end (ru'ya), not the end itself.
This principle is rooted in Arabic usage and common sense.
4
Reflect on the implication
If nazar and ru'ya are different, then the earlier proofs that rely on their equivalence are invalid. This strengthens the position that Allah is visible (ru'ya) but not through a process of looking (nazar).
This connects to the broader debate about seeing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : يقال : انظر إلى زيد حتى ترى وجهه ولو كان النظر عبارة عن الرؤية والإبصار لنزل هذا الكلام منزلة قوله : أبصر زيدا حتى تبصره.
The eighth proof: It is said: Look (unzur) at Zaid until you see (tarā) his face. And if looking were the same as seeing and vision, this speech would be equivalent to saying: See (abṣir) Zaid until you see him (tubṣirahu).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof from linguistic impossibility. The command 'unzur ilā Zaydin ḥattā tarā wajhahu' uses nazar as a means leading to ru'ya. If nazar and ru'ya were identical, the sentence would be tautological: 'see Zaid until you see him.' Since it is not, they must be distinct.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
إبصار (ibsār)
أمر (amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'look until you see' is simply a normal expression and doesn't prove anything about the meaning of the words.
Correction: The argument is based on the logical absurdity of replacing 'look' with 'see' while preserving the structure. If the words were synonyms, the sentence would become redundant, which shows they are not synonyms in this context.

Checkpoint
Why does the command 'Look at Zaid until you see his face' prove that nazar and ru'ya are different?
A) Because it is an imperative.
B) Because if they were the same, the command would be redundant.
C) Because it uses the word 'face'.
D) Because Zaid is a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
The imperative construction 'unzur... ḥattā tarā' proves that nazar and ru'ya are not synonymous, because if they were, the command would be logically redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that conflating two distinct acts (looking and seeing) leads to an absurd command. Where in your own life have you confused the means with the end, reducing a journey of spiritual growth to a mere checklist? What concrete step will you take today to realign your actions with their intended purpose?
?
This proof relies on the precision of language. When you recite or hear commands of Allah (like 'look until you see'), do you pause to reflect on the distinction between the command and its goal? How can you train yourself to not just 'look' but to truly 'see' the deeper meanings in the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The tautology 'see until you see' is meaningless. Are there any acts of worship or habits you engage in that have become tautological—done just for the sake of doing, without progressing toward Allah? What will you change to break that cycle?

The Preposition 'ilā' as Proof of Directional Nazar
Imam Razi uses the preposition 'ilā' to prove that nazar involves directional movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic preposition 'ilā' in the phrase 'nazartu ilā' reveals that nazar (looking) involves directional physical movement toward the object, distinguishing it from ru'ya (seeing).

Core Idea
The preposition 'ilā' proves that nazar is directed movement, not passive sight.

In this lesson, we examine Imam Razi's ninth proof for the distinction between nazar and ru'ya. By analyzing the Arabic phrase 'nazartu ilā Zaid', he argues that the preposition 'ilā' implies a movement terminating at the object. This linguistic evidence shows that nazar involves a physical orientation, setting it apart from direct seeing (ru'ya). We'll unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the feel of Imam Razi's argument. Then read the English translation. Notice the key terms and the flow.
Pay attention to the use of the preposition 'ilā' and its explanation.
2
Analyze the role of the preposition 'ilā'
Understand how 'ilā' functions in Arabic to indicate the endpoint of a motion. Compare with analogous verbs like 'ashartu ilā' (I pointed at) and 'awma'tu ilā' (I gestured at). This shows that nazar is an action that involves direction and termination.
3
Contrast with 'ra'aytu' (I saw)
Recognize that 'ra'aytu' takes a direct object without a preposition, indicating a direct act of seeing without implied motion. This contrast highlights the distinctive nature of nazar as a physical movement.
This distinction is critical for the theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أن قوله : نظرت إلى زيد يفيد أن نظره انتهى إليه؛ لأن حرف " إلى " لانتهاء الغاية، فجرى ذلك مجرى قوله : أشرت إليه وأومأت إليه وذلك يفيد الحركة إلى الشيء على وجه ينتهي إليه، وهذا إنما يصح لو كان النظر عبارة عن تحريك الحدقة إلى مقابلة المرئي
The ninth proof: That his saying: I looked (nazartu) at (ilā) Zaid indicates that his looking terminated at him; because the letter 'ilā' is for the ending of a goal. So this is analogous to his saying: I pointed at him and I gestured at him, and that indicates movement toward a thing in a way that terminates at it. And this is only valid if looking is the movement of the eye-pupil to face the seen object.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the use of the preposition 'ilā' with nazar. In Arabic, 'nazartu ilā Zaid' means 'I directed my looking toward Zaid,' with 'ilā' indicating the endpoint. This implies a directional movement of the eye. In contrast, 'ra'aytu Zaidan' (I saw Zaid) uses direct object without such movement. Therefore, nazar involves physical direction, unlike ru'ya.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
إلى (ilā)
رؤية (ru'ya)
تحريك الحدقة (taḥrīk al-ḥadaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the preposition 'ilā' is merely optional or incidental to the meaning of nazar.
Correction: The preposition 'ilā' is essential; it indicates direction and termination, implying physical movement toward the object.

Checkpoint
What does the preposition 'ilā' in 'nazartu ilā Zaid' indicate according to Imam Razi?
A) That nazar is a direct act like seeing without movement
B) That nazar involves movement toward and termination at the object
C) That nazar is just a thought process
D) That nazar is equivalent to ru'ya

Key Takeaway
The linguistic structure of 'nazartu ilā' proves nazar involves physical orientation, not passive seeing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I looked at...' in your own language, do you think of that looking as a deliberate act of directing your eyes, or as passive reception of images? How does the linguistic proof of 'ilā' challenge you to be more intentional in your looking—especially when it comes to what you choose to gaze upon in daily life?
?
Imam Razi compares nazar to pointing and gesturing—physical actions we control. In what areas of your life do you 'point your eyes' with purpose (e.g., studying, helping), and where do you let your sight wander without direction? What would change if you treated every glance as a directed act of nazar that you are accountable for?

Tenth Proof: How Nazar Differs from Ru'ya
Imam Razi presents a proof from linguistic usage that nazar admits classification whereas ru'ya does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar (looking) can be qualified into different types (e.g., angry, satisfied) while ru'ya (seeing) is uniform, proving they are distinct cognitive acts.

Core Idea
Nazar varies; ru'ya does not.

This proof draws on everyday language: we describe looks as angry, satisfied, or bewildered, but seeing itself is the same for all. The source contrasts nazar and ru'ya using poetry and rational argument. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the poetic examples that illustrate different types of nazar.
The passage is incomplete at the end but sufficient for the proof.
2
Identify the key contrast
Notice that nazar is divided into categories (angry, satisfied, bewildered) while ru'ya is said to be one and cannot vary. This is the core argument.
Also note the explanation that descriptions like 'angry look' refer to the physical state of the eye, not the act of seeing itself.
3
Consider the objection
The passage briefly addresses a counterargument: 'he sees me with an eye of satisfaction.' Understand why Razi dismisses this as metaphorical, not literal.
This reinforces the distinction between the physical act (nazar) and the cognitive act (ru'ya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : أنهم يقسمون النظر إلى أقسام كثيرة، فيقولون : نظر فلان إلى نظر راض، ونظر غضبان ونظر متحيز، قال النابغة : نظرت إليك لحاجة لم تقضها نظر المريض إلى وجوه العود
وقال آخر :
ظـــر الـ يوس إلى شفار الجازر
ومعلوم أن رؤية هؤلاء واحدة لأنهم يرون الشيء على ما هو عليه، ومن الممتنع وقوع التفاوت في نفس الرؤية، بل المراد من قوله : نظر إليَّ نظر غضبان وصف عينه بما تكون عليه عين الغضبان من الانحراف والازورار، ولا يعارض هذا بقولهم: فلان يراني بعين الرضى وبعين الغضب وبعين الذل ؛ لأن في هذا الكلام
The tenth proof: That they divide looking (nazar) into many categories, saying: So-and-so looked with a satisfied look, an angry look, and a bewildered look. An-Nabigha said: 'I looked at you for a need not fulfilled, the look of a sick person at the faces of the visitors.' And another said: 'The ox continued to look at the blades of the butcher.' And it is known that the seeing (ru'ya) of these people is one, because they see the thing as it is, and it is impossible for variation to occur in ru'ya itself. Rather, the meaning of his saying 'he looked at me with an angry look' is a description of his eye with what the eye of an angry person is upon of deviation and squinting. And this is not contradicted by their saying: 'So-and-so sees me with an eye of satisfaction, an eye of anger, and an eye of humility,' because in this speech...
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the fact that nazar can be qualified into different types (satisfied, angry, bewildered), whereas ru'ya is uniform—everyone sees the same thing as it is. Therefore, nazar involves additional physical attributes like eye movement, squinting, etc., while ru'ya is a pure cognitive act. The poetic examples illustrate different kinds of looks. The objection that 'so-and-so sees me with an eye of satisfaction' is dismissed as metaphorical, not literal.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
أقسام النظر (aqsām al-nazar)
عين (ʿayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'seeing with an eye of satisfaction' shows that ru'ya itself varies.
Correction: Razi explains that such phrases are metaphorical; actual ru'ya is uniform.

Checkpoint
Why does the classification of nazar into types (angry, satisfied) prove it is different from ru'ya?
A) Because ru'ya can also be classified.
B) Because ru'ya is uniform and cannot vary.
C) Because nazar is always the same.
D) Because poetry is not evidence.

Key Takeaway
Nazar involves physical and emotional qualifiers, while ru'ya is a uniform cognitive act, establishing their distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you look at it with a satisfied look or an angry look? How does that affect your understanding of its message, knowing that your ru'ya of the truth should be uniform?
?
Think about a time you looked at someone with an angry look. Did your ru'ya of them as a person change? According to this lesson, ru'ya is uniform, so your perception of them remains the same, but your nazar is colored. How does this affect your responsibility to see others as they truly are?
?
Razi says ru'ya is uniform and cannot vary. Yet we often claim to 'see' things differently. How can you distinguish between your nazar (your colored look) and your ru'ya (your actual perception) in your daily interactions with family? Commit to one change today based on this.

Proof 15: Seeking Reward for Looking
Razi's fifteenth proof using poetry to distinguish nazar from ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifteenth poetic proof demonstrates that nazar (looking) is a deliberate action distinct from ru'ya (seeing).

Core Idea
If looking were seeing, asking reward for it would be selfish nonsense.

In the fifteenth proof, Imam al-Razi cites a verse from a poet longing for his beloved. Abu Ali al-Farisi's commentary reveals a subtle linguistic point: the poet seeks a reward for his act of looking, which would be absurd if looking were merely passive vision. This sets the stage for understanding nazar as intentional and effortful.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse and its English translation. Notice the poet's request for a reward for his looking.
The key phrase is 'هل تجزي بكائي بمثله'—asking for reward.
2
Understand al-Farisi's logical inference
Al-Farisi argues that if nazar were ru'ya, the poet would be asking for reward for his own pleasure, which no rational person would do. Grasp why this is irrational.
The irrationality lies in seeking reward for one's own benefit.
3
Apply the distinction to the proof's conclusion
Conclude that nazar must involve effort and merit reward, unlike passive ru'ya. This is one of three similar proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشرة : ما أنشده أبو علي الفارسي في كتاب الحجة : فيا مي هل تجزي بكائي بمثله مرارًا وأنفاسي عليك الزوافر وإني متى أشرف من الجنب الذي به أنت من بين الجوانب ناظر قال أبو علي الفارسي يطلب منها الجزاء على كونه ناظرا إليها، ولو كان النظر عبارة عن الرؤية لها لكان قد طلب الجزاء على منفعته ولذاته وحصول غرضه، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
الحجة السادسة عشرة : أنشد بعضهم : إذا نظر الواشون صدت وأعرضت وإن غفلوا قلت ألست على العهد جعل قوله : وإن غفلوا في مقابلة قوله : نظروا، وإنما يغفل الإنسان عن أمر يفعله، ويشتغل بتحصيله وما ذلك إلا تقليب الحدقة.
الحجة السابعة عشرة: أنشد بعضهم : ونظرة ذي شجن وامق إذا ما الركائب جاوزن ميلا أثبت النظر بعد مجاوزة الركائب ميلاً، ومعلوم أن الرؤية غير حاصلة في هذا الوقت، ولما أثبت النظر حال عدم الرؤية ثبت أن النظر غير الرؤية.
The fifteenth proof: What Abu Ali al-Farisi recited in the book al-Hujja: 'O May, will you reward my weeping with the like of it repeatedly? My breaths upon you are abundant. And whenever I look out from the side from which you are among the sides, looking.' Abu Ali al-Farisi said: He seeks reward from her for his looking at her. If nazar (looking) were ru'ya (seeing), then he would have sought reward for his benefit, his pleasures, and the attainment of his purpose, and no rational person would say that. The sixteenth proof: Someone recited: 'When the gossips look, she turns away and avoids, and when they are heedless, I say: Are you not still upon the covenant?' He made his saying 'and when they are heedless' in contrast to his saying 'they looked'. A person is only heedless of an action that he performs and engages in obtaining, and that is nothing but the turning of the eyeball. The seventeenth proof: Someone recited: 'And a look of a sorrowful, loving person when the riding beasts pass a mile.' He affirmed nazar after the riding beasts had passed a mile, and it is known that ru'ya does not occur at that time. Since he affirmed nazar in the absence of ru'ya, it is established that nazar is not ru'ya.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents three consecutive poetic proofs (fifteenth, sixteenth, seventeenth) that the word nazar (looking) is distinct from ru'ya (seeing). In the fifteenth proof, Razi cites a verse where a poet asks for a reward for looking at his beloved. Abu Ali al-Farisi comments that if nazar were ru'ya, the poet would be asking for reward for his own pleasure and benefit, which is irrational—so nazar must involve effort and action, not passive vision. The sixteenth proof uses a verse about gossips: when they look (nazar) the woman avoids, but when they are heedless (ghafala), the lover speaks. Razi notes that heedlessness applies to deliberate actions like turning the eye (taqlib al-hadaqa), not to involuntary seeing. Thus nazar is an act, unlike ru'ya. The seventeenth proof presents a verse where a person looks (nazara) after the riding beasts have passed a mile, a time when actual vision is impossible. Since nazar is affirmed without ru'ya, they are distinct. Each proof uses everyday language to highlight the difference: nazar is intentional and effortful, ru'ya is involuntary and immediate.

Key Terms
نظر
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that asking for reward for looking is the same as asking for reward for seeing, and therefore acceptable.
Correction: The proof shows that if looking were seeing, the poet would be seeking reward for his own pleasure, which is irrational. Hence they differ.

Checkpoint
According to the fifteenth proof, why does Abu Ali al-Farisi argue that nazar cannot be ru'ya?
A) Because the poet asks for reward for looking, which would be absurd if looking were seeing.
B) Because the poet uses nazar in a metaphorical sense.
C) Because the poet describes turning the eyes.
D) Because the poet mentions crying.

Key Takeaway
The fifteenth proof uses the logic of seeking reward to show that nazar is an intentional act, not passive vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the poet seeks reward for his 'looking' (nazar) at his beloved. Reflect: In your acts of worship—such as reading the Qur'an or praying—do you ever find yourself looking (nazar) with effort and intention, expecting a reward from Allah? Or do you sometimes treat them as passive 'seeing' (ru'ya), merely going through motions? What will you do this week to ensure your devotional acts are true nazar—deliberate and seeking His pleasure, not just your own benefit?
?
The argument shows that seeking reward for one's own benefit is irrational. Examine your heart: When you perform a good deed, do you secretly hope for personal reward—praise, status, or even a guaranteed place in Paradise? How does this lesson's distinction between nazar and ru'ya challenge you to shift from 'what do I get?' to 'what does my Lord deserve?' Commit to one specific act today where you will consciously make it a pure nazar for Allah alone, without expecting anything in return.

Proof 16: Heedlessness and the Eye's Movement
Razi's sixteenth proof using the concept of heedlessness to distinguish nazar from ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixteenth poetic proof links nazar to deliberate physical action (turning the eyeball) in contrast to involuntary ru'ya.

Core Idea
Heedlessness applies to intentional acts like turning the eye, not to passive seeing.

The sixteenth proof presents a verse about a beloved who turns away when gossips 'look' (nazar) but responds when they are 'heedless'. Razi's commentary highlights that heedlessness (ghafla) can only apply to deliberate actions, such as the physical turning of the eyeball (taqlib al-hadaqa). This further reinforces that nazar is an act, not a passive state like ru'ya.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its structure
Read the Arabic verse and translation. Notice the contrast between 'when the gossips look' and 'when they are heedless'.
The opposite of heedlessness is attention, which implies deliberate action.
2
Analyze the logic of heedlessness
Razi notes that heedlessness is only possible for actions one performs, not for passive occurrents. Understand why this links nazar to intentional movement.
You cannot be heedless of something that happens to you automatically.
3
Connect to the physical act of looking
Razi identifies the action as 'turning the eyeball'. This confirms nazar as a deliberate bodily movement.
This is the key distinction from ru'ya, which is involuntary.

Proof 17: Looking Without Seeing
Razi's seventeenth proof showing nazar can occur without ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventeenth proof directly shows that nazar can occur without ru'ya, proving they are distinct.

Core Idea
Looking is possible when seeing is impossible, so they cannot be the same.

The final proof in this section presents a verse where a lover looks (nazara) after the riding beasts have passed a mile—a moment when actual vision is impossible. Razi uses this to demonstrate that nazar does not require ru'ya, establishing them as separate concepts. This is the most direct argument: if nazar were ru'ya, they would always coincide, but here they do not.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and understand the scenario
Read the Arabic verse and translation. The poet says that a sorrowful lover looks after the camels have passed a mile, i.e., when they are out of sight.
The key phrase is 'إذا ما الركائب جاوزن ميلا'—when the riding beasts pass a mile.
2
Recognize the logical implication
Razi points out that ru'ya does not occur at that distance, yet nazar is affirmed. Therefore they cannot be identical.
This is a clear case of nazar without ru'ya.
3
Understand the broader significance
This proof shows that nazar refers to the intentional direction of gaze, regardless of whether vision actually occurs.
This is a decisive linguistic proof.

Checkpoint

A) Because the poet uses different words for each.
B) Because nazar occurs when ru'ya is impossible.
C) Because the poet seeks a reward.
D) Because the poet mentions heedlessness.

Key Takeaway
The seventeenth proof directly shows that nazar can exist without ru'ya, proving they are not synonymous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you 'look' at the Qur'an during recitation without truly 'seeing' its meaning? What single change will you make today to shift from mere nazar to real ru'ya?
?
You claim to 'look' for the truth, but do you actually see it when it confronts you? What is one area of your life where you have been content with nazar and avoided the ru'ya that would demand change?

Quranic Proof: 'Nazar Ila' Means Waiting
Razi uses Quranic verse to show 'nazar ila' means waiting, not seeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quranic grammar and logical reasoning to prove that the phrase 'nazar ila' can mean waiting rather than seeing.

Core Idea
'Nazar ila' can mean waiting—Quranic fronting shows exclusivity, but believers see many things on that Day.

Imam Razi continues his series of proofs on the meaning of nazar. In this eighteenth proof, he turns to the Quranic verse 'ila rabbiha nazirah' and argues that the grammatical structure of fronting the object indicates exclusivity—they look only to their Lord. Yet reason tells us that on that Day, believers will see many other things. Therefore, nazar cannot mean physical seeing here; it must mean waiting. This lesson unpacks this elegant argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice how Razi builds his case step by step.
2
Understand the grammar
Pay attention to the grammatical point about fronting the object (تقديم المفعول) which indicates exclusivity (حصر). This is key to the argument.
3
Follow the logic
Trace the logical flow: exclusivity implies they look only to Allah, but they do see other things, so nazar cannot be seeing—so it must mean waiting.
Think through why the reinterpretation resolves the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة عشرة ومن ههنا نذكر الوجوه الدالة على أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى قد يجيء بمعنى الانتظار، لا بمعنى الرؤية . فنقول : قوله تعالى : إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ) [ القِيَامَة : ٢٣] معناه أنها تنظر إلى الرب لا إلى غيره، وذلك لأن تقديم المفعول يفيد الحصر، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَا إِلى الله تصيرُ الأُمُورُ ﴾ [الشورى: ٥٣ و عَلَيْهِ تَوَكَّلْتُ وَإِلَيْهِ أنيبُ ﴾ [هود : ۸۸] ، ووَأَنَّ إِلَى رَبِّكَ المنتهى [النجم: ٤٢] وإنَّ إلى رَبِّكَ الرجى العلق : ۸] ولا إلَى رَبِّكَ يَوْمَئِذٍ الْمَسَاقُ إلى القِيَامَة : ٣٠] إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : مقتضى الآية أنهم لا ينظرون إلى غير الله تعالى، ولا شك أنهم يرون غير الله تعالى، وذلك لأنهم يرون الجنة والنار ومواقف القيامة، وذلك لأن المؤمنين نظارة في ذلك اليوم ؛ لأنهم هم الآمنون لا خوف عليهم ولا هم يحزنون، ولما دلت الآية على أنهم لا ينظرون إلى غير الله تعالى ودل العقل عليهم ولا يرون غير الله علمنا أن النظر غير الرؤية، أما إذا حملنا هذا اللفظ على الانتظار صح هذا الحصر، وذلك لأنهم لا ينتظرون إلا رحمة الله ولا يتوقعونها إلا منه.
The eighteenth proof: And from here we mention the proofs indicating that nazar combined with the letter ila may come in the sense of waiting (intizar), not in the sense of ru'ya (seeing). So we say: The saying of the Exalted: 'To their Lord they are looking' (nazirah) [Al-Qiyamah 75:23] means that they look to the Lord and not to other than Him, because fronting the object indicates exclusivity (hasr), as in His saying: 'Indeed, to Allah all matters return' [Ash-Shura 42:53], and 'Upon Him I rely and to Him I turn' [Hud 11:88], and 'That to your Lord is the finality' [An-Najm 53:42], and 'Indeed, to your Lord is the return' [Al-Alaq 96:8], and 'To your Lord on that Day is the procession' [Al-Qiyamah 75:30]. When this is established, we say: The implication of the verse is that they do not look (yanzurun) to other than Allah. And there is no doubt that they see (yarun) other than Allah, because they see Paradise and Hell and the stations of the Resurrection, and because the believers are spectators on that day, as they are secure, having no fear nor grief. Since the verse indicates that they do not look to other than Allah, and reason indicates that they do see other than Allah, we know that nazar is not ru'ya. However, if we interpret this word as waiting (intizar), this exclusivity is correct, because they wait for nothing except the mercy of Allah and they expect it only from Him.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a logical and exegetical proof (the eighteenth proof) that when nazar is coupled with the preposition ila, it can mean 'waiting' rather than 'seeing'. Razi first cites a general principle: from this point onward, he will present proofs that 'nazar ila' sometimes means intizar (expectation). He then focuses on the Quranic verse 'ila rabbiha nazirah' (To their Lord they are looking). He argues that the fronting of the object (ila rabbiha) before the verb (nazirah) indicates exclusivity (hasr) — they look only to their Lord, not to anything else. He supports this with other Quranic examples where fronting implies exclusivity. Then he states the logical implication: the verse means that on that Day, the believers do not look (nazar) to anything other than Allah. Yet it is undeniable that they will see (ru'ya) other things, such as Paradise, Hell, and the events of the Day. Therefore, nazar cannot mean ru'ya here. If, however, nazar is interpreted as 'waiting', the exclusivity makes perfect sense: they wait only for Allah's mercy and expect it only from Him. Thus, this verse provides strong evidence that 'nazar ila' can mean waiting.

إلى
انتظار
رؤية
حصر
