Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nazar ila' always means physical seeing in the Quran.
Correction: This proof shows it can mean waiting (intizar) in this context.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that 'nazar ila' in the verse means waiting rather than seeing?
A) Because the fronted object implies exclusivity, but believers will see other things on that Day.
B) Because the verse uses a different grammatical structure.
C) Because the word 'nazirah' is feminine.
D) Because the verse refers to the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
When 'nazar ila' is used in the Quran with fronting of the object, it can indicate expecting or waiting, not necessarily seeing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where do you truly place your hope and expectation? If 'nazar ila' means waiting only on Allah, examine your heart: do you rely on any created thing or person as if it were your ultimate source? Will you repent of that today and redirect your expectation solely to Him?
?
On the Day of Judgment, you will see many overwhelming sights (Hell, Paradise, angels). Despite all of that, the verse says believers are 'looking to their Lord' in expectation. What does that tell you about where your heart should be focused even amid worldly distractions right now? Is there any situation where you are more preoccupied with what you see than with waiting on Allah?
?
Razi shows that fronting the object indicates exclusivity: believers expect mercy only from Allah. Is your hope for forgiveness, provision, and guidance truly exclusive to Allah? Or do you subconsciously rely on your own deeds or others' opinions? What would change if you lived today as if only Allah's mercy matters?

Poetic and Linguistic Proofs for Nazar Ila as Waiting
Razi presents four proofs from language and poetry for nazar ila as waiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses linguistic authorities and poetic verses to demonstrate that 'nazar ila' commonly denotes hopeful expectation, not visual perception.

Core Idea
Nazar ila in Arabic often means hopeful waiting, not visual seeing.

In this passage, Razi marshals four additional proofs from language, poetry, and common usage to reinforce his interpretation of 'nazar ila' as waiting. These proofs range from the testimony of al-Khalil and Ibn Abbas to verses from the battle of Badr and the poetry of al-Kumayt, all demonstrating that the phrase is used for expectation even when physical sight is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic authorities
Read the nineteenth proof where al-Khalil and Ibn Abbas confirm that 'nazar ila' can mean waiting. Notice the Persian parallel and the blind person's usage.
The blind person's speech is key: even without sight, one says 'my look is to Allah' meaning hope.
2
Analyze the Badr poem and its objection
Read the twentieth proof and the objection about the verse possibly referring to Musaylima. Understand Razi's response that both readings support the same meaning of waiting.
3
Consider the analogy with thirst
Read the twenty-first proof by al-Kumayt. Compare the thirsty looking to a rain cloud—they are waiting for rain, not just seeing.
4
Reflect on the king and sea example
Read the twenty-second proof where a person looks to a king across a sea. Since sight is impossible, the word must mean waiting.
This proof highlights that 'nazar ila' is used for anticipation when vision is obstructed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشرة: حكي عن الخليل أنه قال: نظرت إلى فلان بمعنى انتظرته، وعن ابن عباس أنه قال : تقول العرب : إنما أنظر إلى الله تعالى ثم إلى فلان، وهذا الاستعمال أيضًا حاصل في الفارسية، فإنهم يقولون فيمن يطمع في أمر يتوقعه فلان را جشم برفلان کاراست و نظر وبرفلان کاراست وفلان كاري رامي نكرة فثبت أن هذا الاستعمال متعارف مشهور في العربية وفي الفارسية، حتى إن الأعمى قد يذكر هذا اللفظ فيقول : عيني شاخصة إليك ونظري إلى الله تعالى، ثم إليك.
الحجة العشرون: قول الشاعر : وجوه ناظرات يوم بدر إلى الرحمن تنتظر الخلاصا أثبت النظر المقرون بحرف "إلى " لا بمعنى الرؤية، بل بمعنى الانتظار. لا يقال : إن بعض الرواة روى هذا البيت على وجه به آخر : وجوه ناظرات يوم بكر وزعم أن مراد الشاعر يوم اليمامة، وسمي يوم بكر لأن القتال وقع بين عسكر أبي بكر له وبين مسيلمة الكذاب والمراد من الرحمن في البيت مسيلمة الكذاب فإنهم كانوا يسمونه رحمن اليمامة وكانوا ينظرون إلى وجهه ويطمعون فيه أن يخلصهم من ذلك البلاء ؛ لأنا نقول : لا منافاة بين الروايتين فنقبلهما.
الحجة الحادية والعشرون: قول الكميت : وسعت ينظرون إلى بلال كما نظر الظماء إلى الغمام ومعلوم أن الظماء ينتظرون حياء الغمام، فعلمنا أن نظر الشعث على بلال هو بمعنى الانتظار، إلا أنه شبه نظرهم إلى حيا الغمام.
الحجة الثانية والعشرون : قال آخر : وإذا نظرت إليك من ملك والبحر دونك زدتني نعما وصف نفسه بكونه ناظرا إلى الملك حال ما كان البحر حائلا بينهما، والذي يحتمل الرؤية، فعلمنا أن المراد هو الانتظار.
The nineteenth proof: It is narrated from al-Khalil that he said: 'I looked to so-and-so' meaning 'I waited for him'. And from Ibn Abbas that he said: The Arabs say: 'I only look to Allah and then to so-and-so'. This usage is also found in Persian, for they say about one who hopes for something he expects: 'So-and-so's eye is on so-and-so's work, and his look is on so-and-so's work, and so-and-so is watching a task'? So it is established that this usage is common and well-known in Arabic and Persian, such that even a blind person may use this expression and say: 'My eye is fixed on you, and my look (nazari) is to Allah, then to you.' The twentieth proof: The poet's saying: 'Faces looking (nazirat) on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful, waiting for deliverance.' He affirmed nazar combined with the letter ila not in the sense of ru'ya, but in the sense of intizar. It cannot be said: Some narrators transmitted this verse in another way: 'Faces looking on the day of Bakr', and claimed that the poet meant the day of Yamama, and it was called the day of Bakr because the battle occurred between the army of Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) and Musaylima the liar, and the intended meaning of 'the Most Merciful' in the verse is Musaylima the liar, for they used to call him 'Rahman of Yamama', and they were looking at his face and hoping that he would save them from that calamity. For we say: There is no conflict between the two narrations, so we accept both. The twenty-first proof: The saying of al-Kumayt: 'And they heard looking to Bilal as the thirsty look to the rain cloud.' It is known that the thirsty wait for the downpour of the rain cloud, so we know that the look of the disheveled at Bilal is in the sense of waiting, except that he likened their look to the rain cloud. The twenty-second proof: Another said: 'And when I look to you from a king, with the sea between us, you increased me in bounty.' He described himself as looking (naziran) to the king while the sea was a barrier between them, which precludes ru'ya (seeing). So we know that the intended meaning is intizar (waiting).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage contains four additional proofs (nineteenth through twenty-second) reinforcing that 'nazar ila' can mean waiting (intizar). The nineteenth proof cites linguistic authorities: al-Khalil ibn Ahmad and Ibn Abbas both confirm that Arabic speakers use 'nazar ila' to mean 'wait for'. This usage is also widespread in Persian, and even a blind person can say 'my look is to Allah' meaning hopeful expectation. The twentieth proof is a verse from a poem about the battle of Badr, where 'faces looking to the Most Merciful, waiting for deliverance' clearly uses 'nazar ila' in the sense of waiting. An objection is raised: some narrate the verse as referring to the battle of Yamama and 'the Most Merciful' as Musaylima the liar. Razi answers that both narrations are acceptable and consistent with the meaning of waiting (they were looking to Musaylima hoping for rescue). The twenty-first proof quotes al-Kumayt's verse comparing people looking to Bilal to the thirsty looking to a rain cloud—they are waiting for the cloud's rain, not just seeing it. The twenty-second proof quotes a verse where a person looks to a king across a sea, which precludes actual vision, so the meaning must be waiting. Together, these proofs show that 'nazar ila' is commonly used in Arabic and Persian to express hopeful expectation, not physical sight.

إلى
انتظار
رؤية
شعر
خليل
ابن عباس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nazar ila' always implies visual seeing, especially when the object is Allah (anthropomorphism).
Correction: Razi shows that 'nazar ila' is used by Arabs to mean waiting or hopeful expectation, even when vision is impossible (e.g., blind person, across the sea).

Checkpoint
What evidence from the blind person's speech does Razi use in the nineteenth proof to show 'nazar ila' means waiting?

B) The blind person says 'My eye is fixed on you, and my look is to Allah, then to you'.
C) The blind person says 'I wait for you' without using the word nazar.
D) The blind person recites a poem about waiting.

Key Takeaway
Razi's four proofs establish that 'nazar ila' is conventionally used in Arabic to express hopeful waiting, not visual sight, reinforcing his interpretation of the disputed qur'anic verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The blind person says 'My look is to Allah'—do you have that same trust when you cannot see the outcome? What are you waiting for right now that requires you to rely solely on His promise?
?
Razi uses poetry to prove a point. Do you have patience (intizar) in your life that matches the intensity of the thirsty waiting for rain? What concrete step can you take today to turn your waiting into active hopeful reliance on Allah?

Proof 19: Linguistic Usage of 'Nazar Ila' as Expectation
Razi cites Arabic and Persian usage to prove 'nazar ila' means expectation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic phrase 'nazar ila' can mean expectation based on classical usage, Persian parallels, and even usage by a blind person.

Core Idea
'Nazar ila' in Arabic can mean waiting, not just seeing.

This lesson examines the first of three linguistic proofs. Razi cites the renowned philologist al-Khalil and the Companion Ibn Abbas to show that Arabs use 'nazar ila' to mean expectation. The usage is so ingrained that even a blind person employs it, confirming that no physical sight is intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage for proof 19 once, then read the translation. Notice the authorities cited.
The passage includes al-Khalil, Ibn Abbas, and a Persian analogy.
2
Identify the key evidence
Identify each piece of evidence: the statement of al-Khalil, the report from Ibn Abbas, the Persian usage, and the blind person's phrase.
How does the blind person's usage strengthen the argument?
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why Razi uses multiple examples. Think about how this proof supports the theological point that 'nazar ila' in the Quran can mean expectation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشرة: حكي عن الخليل أنه قال: نظرت إلى فلان بمعنى انتظرته، وعن ابن عباس أنه قال : تقول العرب : إنما أنظر إلى الله تعالى ثم إلى فلان، وهذا الاستعمال أيضًا حاصل في الفارسية، فإنهم يقولون فيمن يطمع في أمر يتوقعه فلان را جشم برفلان کاراست و نظر وبرفلان کاراست وفلان كاري رامي نكرة فثبت أن هذا الاستعمال متعارف مشهور في العربية وفي الفارسية، حتى إن الأعمى قد يذكر هذا اللفظ فيقول : عيني شاخصة إليك ونظري إلى الله تعالى، ثم إليك. الحجة العشرون: قول الشاعر : وجوه ناظرات يوم بدر إلى الرحمن تنتظر الخلاصا أثبت النظر المقرون بحرف "إلى " لا بمعنى الرؤية، بل بمعنى الانتظار. لا يقال : إن بعض الرواة روى هذا البيت على وجه به آخر : وجوه نـظـار يـوم بـك وزعم أن مراد الشاعر يوم اليمامة، وسمي يوم بكر لأن القتال وقع بين عسكر أبي بكر له وبين مسيلمة الكذاب والمراد من الرحمن في البيت مسيلمة الكذاب فإنهم كانوا يسمونه رحمن اليمامة وكانوا ينظرون إلى وجهه ويطمعون فيه أن يخلصهم من ذلك البلاء ؛ لأنا نقول : لا منافاة بين الروايتين فنقبلهما. الحجة الحادية والعشرون: قول الكميت : وسعت ينظرون إلى بلال كما نظر الظُّمَاء إلى الغمام ومعلوم أن الظماء ينتظرون حياء الغمام، فعلمنا أن نظر الشعث على بلال هو بمعنى الانتظار، إلا أنه شبه نظرهم إلى حيا الغمام.
The nineteenth proof: It is reported from al-Khalil that he said: 'I looked at so-and-so' meaning 'I waited for him', and from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: The Arabs say: 'Indeed I look to God then to so-and-so', and this usage is also found in Persian, for they say about one who hopes for something he expects... [unclear] so it is established that this usage is commonly known in Arabic and Persian, even a blind person may use this expression and say: 'My eye is fixed on you and my gaze is to God then to you.' The twentieth proof: The poet's saying: 'Faces looking on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful waiting for deliverance.' He affirmed 'nazar' with the preposition 'ila' not meaning vision but meaning waiting. It cannot be said: Some narrators transmitted this verse in another way: 'Faces looking on the day of Bakr' and claimed that the poet meant the day of al-Yamamah, and it was called the day of Bakr because the fighting occurred between the army of Abu Bakr and Musaylimah the Liar, and the intended meaning of 'al-Rahman' in the verse is Musaylimah the Liar, for they used to call him 'Rahman of al-Yamamah' and they were looking at his face and hoping that he would save them from that calamity. We say: There is no contradiction between the two narrations, so we accept both. The twenty-first proof: The saying of al-Kumayt: 'And they heard looking at Bilal as the thirsty look at the clouds.' It is known that the thirsty wait for the life-giving rain of the clouds, so we know that the looking of the people at Bilal is in the sense of waiting, except that he likened their looking to the life-giving rain of the clouds.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a series of three linguistic proofs (19th, 20th, 21st) presented by the author to demonstrate that the term 'nazar' (نظر) when followed by the preposition 'ila' can mean waiting/expectation (انتظار) rather than physical vision (رؤية). The 19th proof cites the philologist al-Khalil and Ibn Abbas, noting that Arabs say 'I look to God then to you' meaning they expect from God and then from the person. This usage is also found in Persian. Even a blind person uses this phrase, confirming it does not require vision. The 20th proof is a verse from a poet describing faces looking on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful expecting deliverance; the preposition confirms expectation. An objection suggests an alternative reading changes the meaning to a different battle, but the author dismisses it by accepting both versions. The 21st proof from the poet al-Kumayt compares people looking at Bilal to the thirsty looking at clouds, clearly indicating expectation of rain. Thus these proofs build the case that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation.

نظر (nazar)
إلى (ila)
انتظار (intizar)
الخليل (al-Khalil)
ابن عباس (Ibn 'Abbas)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'nazar ila' always implies physical sight.
Correction: This proof demonstrates that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation, as shown by usage of a blind person.

B) It means expectation without physical sight.
C) It refers to a type of inner vision.
D) It is a literal expression for the blind.

Key Takeaway
The usage of 'nazar ila' by a blind person conclusively shows it can mean expectation, not physical vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I look to God' in your prayers or daily life, do you genuinely expect from Him, or are the words empty? The blind man's gaze is real trust—where is your gaze fixed right now?

?
If 'nazar ila' can mean expectation, then your understanding of verses like 'Looking at their Lord' (75:23) might shift. Does this change what you hope for in the afterlife? Are you more concerned with seeing God or with His mercy and expectation?

Proof 20: The Poet's Verse on Badr and the Objection
Razi uses a poetic verse to prove 'nazar ila' means expectation and responds to a variant reading objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a poetic verse using 'nazar ila' confirms the meaning of expectation, and how Razi handles a variant reading objection.

Core Idea
Poetic evidence shows 'nazar ila' means waiting; a variant reading does not weaken the proof.

This lesson presents the second proof: a verse about faces looking to the Most Merciful on the day of Badr, clearly meaning waiting for deliverance. An objection arises that a variant reading changes the context, but Razi dismisses it by accepting both readings as non-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and objection
Read the Arabic for proof 20, including the objection and Razi's response. Then read the translation.
Note the poet's phrase 'nāẓirāt ilā l-raḥmān' and the alternative reading.
2
Analyze the argument
Identify how the verse demonstrates that 'nazar ila' means waiting: the faces are 'looking to the Most Merciful waiting for deliverance'.
The word 'tantaẓiru' (waiting) is paired with 'nāẓirāt'.
3
Understand the objection and response
Consider the objection that a variant reading changes the meaning. Then see how Razi accepts both readings, showing no conflict.
This shows that even with variant readings, the meaning of expectation remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشرة: حكي عن الخليل أنه قال: نظرت إلى فلان بمعنى انتظرته، وعن ابن عباس أنه قال : تقول العرب : إنما أنظر إلى الله تعالى ثم إلى فلان، وهذا الاستعمال أيضًا حاصل في الفارسية، فإنهم يقولون فيمن يطمع في أمر يتوقعه فلان را جشم برفلان کاراست و نظر وبرفلان کاراست وفلان كاري رامي نكرة فثبت أن هذا الاستعمال متعارف مشهور في العربية وفي الفارسية، حتى إن الأعمى قد يذكر هذا اللفظ فيقول : عيني شاخصة إليك ونظري إلى الله تعالى، ثم إليك. الحجة العشرون: قول الشاعر : وجوه ناظرات يوم بدر إلى الرحمن تنتظر الخلاصا أثبت النظر المقرون بحرف "إلى " لا بمعنى الرؤية، بل بمعنى الانتظار. لا يقال : إن بعض الرواة روى هذا البيت على وجه به آخر : وجوه نـظـار يـوم بـك وزعم أن مراد الشاعر يوم اليمامة، وسمي يوم بكر لأن القتال وقع بين عسكر أبي بكر له وبين مسيلمة الكذاب والمراد من الرحمن في البيت مسيلمة الكذاب فإنهم كانوا يسمونه رحمن اليمامة وكانوا ينظرون إلى وجهه ويطمعون فيه أن يخلصهم من ذلك البلاء ؛ لأنا نقول : لا منافاة بين الروايتين فنقبلهما. الحجة الحادية والعشرون: قول الكميت : وسعت ينظرون إلى بلال كما نظر الظُّمَاء إلى الغمام ومعلوم أن الظماء ينتظرون حياء الغمام، فعلمنا أن نظر الشعث على بلال هو بمعنى الانتظار، إلا أنه شبه نظرهم إلى حيا الغمام.
The nineteenth proof: It is reported from al-Khalil that he said: 'I looked at so-and-so' meaning 'I waited for him', and from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: The Arabs say: 'Indeed I look to God then to so-and-so', and this usage is also found in Persian, for they say about one who hopes for something he expects... [unclear] so it is established that this usage is commonly known in Arabic and Persian, even a blind person may use this expression and say: 'My eye is fixed on you and my gaze is to God then to you.' The twentieth proof: The poet's saying: 'Faces looking on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful waiting for deliverance.' He affirmed 'nazar' with the preposition 'ila' not meaning vision but meaning waiting. It cannot be said: Some narrators transmitted this verse in another way: 'Faces looking on the day of Bakr' and claimed that the poet meant the day of al-Yamamah, and it was called the day of Bakr because the fighting occurred between the army of Abu Bakr and Musaylimah the Liar, and the intended meaning of 'al-Rahman' in the verse is Musaylimah the Liar, for they used to call him 'Rahman of al-Yamamah' and they were looking at his face and hoping that he would save them from that calamity. We say: There is no contradiction between the two narrations, so we accept both. The twenty-first proof: The saying of al-Kumayt: 'And they heard looking at Bilal as the thirsty look at the clouds.' It is known that the thirsty wait for the life-giving rain of the clouds, so we know that the looking of the people at Bilal is in the sense of waiting, except that he likened their looking to the life-giving rain of the clouds.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a series of three linguistic proofs (19th, 20th, 21st) presented by the author to demonstrate that the term 'nazar' (نظر) when followed by the preposition 'ila' can mean waiting/expectation (انتظار) rather than physical vision (رؤية). The 19th proof cites the philologist al-Khalil and Ibn Abbas, noting that Arabs say 'I look to God then to you' meaning they expect from God and then from the person. This usage is also found in Persian. Even a blind person uses this phrase, confirming it does not require vision. The 20th proof is a verse from a poet describing faces looking on the day of Badr to the Most Merciful expecting deliverance; the preposition confirms expectation. An objection suggests an alternative reading changes the meaning to a different battle, but the author dismisses it by accepting both versions. The 21st proof from the poet al-Kumayt compares people looking at Bilal to the thirsty looking at clouds, clearly indicating expectation of rain. Thus these proofs build the case that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
إلى (ila)
انتظار (intizar)
الرحمن (al-Rahman)
بدر (Badr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Variant readings automatically invalidate a linguistic proof.
Correction: Razi shows that even if the variant reading is accepted, it does not contradict the original meaning of expectation.

Checkpoint
What does the word 'tantaẓiru' (تنتظر) in the verse indicate about the meaning of 'nāẓirāt' (ناظرات)?
A) They are physically looking.
B) They are waiting.
C) They are uncertain.
D) They are despairing.

Key Takeaway
The verse explicitly pairs 'nazar ila' with 'waiting', confirming it means expectation, and variant readings do not refute this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In times of crisis, do your eyes turn to Allah in true expectation, or are you looking to created beings (people, status, money) like the people of Yamama looked to Musaylimah? What specific hope do you need to purify today?
?
The variant reading shows that people misplaced their trust in a false 'rahman'. Are there any impostor 'saviors' in your life—things you rely on for deliverance that cannot truly save? How will you redirect your hope to Allah alone?
?
When you recite 'ila rabbiha nāẓirah' (to their Lord looking) in Surat al-Qiyamah, does your heart feel the weight of waiting for Allah, or have you reduced it to a theory? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely lived in expectation of meeting Him?

Proof 21: Al-Kumayt's Analogy of Thirsty to Clouds
Razi uses al-Kumayt's analogy to prove 'nazar ila' means expectation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Kumayt's poetic analogy reinforces that 'nazar ila' means expectation by comparing people looking at Bilal to the thirsty looking at clouds for rain.

Core Idea
Nazar ila means expectation: thirsty look at clouds awaiting rain, not seeing rain.

This final linguistic proof uses a verse from the poet al-Kumayt. He compares people looking at Bilal to the thirsty looking at clouds. The analogy is powerful because the thirsty do not see rain; they look at clouds with expectation of rain. Thus, the verb 'nazar' here is clearly about waiting.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and explanation
Read the Arabic for proof 21 and translation. Notice the comparison: people looking at Bilal like the thirsty looking at clouds.
The word 'ẓumā' (thirsty) and 'ghamām' (clouds).
2
Understand the analogy
Recognize that the thirsty do not see the rain itself; they look at the clouds hoping for rain. So 'nazar ila' here means expectation.
Razi explicitly says 'they wait for the life-giving rain of the clouds'.
3
Summarize the cumulative evidence
Consider how this proof, together with the previous two, establishes that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation in Arabic.
These linguistic proofs are crucial for the theological argument about seeing God.

The 22nd and 23rd Linguistic Proofs: 'Nazar ila' as Expectation
Two proofs showing 'nazar ila' as expectation using impossible-vision contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two classical Arabic verses demonstrate that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation when physical vision is impossible.

Core Idea
When vision is impossible, 'nazar ila' must mean expectation.

Imam Razi continues his linguistic arguments by citing two verses where the context makes physical sight impossible. In both cases, the poet uses 'nazar ila' to express anticipation or expectation, not visual perception. Let's examine these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs carefully
Read the Arabic passage for both proofs and their translations. Notice how each verse describes a situation where the looker cannot physically see the object.
The sea barrier in proof 22 and the great distance in proof 23 are key.
2
Identify the impossibility of vision
For each proof, identify the specific obstacle that prevents physical vision: the sea in proof 22, the distance in proof 23.
Razi explicitly states 'لا يحتمل الرؤية' (does not permit vision).
3
Understand the conclusion
Since vision is impossible, the only plausible meaning for 'nazar ila' is expectation (intizar). Confirm this from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية والعشرون : قال آخر : وإذا نظرت إليك من ملك والبحر دونك زدتني نـعـمـا وصف نفسه بكونه ناظرا إلى الملك حال ما كان البحر حائلا بينهما، والذي يحتمل الرؤية، فعلمنا أن المراد هو الانتظار. الحجة الثالثة والعشرون: قول البعيث : وجوه بهاليل الحجاز على النوى إلى ملك زان المغارب ناظره المسألة التاسعة عشر أثبت أن " بهاليل " الحجاز ينظرون إلى ملك المغرب وذلك لا يحتمل الرؤية، بل يحتمل الانتظار الحجة الرابعة والعشرون : قال بعضهم : ويوم بذي قار رأيت وجوههم إلى الموت من وقع السيوف نواظرا والمراد الانتظار لأن الموت لا خلاف في أنه لا يرى. لا يقال : المراد من الموت الرجل القتال كما في قوله : أنـ الـ لأنا نقول : لا شك أن تسمية الرجل القاتل بالموت مجاز، فلا يصار إليه إلا عند المضرورة. هي الحجة الخامسة والعشرون : قول (الأبيوردي ) في صفة عين ممدوحه : التي لا تزال الدهر ناظرة إلى العلى، ولزوار، وفي كتب فقوله : ناظرة إلى العلى معناه كونها طالبة للعلى ومتوقعة له . فجملة هذه الوجوه الأول دالة على أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى، ليس للرؤية، وهذه الوجوه الأخيرة دالة على أنه للانتظار.
The twenty-second proof: Another said: 'And when I looked at you from a king and the sea was between us, you increased me in blessings.' He described himself as looking at the king while the sea was a barrier between them, which does not permit vision, so we know that the intended meaning is expectation. The twenty-third proof: The saying of al-Bu'ayth: 'Faces of the nobles of al-Hijaz in their longing look to a king who adorned the Maghrib.' The nineteenth issue: He confirmed that the nobles of al-Hijaz look to the king of the Maghrib, and that does not permit vision, but permits expectation. The twenty-fourth proof: Some said: 'And on the day of Dhi Qar I saw their faces looking at death from the falling of swords, gazers.' The intended meaning is expectation because death is unquestionably not seen. It cannot be said: what is meant by death is the fighting man, as in the saying: ... [unclear] We say: There is no doubt that calling the killer 'death' is a metaphor, so recourse is not made to it except under necessity. The twenty-fifth proof: The saying of (al-Abywardi) in describing the eye of his praised one: 'Which never ceases to look to high positions and visitors, and in books...' So his saying: 'looking to high positions' means it is seeking high positions and expecting them. So the sum of these first proofs indicates that 'nazar' coupled with 'ila' is not for vision, and these latter proofs indicate that it is for expectation.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage continues the series of linguistic proofs (22nd to 25th) that 'nazar ila' means expectation, not vision. The 22nd proof uses a verse where a poet says he looked at a king despite a sea barrier, making vision impossible, so it must mean expectation. The 23rd proof cites al-Bu'ayth describing the nobles of Hijaz looking to a distant king, again impossible for vision, so expectation. The 24th proof uses a verse about looking at death (the falling of swords); since death is not seen, it means expecting death. An objection that 'death' might be a metaphor for a man is rejected because metaphors require necessity. The 25th proof from al-Abywardi describes an eye that 'looks to high positions', meaning it expects them. The summary states that all these proofs confirm that 'nazar ila' can mean expectation.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
إلى (ila)
انتظار (intizar)
رؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'nazar ila' exclusively as physical vision.
Correction: These proofs show that when vision is impossible, the phrase must be understood as expectation.

Checkpoint
In the 22nd proof, what prevents the poet from seeing the king?
A) Night time
B) The sea
C) The king's anger
D) A wall

Key Takeaway
When 'nazar ila' is used in a context that physically prevents sight, it must be interpreted as expectation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a barrier in your life—like distance, time, or hardship—do you 'look' to Allah with expectation of His help, or do you give up hope? What would change if you truly expected His aid as the poets expected the king?
?
The poets used 'nazar ila' to express longing for something physically out of reach. Do you long for Allah's mercy and Paradise with the same intensity, even though you cannot see them? How can you cultivate that expectation today?
?
This lesson shows that words can shift meaning based on context. Are there areas of your faith where you take words too literally and miss the deeper expectation Allah wants from you? What step can you take to realign your understanding?

The 24th and 25th Linguistic Proofs: Expectation of Death and Abstract Goals
Two proofs using death and abstract goals to show 'nazar ila' means expectation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how 'nazar ila' is used for expecting death and seeking abstract goals, further supporting its meaning of expectation.

Core Idea
Invisible objects like death and abstract concepts confirm 'nazar ila' means expectation.

Razi adds two more proofs: one uses 'nazar ila' toward death, which is invisible, and another uses it toward abstract goals. Both reinforce that 'nazar ila' cannot mean literal sight but expresses anticipation or expectation.

Guided Path
1
Read both proofs and their translations
Carefully read the Arabic for proofs 24 and 25 along with the translation. Note what is being looked at: death in proof 24, and 'high positions' (abstract) in proof 25.
Proof 24 includes an objection and response.
2
Analyze the 24th proof: death
In proof 24, the faces are described as looking at death. Since death is not a physical object that can be seen, 'nazar ila' must mean expectation. Note the objection that 'death' might be a metaphor for a man, and Razi's refutation.
Razi says calling a man 'death' is a metaphor, not to be used without necessity.
3
Analyze the 25th proof: abstract goal
In proof 25, an eye is described as looking to high positions and visitors. Since these are abstract or intangible, 'nazar ila' cannot be vision; it means seeking and expecting.
4
Connect to the overall argument
These proofs, along with the previous ones, establish that 'nazar ila' often means expectation, not vision. Reflect on how this supports Razi's broader theological point.

According to the 24th proof, why can 'nazar ila' to death not mean physical vision?
A) Because death is a man
B) Because death is unseen
C) Because death is a metaphor
D) Because the poet said so

Key Takeaway
When 'nazar ila' is directed toward an invisible entity like death or an abstract goal, it must be interpreted as expectation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'anic phrase 'nazar ila rabbihim' (looking at their Lord), have you always thought of it as physical sight? This lesson argues that if the object is invisible or abstract, 'nazar ila' means expectation. What would it mean for your daily relationship with Allah if you truly expected to see Him, not just as a distant vision but as a real anticipation that shapes your choices? Are you living in that expectation, or is it just a belief you hold without effect?
?
The poet describes faces 'looking at death' from the falling swords—the death is certain and imminent. How certain are you of your own death? Does your 'looking at death' translate into eager expectation that changes how you spend each hour? Or do you avoid the thought entirely, living as if you will never die? This lesson forces you to answer: Is your death just an abstract concept, or do you truly expect it?
?
You claim to 'look to high positions' like good deeds and Paradise, but if 'nazar ila' requires expectation, are you actively seeking and expecting those goals, or are you merely gazing at them from a distance without commitment? What concrete action will you take today to transform your 'looking' from passive vision into active expectation?

Razi's Final Analysis of 'Nazar'
Razi responds to the objection and gives his final view on 'nazar'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi reconciles conflicting linguistic evidence concerning the word 'nazar' and explains its core meaning of turning the pupil.

Core Idea
The physical act of turning the pupil is the root meaning of 'nazar'.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi concludes his verbal analysis by addressing a potential objection from linguists who claim that 'nazar ila' never means expectation. He offers a reconciliation that preserves the strength of his earlier proofs, and then presents his own preferred analysis of the word's origin. This lesson examines the objection, the response, and the final synthesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection: linguists claim 'nazar ila' never means expectation.
The objection is introduced with 'لا يقال'.
2
Understand the reconciliation
Razi reconciles by distinguishing between waiting for a person (without ila) and waiting for aid (with ila). Reflect on the example 'نظري إلى الله ثم إليك'.
This preserves the validity of both sides.
3
Grasp the author's final analysis
Razi states the original meaning is turning the pupil (تقليب الحدقة). From this physical act, both vision and expectation derive metaphorically.
This unifies the seemingly disparate meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : قد اشتهر من علماء اللغة أنهم قالوا : النظر إذا لم يكن مقرونا بحرف إلى أفاد الانتظار، يقال نظرته أي انتظرته. أما إذا كان مقرونا بحرف إلى، فإنه ليس للانتظار. لأنا نقول : لما حصل التعارض بين دلائلنا ودلائلكم، فلا بد من التوفيق. وطريقه أن قولهم: نظرته بمعنى انتظرته إنما يقال في انتظار مجيء الإنسان بنفسه، أما إذا كان منتظر الرفد أو المعونة. فقد يقال فيه : نظرت إليه . ومنه قولهم : إنما نظري إلى الله، ثم إليك. فهذا مجموع البحث في هذه المسألة اللفظية. واعلم أن الأقرب أن يقال: الأصل في قول القائل: نظرت إليك، تقليب الحدقة نحوه . ثم قد يستعمل في الرؤية، من حيث إن تقليب الحدقة سبب للرؤية، ويستعمل أيضا في الانتظار، من حيث إن تقليب الحدقة سبب للانتظار، فإن من انتظر شيئا فإنه يقلب الحدقة نحو الجهة التي ينتظر المقصود منها.
It cannot be said: It is well-known from linguists that they said: 'Nazar' when not accompanied by the preposition 'ila' indicates expectation, e.g., 'nazartuhu' meaning 'I waited for him'. But when accompanied by 'ila', it is not for expectation. We say: Since there is a conflict between our proofs and your proofs, reconciliation is necessary. The way of reconciliation is that: When they said 'nazartuhu' meaning 'I waited for him', that is only said when waiting for the person himself to come. But if one is waiting for aid or assistance, one may say 'nazartu ilayhi'. And from this is their saying: 'My looking is to God, then to you.' So this is the sum of the discussion on this verbal issue. Know that the closest [to truth] is to say: The original meaning of someone saying 'nazartu ilayka' is the turning of the pupil toward him. Then it may be used for vision, because turning the pupil is a cause of vision, and it is also used for expectation, because turning the pupil is a cause of expectation, for one who expects something turns his pupil toward the direction from which he expects the object.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the discussion on the word 'nazar' (نظر). The author first presents an objection from linguists who claim that 'nazar ila' never means expectation; only 'nazar' without 'ila' does. The author responds by proposing a reconciliation: when 'nazartuhu' is used, it means waiting for the person to come in person; but when waiting for aid, the idiom 'nazartu ilayhi' is used, as in 'my expectation is to God then to you'. After summarizing the discussion, the author gives his own preferred analysis: the literal origin of 'nazartu ilayka' is the act of turning the pupil toward something. This physical action is a cause for both vision (if the object is visible) and expectation (if one is anticipating something). Thus, the word can be used metaphorically for both meanings.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
إلى (ila)
انتظار (intizar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlib al-hadaqa)
توفيق (tawfiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nazar ila' only means 'look' and 'nazar' (without ila) only means 'wait', and these are unrelated.
Correction: Razi shows both meanings derive from the same physical act of turning the pupil, so they are connected.

Checkpoint
According to the author's final analysis, what is the original meaning of 'nazartu ilayka'?
A) I saw you
B) I waited for you
C) I turned my pupil toward you
D) I expected your aid

Key Takeaway
The root meaning of 'nazar' is the physical act of turning the pupil, from which both vision and expectation derive metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart is constantly 'turning' toward something—what is it truly focused on? Are you looking toward God for aid, or are you waiting for worldly things? Commit today to turning your inner gaze toward Him in every expectation.
?
The act of turning the pupil is both a cause for seeing and for waiting. Reflect: What are you physically and mentally directing your attention to? Does your daily routine reflect that you are expecting from God or from others? What change must you make?
?
Razi shows that a simple physical act can have deep spiritual meanings. Consider: When you say 'إليك نظرتي' (my gaze is to You), is it a genuine turning of your entire being toward God? What would it take for your 'nazar' to be truly sincere?

Razi's Linguistic Analysis of 'Nazar'
Razi reviews arguments and gives his own linguistic analysis of 'nazar'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic analysis of 'nazar' as presented by Imam Razi, including its root meaning and usage with and without the preposition 'ila'.

Core Idea
The word 'nazar' literally means turning the pupil, not seeing or waiting.

This lesson examines Imam Razi's detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'nazar' (looking). Razi responds to arguments that 'nazar ila' must mean waiting rather than seeing, and he ultimately explains the root meaning. Understanding this is essential for the theological debate on seeing God in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: Razi first presents opposing proofs, then a counter-argument, and finally his own conclusion.
The passage uses poetic examples.
2
Identify the two main proofs
Find the twenty-fourth and twenty-fifth proofs. What do they argue about 'nazar'? How do they support the claim that 'nazar ila' means waiting?
These are crucial for the debate.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Razi addresses a counter-argument from linguists. He explains the difference between 'nazartuhu' and 'nazartu ilayhi'. Grasp his distinction.
This shows his linguistic precision.
4
Reflect on Razi's conclusion
Razi states his own view: the root meaning is 'turning the pupil'. Consider how this resolves the dispute and why it matters for theology.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة عشر أثبت أن " بهاليل " الحجاز ينظرون إلى ملك المغرب وذلك لا يحتمل الرؤية، بل يحتمل الانتظار الحجة الرابعة والعشرون : قال بعضهم : ويوم بذي قار رأيت وجوههم إلى الموت من وقع السيوف نواظرا والمراد الانتظار لأن الموت لا خلاف في أنه لا يرى. لا يقال : المراد من الموت الرجل القتال كما في قوله : أنـ الـ لأنا نقول : لا شك أن تسمية الرجل القاتل بالموت مجاز، فلا يصار إليه إلا عند المضرورة. هي الحجة الخامسة والعشرون : قول (الأبيوردي ) في صفة عين ممدوحه : التي لا تزال الدهر ناظرة إلى العلى، ولزوار، وفي كتب فقوله : ناظرة إلى العلى معناه كونها طالبة للعلى ومتوقعة له . فجملة هذه الوجوه الأول دالة على أن النظر المقرون بحرف إلى، ليس للرؤية، وهذه الوجوه الأخيرة دالة على أنه للانتظار. · لا يقال : قد اشتهر من علماء اللغة أنهم قالوا : النظر إذا لم يكن مقرونا بحرف إلى أفاد الانتظار، يقال نظرته أي انتظرته. أما إذا كان مقرونا بحرف إلى، فإنه ليس للانتظار. لأنا نقول : لما حصل التعارض بين دلائلنا ودلائلكم، فلا بد من التوفيق. وطريقه أن قولهم: نظرته بمعنى انتظرته إنما يقال في انتظار مجيء الإنسان بنفسه، أما إذا كان منتظر الرفد أو المعونة. فقد يقال فيه : نظرت إليه . ومنه قولهم : إنما نظري إلى الله، ثم إليك. فهذا مجموع البحث في هذه المسألة اللفظية. واعلم أن الأقرب أن يقال: الأصل في قول القائل: نظرت إليك، تقليب الحدقة نحوه . ثم قد يستعمل في الرؤية، من حيث إن تقليب الحدقة سبب للرؤية، ويستعمل أيضا في الانتظار، من حيث إن تقليب الحدقة سبب للانتظار، فإن من انتظر شيئا فإنه يقلب الحدقة نحو الجهة التي ينتظر المقصود منها. فهذا ما عندي في هذا البحث اللفظي.
The nineteenth issue: He established that the 'Bahālīl' of the Hijaz look at the king of Morocco, and that does not imply seeing, but rather implies waiting. The twenty-fourth proof: Some said: 'And on the day of Dhi Qār, I saw their faces, toward death from the fall of swords, looking (nawāẓirā),' and the intended meaning is waiting, because death is undisputedly not seen. It cannot be said: The intended meaning of death is the fighting man, as in his saying: 'An... al...' For we say: There is no doubt that naming the killer 'death' is a metaphor, so it is resorted to only in necessity. The twenty-fifth proof: The saying of (al-Abyurdī) in describing the eye of his praised one: 'Which does not cease, forever, looking (nāẓira) toward lofty things and visitors.' And in books, his saying: 'Looking (nāẓira) toward lofty things' means it is seeking lofty things and expecting them. So the totality of these first proofs indicate that 'nazar' connected with the preposition 'ilā' is not for seeing, and these latter proofs indicate that it is for waiting. It cannot be said: It is well-known from linguists that they said: 'Nazar' when not connected with the preposition 'ilā' indicates waiting; one says 'nazartuhu' meaning 'I waited for him.' But when it is connected with the preposition 'ilā', then it is not for waiting. For we say: When contradiction occurred between our proofs and your proofs, reconciliation is necessary. Its method is that their saying 'nazartuhu' in the meaning of 'I waited for him' is only said regarding waiting for the coming of the person himself. But if one is waiting for aid or assistance, it may be said therein: 'nazartu ilayhi.' And from this is their saying: 'My looking (naẓarī) is only to God, then to you.' So this is the sum of the discussion on this verbal issue. Know that the closest view is to say: The root in the saying of the one who says 'nazartu ilayka' is turning the pupil toward him. Then it may be used for seeing, because turning the pupil is a cause for seeing, and it may also be used for waiting, because turning the pupil is a cause for waiting, for whoever waits for something turns his pupil toward the direction from which he expects the object. So this is what I have regarding this verbal discussion.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis by Razi of the word 'nazar' (looking) and its usage in Arabic, particularly when connected with the preposition 'ilā'. Razi first presents several proofs from others who argue that 'nazar ilā' means 'waiting' rather than 'seeing'. He then gives his own conclusion. The passage begins with a reference to earlier arguments about Hijazi people looking at the king of Morocco, which is interpreted as waiting. Then he cites the twenty-fourth proof: a line of poetry describing faces looking toward death, which must mean waiting since death is invisible. The objection that 'death' might mean 'fighting man' is dismissed as metaphorical and not default. The twenty-fifth proof quotes al-Abyurdī describing an eye as 'looking toward lofty things,' which is explained as seeking and expecting. Razi then notes that the sum of these proofs indicates that 'nazar' with 'ilā' does not mean seeing but waiting. He then addresses a counter-argument from linguists that 'nazar' without 'ilā' means waiting, but with 'ilā' it does not. Razi reconciles by specifying that 'nazartuhu' (without ilā) is used for waiting for a person's arrival, while 'nazartu ilayhi' (with ilā) is used for waiting for aid or benefit. He gives the example 'my looking is only to God, then to you.' Finally, Razi gives his own view: the root meaning is physically turning the pupil toward something. This then can be used metaphorically for seeing (since turning the pupil causes seeing) or for waiting (since turning the pupil is involved in expecting something). Thus he concludes that the word is flexible and context-dependent.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
انتظار (intizār)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlīb al-ḥadaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'nazar ila' always means physical sight.
Correction: Razi shows that it can also mean waiting, as in expecting aid, and the root is turning the pupil.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's conclusion, what is the original meaning of 'nazar' in phrases like 'nazartu ilayka'?
A) Seeing with the eye
B) Turning the pupil toward something
C) Waiting for someone's arrival
D) Hoping for a benefit

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that the literal meaning of 'nazar' is turning the pupil, which can metaphorically indicate seeing or waiting depending on context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or Hadith, do you assume you know the meaning of key words like 'nazar' without considering their root and context? How might this lead you to misunderstand Allah's attributes, especially regarding the vision of Him on the Day of Judgment? Will you commit to studying words more carefully?
?
Razi's analysis shows that even a common word like 'nazar' can have multiple layers of meaning. In your own understanding of Islamic theology, are you quick to pick one meaning and dismiss others? How can you adopt Razi's method of seeking reconciliation and deeper understanding, even when proofs seem contradictory?
?
This lesson revolves around a verbal dispute. But think about your own speech: do you use words carelessly, or do you reflect on their precise connotations? How does the precision of language in kalam challenge you to be more deliberate with your words, especially when discussing matters of faith?
